Reprinted with permissions from ref. 210 (a) and ref. 221 (b and c). Copyright (2016) The Royal Society of Chemistry (a); (2017) American Chemical Society (b and c). 
The phase of CTS NCs can also be controlled by the nature of sulfur precursor. The heating-up synthesis with S/OLA and 1-DDT was reported to lead selectively to the zinc blende and wurtzite modifications of Cu2SnS3 NCs, respectively.223 The CTS NCs was then converted into CZTS by the substitution of Cu with Zn/Sn with the preservation of the phase composition of the original CTS template.223


2.2 Hot-injection synthesis. 
In the hot injection synthesis the metal precursors are typically dissolved in OLA, oleic acid and other media at around 100 °C, then the temperature is rapidly increased to 180–200 °C and a chalcogen source (for example sulfur solution in OLA – S/OLA or 1-DDT) is injected to trigger the nucleation of MC NCs. The solution is kept at this temperature for a certain period of time to allow the primary nuclei to grow to a desired size and then the temperature is sharply reduced thus arresting the further NCs growth and ”freezing” the existing size distribution. Therefore, in the hot-injection method, the variation of the heating duration after the injection event is most frequently used to tailor the average size of the resulting NCs.83,126,224 For example, the average size of AIS NCs growing in 1-DDT increases from 1.9 nm just after the S/OLA injection to 2.3 nm after a 30 s treatment and to 3.1 nm – after the heating for 20 min.224
CIS NCs are typically produced by the hot-injection technique from Cu+ and In3+ chlorides or acetates dissolved at 80–90 °C in Ar- or N2-saturated OLA or other coordinating solvent to yield amine complexes. The sulfide source is then rapidly injected at 240–280 °C and the mixture is kept at this temperature for a given time to increase the NC size.133,225–227 After the CIS NC formation a protective ZnS shell is often deposited by the secondary hot-injection of zinc(II) acetate and an additional heating cycle.226
AIS NCs were synthesized by the hot-injection procedure with 1-DDT as a capping ligand.81,126,228,229 The NCs formation was induced by the S/OLA injection at 110 °C. When the Ag-to-In ratio is reduced the synthesis yields AgIn5S8 NCs. An increase in the In:Ag ratio from 1:1 to 5:1 was reported to forward the hot-injection synthesis towards the preferential formation of cubic AgIn5S8 NCs with a well-defined stoichiometry.230 Similar methods were applied to synthesize copper-diffused AIS NCs and CIS NCs. The 1-DDT ligands reduce in situ Cu(II) to Cu(I) which diffuses into the AIS lattice during the incubation of the reaction mixture at 180 °C.231
The shape of CIS NCs formed by the hot-injection can be changed from polygonal to elongated bullet-like one by adding OLA to the TOPO solvent (Fig. 16a and b). Oleylamine decreases the concentration of monomeric CIS available for the NC growth favoring to the elongated NC shape.232 Additionally, phase-selected wurtzite and zinc blende CIS NCs can be synthesized with different sulfur sources – 1-DDT and S/OLA, respectively. By adjusting the composition of the dispersive media, the phase-selected tetragonal and orthorhombic 7–25 nm AISe NCs were synthesized.233 The type of crystalline lattice of AIS NCs grown by the hot-injection can be easily changed from orthorhombic to tetragonal by elevating the after-injection temperature from 120 to 180 °C.230 In the intermediate temperature range, both phases co-exist.83
 Fig. 16  (a and b) TEM of CIS NCs produced by the hot-injection in different dispersive media. (c) Elemental maps of Cu and In for single CIS NCs (scale bar 2 nm). Reprinted with permissions from ref. 223 (a and b) and ref. 51 (c). Copyright (2011) Springer (a and b). 
Recent findings made by the high-resolution STEM coupled to the electron energy loss spectroscopy51 showed that the highly crystalline and stoichiometric CIS NCs produced by the classical heating-up/hot-injection methods comprise inhomogeneities of copper and indium distribution within each single NCs. The CIS NCs showed the presence of Cu-rich and In-rich areas (Fig. 16c) without any apparent discontinuity of the chalcopyrite lattice structure.51 These findings indicate that the unambiguous assignment of the CIS/AIS NC structure to some certain lattice type is tricky and obviously does not reflect the real picture on the atomic scale.
The thiol ligands bind strongly to the MMC NC surface presenting obvious difficulties for the post-synthesis ligand exchange and introduction of the NCs into the light-harvesting systems. In this view, various thiol-free hot-injection approaches are probed. In particular, the thiol-free CIS NCs were successfully prepared by hot-injecting bis(trimethylsilyl) sulfide into the solution of copper and indium halides in OLA/TOP/ODE.95,234
CuInSe2 (CISe) NCs form in a 1-DDT/ODE mixture from metal oleates after the injection of tributylphosphine selenium at 200 °C.71,235 The average size of CISe NCs can be varied by the post-injection heating duration in the range of 2.5–3.5 nm.235 Similar syntheses of CISe NCs were realized with selenourea.236 A more reactive selenium precursor – diphosphine selenide allowed to tune the CISe NC size from less than 2 to 7 nm.237 Mixed CISSe NCs with a varied composition were formed by combining 1-DDT and TOP-Se as sulfide and selenide source, respectively.238
Alloyed CuInxGa1−xSe2 NCs can be produced in the entire x range from 0 to 1 by the hot-injection in hexadecylamine-based mixtures by injecting Se/hexadecylamide precursor with metal–amine complexes at 240 °C.239
Alternatively, CIGS NCs can be formed by introducing Ga(acac)3 and Se/OLA240–242 or diphenylphosphine selenium.98 Alloyed Cu2Ge(S3−xSex) NCs were synthesized by the injection of GeCl4/OLA into the OLA precursor of copper followed by the second injection of S/Se dissolved in OLA.243 The average size of NCs increased from 11 nm to 19 nm as S was completely substituted with Se. Moreover, the bandgap was found to depend linearly on the S:Se ratio.243
A general approach to the synthesis of CITe and AITe NCs was developed244 utilizing TOP-Te complex as a telluride ion source. The 10 nm CdIn2S4 NCs were prepared by the hot-injection of S/OLA into the mixture of Cd(II) and In(III) OLA complexes at 180 °C.245 The antimony-based Cu12Sb4S13 and Cu3SbS4 NCs were produced by injecting bis(trimethylsilyl)sulfide into the hot solution of copper(I) and Sb(III) chlorides in ODE/oleic acid at 190 °C.246
By injecting S/OLA into a mixture of Cu2+ and Sn2+ OLA complexes at 240 °C, CTS NCs were synthesized with an average size of 10–11 nm.247 Similarly, CTS NCs were produced by the hot-injection in TOPO/OLA mixtures with the elemental sulfur dissolved in OLA or ODE as a sulfide ion source.248 The hot-injection synthesis in ethylene glycol at 190 °C yields CTS nanoflakes with a lateral size of around 100 nm.249 Hexadecylamine was used as a universal reaction medium for the formation of Cu2+ and Sn2+ precursor complexes, dissolution of selenium and stabilization of ∼20 nm Cu2SnSe3 NCs.114
A selection of the solvent composition and selenium precursor can forward the hot-injection synthesis of Cu2SnSe3 either to hexagonal or to cubic CTSe NCs.117,250 In particular, the hot-injection of a Sn–OLA complex into a mixture of diphenylselenide and Cu–OLA at 240 °C yields predominantly wurtzite CTSe NCs, while the injection of separately prepared Cu– and Sn–OLA complexes into the hot Se/OLA solution produces cubic CTSe NCs. The time profiles of the NC evolution showed that in each case the ternary NCs form as a result of the Sn ions insertion into the primary copper selenide NCs.250
The phase-selective hot-injection syntheses of kesterite, wurtzite and mixed CZTS NCs were realized by using elemental sulfur, 1-DDT, and thioacetamide as sulfur sources, respectively.115 The key stage of the kesterite phase formation is the H2S release in a reaction between elemental sulfur and OLA.115
By adjusting the ratio of 1-DDT and sulfur the phase composition and purity of CZTS NCs can be controlled.251 As the 1-DDT-to-S ratio increases, the CZTS NCs gradually evolve from kesterite nanoplates to wurtzite nanorods, the NCs becoming smaller and more uniform. A similar effect of the 1-DDT/S ratio on the NC phase and morphology was observed in the hot-injection synthesis of CZTS NCs from Sn(II) ethyl hexanoate.252 This precursor is stable in air and can be easily mixed with the solvents commonly used in the hot-injection procedure.
The hot-injection approach is universal and can be extended to complex MMC compositions. In particular, the CZTS NCs with a varied stoichiometry and a size of 4–5 nm were produced by the hot-injection using dioctylamine complexes of metals and 1-DDT injected at 230 °C.253 Similar syntheses of CZTS NCs were realized in OLA with S/OLA92,211,254–265 as well as in ethylene glycol266 and triethylene glycol.267 The method was generalized to synthesize CIS267 and Cu2−xAgxZnSnS4 NCs.267,268 A mixture of Cu, Zn, and Sn diethyldithiocarbamates injected into the hot OLA at 290–320 °C serves as a universal source of both metal and sulfide ions for the CZTS NC growth.269
Similar to the heating-up approach, the injection results in the formation of primary CuxS NCs followed by the incorporation of Zn2+ and Sn4+ ions regardless of the injection temperature.211 However, an increase in the heating temperature from 200 °C to 300 °C allows to tune the average NC size from ∼5 nm to 8 nm.211 At that, the variation of the bandgap of the resulting CZTS NCs with the injection/heating temperature does not necessarily reflect only the size variation of the NCs but can also originate from variations of the phase composition and stoichiometry of CZTS NCs.254
Alloyed CZTSSe NCs can be prepared by the hot-injection by using mixtures of elemental S and Se dissolved/suspended in OLA.270–274 Using solely Se/OLA results in quaternary CZTSe NCs.275 NaBH4 is sometimes added as a reducing agent to enhance the solubility of selenium in hot OLA and to ensure the homogeneity of the mixed S/Se precursor.116,276,277 Alternatively, selenium can be reduced by 1-DDT to the OLA-soluble oleylammonium selenide that was used for the hot-injection synthesis of CISe and CZTSe NCs.278 Apparently, the NCs may contain some sulfur admixtures from the 1-DDT decomposition.
The metastable wurtzite CZTS NCs were produced by the hot-injection of a 1-DDT/t-DDT precursor into a mixture of OLA complexes of Cu2+, Zn2+, and Sn2+.279 A similar approach was applied to form wurtzite Cu2ZnGeS4 NCs.280 The post-synthesis heating of wurtzite CZTS NCs transforms them into very stable cubic zinc blende modification.117
OLA was found to decrease the temperature of the thermal decomposition of metal dedtc complexes as a result of the coordination to thiocarbonyl carbon atoms of dedtc. This effect was exploited64 to produce small (2–7 nm) CZTS NCs from the mixtures of dedtc complexes of Cu, Zn, and Sn, that have different decomposition temperatures. The hot injection of OLA at a temperature lower than the decomposition temperature of any of the individual three complexes but higher than the decomposition temperature of such complexes dissolved in OLA triggers simultaneous decomposition of all three complexes and the formation of stoichiometric CZTS NCs. It was found that the concentration of primary nuclei and, therefore, the size of final NCs depends on the OLA concentration, varying from 2 to 7 nm as the OLA content is increased.64
The injection of copper, tin, and zinc diethyldithiocarbamates into hot OLA results in rapid nucleation and growth of CZTS NCs finishing within 10 min.281 By elevating the OLA temperature 150 to 340 °C the average size of kesterite NCs can be gradually increased from around 2 to ∼40 nm.
Kesterite Cu2FeSnS4 NCs with an average size of 14 nm were prepared by the hot-injection in OLA using acac complexes of metals and S/OLA (Fig. 17a).282,283 The product contained admixtures of FeS and Cu2SnS3 phases and thus the synthesis conditions need to be further optimized. Similar hot-injection approaches were used to synthesize Cu2(FexZn1−x)SnS4,284 Cu2NiSnS4,285 Cu2CdSnS4 and Cu2MnSnS4,259 as well as Cu2CoSn(S,Se)4 NCs.274
 Fig. 17  (a) TEM of Cu2FeSnS4 NCs (inset: the NC size distribution). (b) TEM of CZTS/Au–Ag nanoheterostructures produced with a different duration of the metal deposition (indicated on the figure). Reprinted with permissions from ref. 282 (a) and ref. 286 (b). Copyright (2012) The Royal Society of Chemistry (a); (2017) American Chemical Society (b). 
Alloyed Cu2Zn(Ge,Sn)S4 (CZTGS) NCs revealed a dependence of the average NC size on the germanium precursor used in the hot-injection procedure.287 While the CZTGS NCs synthesized using Ge–citrate complex have an unimodal size distribution centered at 15 nm, the NCs produced from GeCl4 or complexes of germanium(IV) with glycine and tartrate ions have a bimodal size distribution with maxima at ∼5 nm and ∼15 nm. The citrate-mediated synthesis, which is a preferable one due to a better control of the NC morphology, allows to tune a Sn:Ge ratio as well as the optical band of the produced NCs from 1.6 eV for pure CZTS to 2.7 eV for CZGS.287 The mixed tin–germanium CZTGS NCs were formed using two-step sequential Ge–OLA and S–OLA injections at 130 °C and 280 °C, respectively.288 The hot injection of a mixture of 1-DDT and t-DDT into TOPO/OLA metal precursor yields wurtzite/stannite 18 nm Cu2CdGeS4 NCs.289
A general approach to a series of Cu2ZnAS4−x and CuZn2AS4 (A = Al, Ga, In) NCs was developed73 based on the hot-injection of a mixture of 1-DDT/t-DDT into OLA complexes of corresponding metals. Similar to the ternary MC NCs, this combination of sulfur precursors, acting simultaneously as capping agents and sulfur sources leads to the 50–60 nm long and 10 nm thick CZTS NRs.
By two consecutive hot-injection procedures, first CZTS NCs and then binary CZTS/Au–Ag heterostructures can be produced.286 At that, by varying the duration of the second hot-injection stage of metal deposition the average size of the silver–gold alloyed NCs was smoothly increased from around 2 nm for 5 s heating exposure after the precursor injection to about 5 nm – for the 1 h thermal treatment (Fig. 17b). In a similar way, PtCo–Cu2ZnGeS4 nanocomposites were synthesized.280
The ternary CuSbS2 NCs were formed by hot-injecting S/OLA into Cu(acac)2 + SbCl3 solution in OLA at 230 °C.290 The synthesis yields nanoplates with the length/width/thickness of 50–120 nm/20–40 nm/6–9 nm, respectively. In this method, various CAS phases can be formed by varying the copper-to-antimony ratio, including CuSbS2, Cu3SbS3, Cu3SbS4, and Cu12Sb4S13.291 The average size of Cu12Sb4S13 NCs was reported to grow from 4 to 16 nm as the synthesis temperature was elevated from 150 to 230 °C.112
Relatively low-temperature hot-injection syntheses of AgBiS2 NCs were performed at 100 °C from metal oleates and hexamethyldisilathiane292 and trimethylsilylsufide293 as sulfur sources.


2.3 Ligand exchange. 
The heating-up/hot-injection procedures are typically followed with ligand exchange aimed at replacing bulky organic capping agents with much smaller bifunctional species containing simultaneously carboxylic and thiol group, such as TGA or MPA, or a combination of thiol, COOH and amino groups as in the case of cysteine or glutathione (GSH). The new ligand binds strongly on the surface of MC NCs by the thiol group while the carboxylic group becomes deprotonized rendering NC soluble in the polar solvents, specifically, in water and providing an electrostatic barrier for the NC agglomeration. Also, the carboxylic group serves as a “bridge” for the attachment of MC NCs to the surface of titania or zinc oxide.
The substitution of OLA/1-DDT ligands with GSH or MPA in N,N-dimethylformamide (DMF) yields water-dispersible CIZS NCs stable for months and showing a hydrodynamic size of less than 10 nm.294 The process is scalable and can be upgraded to the gram-scale synthesis. The exchange of bulky OLA ligands with 1,2-ethanediol binding together the adjacent NCs allows to form very compact films of ∼7 nm CISe NCs236 and therefore such approach should have good perspectives for the preparation of NC inks for the jet-printed photovoltaics. The ligand exchange with MPA followed by the anchoring of MC NCs to the surface of titania was applied to produce the titania heterostructures with CIS NCs,295 CIZS NCs,133 CISe NCs,168 and CIZSe NCs.96,296 Similar ligand exchange procedures were performed with less stable to oxidation but smaller TGA molecules yielding photoactive TiO2/CIZS nanocomposites.148 The above-discussed general ligand exchange methodology was applied to produce water-soluble CIS,142,297–299 AIS,72 CIGS,242,300 CIGSe,98 CTSe,174 CZTS,213,258,301–305 and CZTSSe NCs.271
The ligand exchange using 5-amino-1-pentanol yielded the concentrated and stable CZTS inks in ethanol or n-propanol with the NC content of up to 200 mg mL−1.306 OLA ligands on the CZTS NC surface were also substituted with smaller tert-butylpyridine by the solvothermal treatment at 90 °C.91
Copper antimony sulfide NCs produced by the heating-up approach and deposited as a film on the ITO (indium-tin oxide) surface can be stripped from the surface OLA ligand by contacting the film with the Meerwein's agent (C2H5)3OBF4,307 however, no details on the chemistry of this process were reported. As discussed in ref. 308, the contact of OLA-capped CZTS NCs with (C2H5)3OBF4 or (CH3)3OBF4 results in the amine elimination and the modification of the NC surface with BF4− anions.
Recently a very promising synthesis strategy was developed by Talapin et al.309 profiting from the ligand exchange of bulky organic moieties like OLA with charged metal chalcogenide complexes (MCC), like In2Se42−. In this approach, the OLA-capped Cu2−xSe (x = 0–0.2) NCs were transferred from hexane to dimethylsulfoxide (DMSO)/ethanolamine solvent by the ligand exchange with In2Se42− and a charge-compensating N2H5+ cation. Such MCC-capped NCs are soluble in a variety of solvents including formamide, DMF, DMSO, ethylacetate, etc. The annealing at 500 °C transforms the MCC-capped NC films into uniform CISe layers with only 2–3% of mass loss309 making such colloidal NCs an ideal ink for the jet-printing of thin-film solar cells. The approach is very versatile and can be universally used for the preparation of a variety of MMC nanomaterials. In particular, by replacing In2Se42− with an equivalent amount of gallium selenide MCC, CIGS layers were produced in a similar way.292 Only 0.1 mol equivalent of In2Se42− or a more complex [In2Cu2Se4S3]3− MCC was reported to be sufficient for the complete substitution of OLA ligands on the surface of 16 nm CISe NCs producing concentrated NC inks. Moreover, by combining Cu2−xSe and ZnS NCs both stabilized by a Sn2S64− MCC, the high-quality CZTS films can be fabricated by this approach. The composition of the CZTS layer can be easily adjusted by varying the molar ratio of MCC-capped NCs. The same procedure can be reproduced for ZnSe and Cu2−xSe NCs stabilized by Sn2Se64− to synthesize phase- and composition pure CZTSe thin films, while the mixtures of the S- and Se-containing NCs yield alloyed CZTSSe layers with a predefined S:Se ratio.309


2.4 Precursor deposition and annealing. 
This method is based on the heating-driven transformation (reduction, decomposition, hydrolysis, etc.) of a chalcogenide precursor in a solution containing simultaneously metal ions.310 The nucleation occurs on the surface of a substrate – the optically transparent electrode (OTE) or a semiconductor substrate. The nucleation and growth can be controlled by the rate of the chalcogen precursor decomposition as well as by introducing complexing agents that bind metal ions and reduce the concentration of free cationic species. In this way, Zn-, Sb-, and Ni-doped nanocrystalline CIS films were formed at 80 °C311 as well as CuBiS2 NCs on titania at room temperature.312 Nanocrystalline CuSbS2 and Cu12Sb4S13 films on glass or mesoporous TiO2 and ZrO2 were synthesized by annealing a mixture of metal xanthates at 300 °C in nitrogen atmosphere.313
Nanocrystalline MMC films can be relatively easily produced by spin coating of a liquid layer of molecular precursors on a chosen substrate, in particular, ITO, FTO, or the Mo layer on the soda lime glass. The spin coating is followed by the solvent evaporation and annealing resulting in the dense nanocrystalline MC films. In this way, the nanocrystalline films of CTS314 were prepared. The CIS NCs with a size of around 25 nm can be entrapped in a 3D carbon network by the thermal decomposition of chicken eggshell membranes impregnated by copper and indium salts and thiourea in nitrogen atmosphere at 900 °C.315
A molecular precursor for the synthesis of CTS NCs was prepared by dissolving copper(I) and tin(II) sulfides in 1,2-ethanedithiol.314 In a sol–gel version of this method the precursor sol is first prepared, then it is transformed into the gel and into the final NCs by the annealing. For example, a sol for the CTS NCs deposition was produced from Cu(II) and Sn(II) chlorides, thiourea and methoxyethanol.316 Similar sol was used for the synthesis of nanocrystalline Cu2CdSnS4 films.317 The sol precursor of Cu2FeSnS4 NCs based on thiourea/methoxyethanol combination with metal salts was spin-coated onto the ZnO/ZnS nanorod array, preheated at 150 °C to produce a xerogel and then annealed at 250 °C. This procedure can be repeated several times to increase the thickness of the MMC layer. The method was also applied to form Cu2CoSnS4 and Cu2NiSnS4 layers.318
The spin coating of metal salts with thiourea in ethanol/water mixtures onto nanocrystalline titania followed by the annealing in sulfur vapors was applied to produce TiO2/CZTS nanoheterostructures.319 The films were subjected to the post-synthesis treatment with concentrated HCl leaving intact selectively kesterite CZTS phase. In a similar way, ternary ZnO/CdS/CZTS320 and ZnO/Ag/CIS321 nanoheterostructures were synthesized. The water/ethanol Cu–Zn–Sn–S NC precursor can also be spray-coated on conductive substrates and annealed sequentially in the N2 and Se vapor atmosphere to yield CZTSSe films.322
The light-harvesting MMC films are often produced from the NC inks subjected to the high-temperature sulfidation/selenidation. This method was applied to synthesize the films of CTZS and CZTSe,323–326 CZTSSe,327 Cu2FeSnS4 and Cu2CdSnS4.328 Especially uniform and compact CZTSSe films were formed by the annealing of aqueous NC inks produced by the stabilization of copper and zinc sulfide NCs with Sn2S64− and Sn2S76− MCCs.329 The inks can be relatively easily prepared by dissolving elemental Sn and S in aqueous (NH4)2S solutions with the following addition of Cu2+ and Zn2+ ions. The ∼10 nm CuxS and ZnS NCs are securely stabilized by the electrostatic repulsion of negatively charged MCCs that have a strong affinity to the metal sulfide NC surface.329 The annealing of electrospun polyvinylpyrrolidone (PVP) fibers soaked with a precursor solution yields highly crystalline kesterite CZTS nanofibers.330
The precursors for CTZSe films typically contain metal chlorides, Na2SeO3 and a reducing agent, such as hydrazine.331 Alternatively, the selenide species in the precursor can be generated during the annealing.332 By this method, the nanocrystalline CTZSe films with a grain size of 20–30 nm were formed at moderate temperatures of ∼180 °C.331 Alternatively, the CTZSe films can be produced using selenourea at much higher annealing temperatures (∼500 °C in Se atmosphere).333 Powdered CZTS NCs were synthesized by the sulfidation of a milled mixture of nanocrystalline CuxS, SnS, and ZnS.67 The grain size of the final CZTS powders depends on the sulfidation temperature increasing from ∼14 nm (300 °C) to 85 nm (450 °C).
The sols containing metal dithiocarbamates were used to form Cu2MSnS4 (M = Co2+, Ni2+) NC thin films subsequently converted to selenides by the thermal treatment in Se vapors.334


2.5 Hydrothermal/solvothermal synthesis. 
Formally, this method is a version of the heating-up procedure but carried out under a much higher pressure, when the precursor mixture is confined into an autoclave and heated above the boiling point of the solvent for a prolonged time. For example, the aqueous precursor solutions are typically heated at 180–220 °C for 12–48 h. As the reaction medium is characterized by high temperatures and pressures that cannot be reached in the conventional heating-up process, the products of the hydrothermal/solvothermal synthesis can reveal quite unexpected morphology and phase composition. The chalcogens X are typically introduced in the form of stable compounds releasing chalcogenide ions as a result of the precursor hydrolysis (and reduction) to X2− during the treatment.140 The solvothermal treatment can also be adapted for continuous flow syntheses of MC NCs.335
The hydrothermal treatment (HTT) and similar solvothermal (STT) procedures typically yield microscopic secondary agglomerates of primary MC NCs. To control the morphology (size, shape, porosity, etc.) of the secondary agglomerates various structure-directing agents are introduced during the HTT/STT such as multi-functional molecular species (TGA,336,337 MPA,338 cysteine,339–343 cystine,151 GSH344), organic polymers such as PVP345–350), and surfactants (sodium dodecylsulfonate,351 cetyltrimethylammonium bromide,352 Triton X-100,353etc.).
Highly monodisperse microspheres composed of CIS NCs (Fig. 18a) were produced by the HTT of aqueous suspensions of Cu2O, In(OH)3 containing thioacetamide as a sulfide source and PVP.354 Such scheme excludes any potential impurities such as halide ions and can be applied universally to synthesize various MMC NCs. Using a similar composition of precursors, Cu2O, ZnO, and In(OH)3, mesoporous hierarchical CIZS microspheres composed of 7 nm NCs were produced by the STT in ethanol.355 The synthesis was performed in the presence of CS2 and ethanolamine which in situ formed ethanoldithiocarbamic acid serving as a capping ligand, a structure-directing agent, and a sulfur source.
 Fig. 18  (a and b) SEM image of CIS microspheres produced by the HTT. (b) SEM of CIS, CISe, and CITe nanocubes produced by the HTT. (c) A unit cell of a crystal formed by [Cu5In30S56H4]13− cluster anions and HTEA+ cations. The cation is depicted with violet circles. (d) SEM of hierarchical ZnIn2S4 microspheres. Reprinted with permissions from ref. 354 (a), ref. 372 (b), ref. 373 (c), and ref. 374 (d). Copyright (2013, 2017) The Royal Society of Chemistry (a and c); (2014, 2017) Elsevier (b and d). 
The HTT/STT is broadly applied to produce nanocrystalline CIS,356–362 CIZS,338,352,363 AIS,160,364 ZAIS,365 ZIS,366,367 and CISe.368 By performing the STT of copper and indium chlorides with thioacetamide in OLA or ethylenediamine, the selective formation of respectively zinc blende and wurtzite 10–20 nm NCs was observed.359
The aggregates of CIS NCs were produced by the STT of a mixture of CuCl2, In2O3, oxalic acid and thiourea without additional solvents.369 As the autoclave was kept at 230 °C for 5 days the molten thiourea served as a solvent, while oxalic acid neutralized releasing OH− ions inhibiting the formation of metal hydroxides.
The growth of CIS NCs during the solvothermal synthesis can be restricted by introducing graphene oxide (GO) sheets that supplied the nucleation sites by adsorbing Cu2+ ions and anchored the growing CIS NCs thus inhibiting the agglomeration and growth processes.370 Simultaneous reduction of GO occurs in the autoclave yielding final CIS nanocomposites with reduced graphene oxide (RGO). A similar method was applied for the synthesis of CZTS/RGO heterostructures.202
A reliable control over the stoichiometry of CIX (X = S, Se, Te) materials produced by the HTT/STT can be achieved by using single-molecular precursors containing Cu, In and X in desired proportions. This approach was successfully applied to prepare CIS and CISe from [(Ph3P)2CuIn(SEt)4] and [(Ph3P)2CuIn(SePh)4] complexes, respectively.371
The HTT of a suspension of elemental chalcogens, metallic indium and CuCl2 is a universal method for the preparation of large (100–200 nm) CIX particles crystallized in a cube-like morphology (Fig. 18b).372 The solvothermal synthesis of CIS NCs in OA with a very large excess of sulfur (Cu:In:S = 1:1:100) allows to reach a burst-like nucleation already at 110 °C and produce nearly stoichiometric CIS NCs with a rather small size of 3.5 nm increasing to 4.8 nm as the treatment temperature is elevated to 170 °C.68 The enhanced nucleation also results in a narrow NC size distribution of 7–11%, quite untypical for the solvothermal syntheses.
The HTT of an aqueous suspension of CuI, In2O3, and triethylamine (TEA) at 160 °C for 5 days was reported to yield macrocrystals composed of atomically-resolved anionic pyramidal [Cu5In30S56H4]13− clusters compensated with HTEA+ cations (Fig. 18c).373 A strong binding between the cluster and HTEA+ makes the crystals unsoluble in most solvents but an increase in the ionic strength by adding LiBr results in the fast dissolution of the crystals in DMF. Therefore, the results on further structural and optical characterizations of these ultra-small CIS particles can be expected to appear in the near future. Similar to the successful stabilization of multinary NCs with highly charged MCCs, it can be anticipated that such highly charged CIS anions can potentially be used for the stabilization of larger NCs allowing to produce dense nanocrystalline films for solar light harvesting applications.
Solvothermal synthesis of alloyed Cu(InxB1−x)Se2 NRs in diethylenetriamine at 180 °C allows to vary the composition of the products from CISe to pure CuBSe2 and in this way to tune the NC bandgap from 3.13 (x = 0) to 1.04 eV (x = 1).375
The hydrothermal/solvothermal approaches are broadly applied for the preparation of ternary ZIS materials as well as related compounds with other cations. In particular, the hierarchical ZIS and CdIn2S4 microspheres were prepared by the HTT with TAA as a sulfur source (Fig. 18d).374 Thiourea was used as a sulfur source for the HTT synthesis of nanocrystalline CaIn2S4.376 Copper-doped ZIS nanosheets were produced by the STT from layered Cu-doped NaInS2 nanosheets in the presence of zinc(II) chloride.377
A partial Zn2+ ion exchange with Ag+ in the mesoporous ZnIn2S4 microspheres produced by the HTT results in a transformation of the surface layer of the ZIS microspheres into AgIn5S8378 which forms preferentially in Ag-deficient AIS systems. According to the XPS results, the depth of Ag+ penetration into the ZIS, that is, the thickness of the AIS shell, depends on the starting Ag:In ratio and increases from around 90 nm for Ag:In = 0.5 to 160 nm for Ag:In = 1.25.378 The samples preserve their hierarchical microspheroidal morphology during the ion exchange (Fig. 19a–c). As a result of the transformation the absorption threshold of the microspheres shifts from around 550 nm for ZIS to ∼900 nm for the Ag-ZIS samples with the highest silver content (Fig. 19d).
 Fig. 19  (a–c) SEM images of ZIS microspheres (a, insert shows a resemblance between the microspheres and a flower) and the products of ion exchange with Ag+ for Ag:In ratio of 0.5 (b) and 1.25 (c). (d) Absorption spectra of ZIS microspheres and ion exchange products (Ag:In = 0.5 (H1), 0.75 (H2), 1.0 (H3), and 1.25 (H4)). Reprinted with permissions from ref. 378. Copyright (2015) The Royal Society of Chemistry. 
The morphology of AgGa1−xInxS2 nanoaggregates forming during the HTT at 180 °C is strongly affected by the introduction of alcohols – pentanol or heptanol resulting in the highly porous hierarchical AIGS microspheres composed of nanosheets and nanorods.379 By adjusting the ratio of metal precursors x can be varied from 0 to 1 with the bandgap changing from 2.71 eV to 1.93 eV.
The HTT of zinc and indium acetates combined with GSH and TAA yields ZIS NCs with an average size increasing from 3.2 nm to 7.5 nm as the HTT temperature is elevated from 120 °C to 160 °C.344 The authors assume that TAA serves not only as a S2− source but also forms various chelate complexes with Zn2+ and In3+ ions that gradually combine to form the nuclei and favor in this way the isotropic growth of spherical NCs with the lowest relative surface energy.
The introduction of tartaric acid forwards the HTT process to the preferential formation of cubic ZIS films, while the hexagonal ZIS with an admixture of In(OH)3 is typically obtained with no special additions.380 The latter process can be used for the formation of ZIS/In(OH)3 heterojunctions with the indium hydroxide selectively assembling on the rims of ZIS nanosheets.381 The self-assembling of MoS2 nanosheets on the surface of ZIS nanosheets during the solvothermal reduction of sodium molybdate in the presence of thiourea was observed377 resulting in a very close contact between both layered compounds.
The ZIS replicas of 3D structure of aquatic weed hydrilla that have a high specific surface area can be produced by the HTT using the hydrilla as a sacrificial template.382
A cysteine-assisted HTT was found to produce ultra-thin one-unit-cell ZIS layers.340 An electron paramagnetic study of such nanolayers showed them to be rich with Zn vacancies when the synthesis is performed at 200 °C in contrast to the similar samples produced at a slightly lower temperature of 180 °C.340 The HTT of an inverted micellar aqueous Zn and In salt solution in an ionic liquid 1-butyl-3-methylimidazolium hexafluorophosphate containing thiourea was found to yield the mesoporous ZIS microspheres composed of separate ZIS NCs.353
A two-step HTT process was proposed to anchor cubic 3–6 nm ZIS NCs on the surface of hexagonal 5–8 μm ZIS microspheres.339 The hexagonal carrier was first synthesized by the HTT in glycerol/ethanol solutions in the presence of cysteine, and then the cubic ZIS NCs were formed by the second HTT round in aqueous TAA solutions.339
Ternary Cu2SnS3 microspheres consisting of nanocrystalline platelets were synthesized in triethylene glycol using elemental sulfur reduced during the solvothermal process most probably by the solvent.383 By introducing PVP as a structure-directing agent, the individual 3 nm CTS NCs,346 mesoporous 120–140 nm CTS nanospheres and hexagonal CTS sheets with a lateral size of 0.5–2 μm345 were synthesized in the solvothermal regime. The spherical aggregates of Cu2Zn1−xFexSnS4 NCs were reported to form in the presence of PVP during the HTT with thiourea as a sulfur source.384 The variation of iron content x from 0 to 1 resulted in a bandgap narrowing from 1.52 to 1.21 eV thus increasing the optical response of the kesterite NCs. Similar PVP-assisted solvothermal approach was applied to synthesize alloyed Cu2ZnSn(SxSe1−x)4 NCs with the bandgap varying from 1.11 eV (x = 0) to 1.49 eV (x = 1).350
Nanocrystalline Cu12Sb4S13 samples with a high phase purity and a tunable morphology were synthesized by the STT of a mixture of copper and antimony dedtc complexes in 1-DDT.385
Single-crystalline Cu4Bi4S9 nanoribbons were grown by the STT of toluene suspensions of CuCl, BiCl3, and dodecylamine as a structure-directing agent.386 To form the MMC NCs, CS2 was injected into the autoclave and the mixture kept at 200 °C for 30 h. The nanoribbons are believed to grow via the layer-by-layer stacking of thin Cu4Bi4S9 sheets separated by the dodecylamine linkers, the ribbon thickness depending on the amine concentration.386
AgBiS2 NCs with an average size of ∼8 nm were synthesized in a solvothermal regime in a mixture of cyclohexane and 1-decyl alcohol with CS2 as a sulfur source.387
Similar to the ternary compounds, the hydrothermal/solvothermal preparation of quaternary kesterite-type compounds also typically yields microspherical aggregates of nanocrystal-line primary building blocks – petals, rods, wires, etc. In particular, CZTS microspheres were produced by the STT of metal precursors in ethylene glycol in the presence of thiourea388 and triethylene glycol with the elemental sulfur.389 The microspheres are composed of micropetals which, in turn, are built of 3–5 nm CZTS NCs (Fig. 20a and b).
 Fig. 20  SEM of nanocrystalline CZTS microspheres (a and b) and nanosheets (c and d). Reprinted with permissions from ref. 389 (a and, b) and ref. 407 (c and d). Copyright (2012) American Chemical Society (a and b); (2016) The Royal Society of Chemistry (c and d). 
The 5–10 nm kesterite NCs were produced by the HTT in the presence of sodium dodecylsulfonate as a structure-directing agent and capping ligand in water and water/ethylenediamine mixtures.351 The resulting CZTS phase contains admixtures of SnS, Sn2S3, and ZnS (depending on the solvent composition) that can be removed by the annealing of the NC films at 500 °C in the sulfur vapor atmosphere.351 The kesterite NCs were also synthesized by the STT of metal chlorides with thiourea in ethylene glycol, OLA, and ODE.348
The choice of the solvent can be crucial for the morphology and phase composition of kesterite NCs produced by the solvothermal methods. In particular, the synthesis in triethylenetetramine yields predominantly 160 nm kesterite crystals, while ethylenediamine favors to the formation of wurtzite/kesterite mixtures with a reduced grain size.390 The treatment in ethylene glycol yields kesterite nanoplates with a thickness of around 45 nm, while the synthesis in oleic acid produces mostly the flower-like micro-aggregates of CZTS NCs.390 Similar solvothermal syntheses were abundantly reported for of CZTS NCs,91,349,391–404 Cu2NiSnS4 NCs,405 CZTSe NCs.350,406 The selenide kesterite NCs are typically produced using a Se/N2H4 complex406 or selenium solutions in monoethanolamine350 as Se2− sources.
The phase composition of the products of the hydrothermal syntheses of nanocrystalline kesterites depends considerably on pH of the autoclaved solution. In particular, the formation of pure-phase CZTS NCs was observed only at pH 7, while at a lower pH (4.3–5) and a higher pH (9) additional CTS, Cu2−xS, SnS2, and Sn2S3 phases appeared.394 The average size of CZTS NCs produced by the HTT can be tailored from 3 to 10–11 nm by varying the treatment duration from 6 to 48 h.65
The introduction of TGA during the HTT results in a very uniform size distribution of final kesterite NCs. The thio-acid reduces Cu2+ to Cu+ and provides primary Cu2−xS nuclei for the formation of CZTS NCs via the heating-assisted diffusion of Zn2+ and Sn4+ ions into the bulk of copper sulfide particles.336
A surfactant-free hydrothermal approach to ∼12 nm CZTS NCs was proposed based on the ammonia addition to the conventional aqueous solutions containing metal chlorides and TAA as a sulfur source.408 The ammonia plays a crucial and manifold role. First, the presence of NH3 governs the kinetics of TAA hydrolysis and enhances the S2− release thus providing abundant nuclei for the NC growth. Then, ammonia binds Cu2+ and Zn2+ ions into stable complexes and keeps to a minimum the concentration of free ions thus allowing for a slow NC growth. In this way, the combination of fast nucleation and the slow growth yield small and uniform CZTS NCs without additional surfactants.408
A general approach to CuInS2, Cu2ZnSnS4, and Cu2CoSnS4 NCs was found301,409 based on the STT in ethylene glycol, ethanol, or 2-propanol in the presence of thiourea and tetramethylammonium hydroxide. The post-synthesis ball milling of the products yields 150–250 nm aggregates of 5–8 nm MMC crystals.409 Hierarchical Cu2FeSnS4 microspheres consisting of ∼100 nm-thick stannite nanosheets were synthesized by the STT in ethylene glycol with L-cystine acting both as a capping agent and a sulfur source.151 The HTT of FTO plates in a water/ethanol solution of CuAc2, ZnAc2, SnCl4 and thiourea at 200 °C for 24 h yields films of nanocrystalline CZTS microplatelets composed of 10–30 nm kesterite NCs.410 The kesterite nanosheets were also produced by the STT of electrochemically deposited mixture of Cu, Sn, and Zn in ethanol suspension of elemental sulfur at 200 °C (Fig. 20c and d).407
The HTT of Cu2S nanowire arrays in the presence of InCl3 and TAA as a sulfur source results in the incorporation of In3+ ions into the lattice of copper sulfide nanowires and their transformation into CIS nanowires composed of nanosheets (Fig. 21a and b).411
 Fig. 21  (a and b) SEM of Cu2S (a) and CIS nanowires (b) produced by In3+ incorporation during the HTT. (c and d) TEM of CuxS NCs (c) and CuGaS2 NCs (d) produced by the solvothermal incorporation of Ga ions. (e) TEM of CdS nanowires decorated with CdIn2S4 nanosheets. (f) Cross-sectional SEM of Si nanowire/TiO2/ZnIn2S4 nanostructure. Reprinted with permissions from ref. 411 (a and b), ref. 412 (c and d), ref. 415 (e), and ref. 416 (f). Copyright (2017) Elsevier (a and b); (2016) The Royal Society of Chemistry (c and d); (2017, 2015) Springer (e and f). 
The CuxS NCs grown in OLA were used as a template for the growth of CuGaS2 NCs by Ga incorporation in the solvothermal regime.412 The procedure took place in OLA at 210 °C with the participation of Ga(acac)3 complexes and resulted in a change of the NC shape from roughly spherical for CuxS to elongated NR-like for CGS NCs (Fig. 21c and d).
Hierarchical CIS microspheres grown by the solvothermal synthesis in ethylene glycol were also found to form from primary copper sulfide microspheres acting as a sacrificial template and translating the characteristic “microsphere-of-nanoplatelets” morphology to the resulting ternary copper indium sulfide.413
The interaction of Cu2O nanocubes with tin-sulfide MCCs [Sn2S6]4− or [Sn2S7]6− converts the former into the hollow CuxS nanoboxes capped by the tin complexes414 which can be converted into the hollow kesterite (at pH 12) or wurtzite (at pH 7) CZTS nanoboxes upon the introduction of Zn2+ salts and the HTT at 190 °C.
The ternary CuS/CdIn2S4/ZnIn2S4417 and ZnS–In2S3–CuS418 composites synthesized respectively by the microwave-assisted HTT403 and the STT in pyridine,418 most probably, also started to form from the primary copper sulfide NCs. This reaction appeared to be incomplete and yielded complex nanoheterostructures. In a reverse way, Cu+ can be incorporated into the In2S3 nanoplates in the solvothermal regime and the optical properties of the resulting CIS nanoplates tuned by varying the Cu:In ratio.108,419 The spinel In3−xS4 NCs were used as a sacrificial precursor of 5–10 nm CIS NCs in a solvothermal synthesis with Cu(dedtc)2 at 180 °C.420 The synthesis showed a remarkable phase selectivity yielding wurtzite CIS, zinc blende CIS, and spinel CuIn5S8 NCs when performed in DDT, OLA, and a mixture of OLA with oleic acid, respectively.
The hydrothermal/solvothermal processes are broadly used for the synthesis of various binary and more complex heterostructures. For this purpose, the formation of MC NCs is carried out in the presence of a prospective carrier, for example, TiO2 or other wider-bandgap semiconductor. Alternatively, both components of the desired heterostructure can be formed simultaneously in the autoclave. In particular, TiO2/CIS composites were synthesized by the STT of titania sols in ethanol suspension of copper and indium chlorides with thiourea as a sulfur source.421 In a similar way, TiO2/CIS422 and TiO2/AIS423 heterostructures based on the commercial nanocrystalline TiO2 were produced.
The solvothermal treatment of CdS nanowires suspended in ethylene glycol with InCl3 and cysteine at 200 °C results in the modification of the nanowire surface with CdIn2S4 nanosheets (Fig. 21e).415 A similar procedure was developed for the synthesis of CdS nanowires decorated with ZnIn2S4 nanosheets.424
The HTT deposition of ZIS onto the nanocrystalline titania films results in a uniform cover composed of ZnIn2S4 nanosheets with a thickness of 20 nm and a lateral size of around 20 μm.425 Similar ZIS microflakes form during the HTT on the titania-coated Si nanowires416 (Fig. 21f), TiO2 NRs,119 and hollow titania microspheres,426 indicating that the sheet-like morphology is general for the ternary In-based compounds with Zn and Cd, regardless of the substrate used for the deposition.
The solvothermal approaches were applied to produce numerous nanocomposites of multinary NCs with TiO2,427–430 ZnO,431,432 g-C3N4,337,433–437 and carbon nanomaterials.438–444 The HTT of Zn/In/Ag salts and TAA in the presence of MoS2 flakes at 180 °C results in the ZAIS/MoS2 heterostructures with the MMC component having a nominal composition of Ag0.26In0.8Zn4S5.33.88
A multi-stage HTT approach was developed to produce ITO/ZnO NR/CAIS nanocomposites.445 The synthesis starts with the “seeding” of a ZnO NC layer on the ITO surfaces by the sol–gel/annealing followed by the HTT in aqueous solutions of zinc nitrate and hexamethylenetetramine. The latter procedure is a generally accepted protocol for the synthesis of ZnO NR arrays. In the next step, the ITO/ZnO/ZnS heterostructure was produced by the HTT in the aqueous TAA solution followed by the ion exchange with silver(I) to yield a ITO/ZnO/Ag2S composite. Finally, the films were subjected to the HTT in triethylene glycol for the In3+ incorporation and exposed to the aqueous Cu2+ solution to form the final ITO/ZnO/CAIS heterostructure. A similar multi-stage process was applied to form ZnO/CIS (ZnO → ZnO/ZnS → ZnO/CuS → ZnO/CIS),446 ZnO/AgSbS2 (ZnO → ZnO/ZnS → ZnO/Ag2S → ZnO/AgSbS2),447 and ZnO/Ag–Cu–Sb–S (ZnO → ZnO/ZnS → ZnO/CuS → ZnO/CuS/Ag2S → ZnO/Ag–Cu–Sb–S) composites.448
Ultra-thin CIZS nanosheets with a varied Cu:In ratio were deposited onto the surface of TiO2 nanotube arrays by the STT in triethylene glycol at 180 °C.449 The CZTS nanosheet arrays were formed exploiting the titania nanosheets as a structure-directing template by the STT in ethylene glycol in the presence of PVP.347
A binary BiVO4–BiFeO3 heterostructure was sensitized with CuInS2 NCs by the solvothermal deposition in ethanol in the presence of thiourea and cetyltrimethylammonium bromide.450
The STT of lanthanum titanate in ethylene glycol suspension of Cu, Sn, and Zn chlorides with TAA yields La2Ti2O7/CZTS nanocomposites with ∼50 nm kesterite NCs attached to the 10 nm thin titanate nanosheets.451


2.6 Aqueous synthesis. 
As discussed above, the conventional heating-up, hot-injection and solvothermal approaches typically produce MMC NCs capped with protecting ligands and surfactants that impede the interfacial electron transfer in the NC-based light-harvesting systems. To increase the efficiency of the photoinduced charge transfers as well as to render the MMC NCs soluble in polar solvent the original bulky organic stabilizers such as OLA, 1-DDT and others are substituted with smaller multifunctional ligands, such as mercaptocarboxylic acids by ligand exchange procedures. Summarily, the synthesis of MMC NCs ready for the implementation as sensitizers and light-harvesting moieties includes also the procedures of post-synthetic purification and ligand exchange leaving behind a lot of wastes to be recycled and/or disposed off. In view of the fact, alternative methods of the direct synthesis of MMC NCs in polar media, in particular, in water are incessantly searched and optimized. As the synthesis temperature in such systems is naturally limited by the solvent boiling temperature (100 °C for aqueous syntheses) and the reactivity of various metal ions can be considerably inhibited by partial hydrolysis or solvation effects, the preparation of crystalline products with a controlled NC size and a reasonably narrow size distribution becomes a challenge for many MMC compounds.
Typically aqueous syntheses are based on the interaction between metal complexes with mercaptocarboxylic acids or other multifunctional molecules (cysteine, GSH, etc.) and a chalcogenide source. In this approach, CIS NCs were produced from mercaptoacetate (thioglycolate) complexes of Cu and In and Na2S.452,453 Such synthesis typically yields relatively small, 2–3 nm, colloidal CIS NCs that show a crystalline motif of the tetragonal chalcopyrite.55,453 As discussed in the previous section, the light absorption properties of CIS NCs are determined mostly by the copper content. For the non-stoichiometric NCs, which is the most often case for aqueous syntheses, the NC composition can be conveniently expressed by a molar ratio Cu:In:S = xCu:yIn:zS. The molar fraction of copper, xCu, can be varied in a broad range yielding a spectrum of non-stoichiometric CIS and CIZS NCs with a band edge shifting toward smaller wavelengths (and the bandgap – to lower energies) as xCu is elevated (Fig. 4a).55
The light absorbance in the visible range increases almost linearly with an increase in the copper content (Fig. 4a, inset). Similar trends were observed for the TGA-capped AIS and ZAIS NCs.59,63 The heating of the precursor solution results in a gradual growth of TGA-capped CIS and AIS NCs reflecting in a red shift of the absorption threshold and PL band (Fig. 22a). At that, the synthesis in mixed water/glycerol media allows to increase the post-synthesis heating temperature to ∼120 °C thus achieving deeper crystallization and NC growth as compared to pure aqueous AIS colloids.63
 Fig. 22  (a) PL spectra and photographs of AIS NCs in water/glycerol mixtures before (curve 1) and after heating at 120 °C for 5 (2), 10 (3), 15 (4), 20 (5), 25 (6), 30 (7), 35 (8), and 40 min (9). (b) Relative population of the size-selected AIS NCs in fractions produced by the size-selective precipitation (upper panel) and a photograph of the size-selected AIS/ZnS NCs under the UV excitation (fraction numbers given under the image). (c) Size distribution of ZAIS NCs in two of the size-selected fractions. (d) Atomic In-to-Ag and Zn-to-In ratios determined by XPS for the size-selected TGA-capped AIS/ZnS NCs. Reprinted with permissions from ref. 63 (a) and ref. 59 (b–d). Copyright (2015) Springer (a) and (2017) American Chemical Society (b–d). 
Interaction between copper and indium iodides and Na2S in pyridine in the absence of any capping ligands yields amorphous CIS nanoparticles that were used as the precursor inks for the preparation of CISe films.122
CIS and CIZS NCs were synthesized from the MPA-complexes of metal ion precursors with Na2S and kept at ∼100 °C to finish the crystallization.454 As a second step, a ZnS shell was deposited onto the NCs to enhance their stability toward the aggregation and corrosion and to increase considerably the PL efficiency.454 A similar approach was applied to produce aqueous GSH-capped AIS,455 CIS and CZTS NCs.456
ZAIS NCs with an excellent stability were produced directly in aqueous solutions of a mixture of polyacrylic acid and MAA.457
An elegant way of the post-synthesis size control of AIS NCs was proposed by Torimoto et al.72 and based on the size-selective photoetching. An original ensemble of multi-sized NCs is illuminated in the oxygen-saturated water with a laser wavelength corresponding to the absorption band edge of a fraction of larger NCs (Fig. 23a). The oxidative photocorrosion, that can be described by a brutto-equation: AgInS2 + 4O2 = Ag+ + In3+ + 2SO42−, results in gradual dissolution of such NCs and then more shorter-wavelength laser light is applied to photocorrode the next fraction of smaller NCs. By repeating this procedure consecutively with a decreasing excitation wavelength (increasing quantum energy), several fractions of the size-selected AIS NCs can be isolated with an average size varying from 5.1 (Eg = 1.8 eV, Fig. 23b) to 3.0 nm (Eg = 2.4 eV, Fig. 23c).72
 Fig. 23  (a) Absorption spectra of original and photoetched AIS NCs (the laser wavelength used for the photoetching is displayed on the figure). (b and c) Size distribution of original (b) and the smallest photoetched AIS NCs (c). Reprinted with permissions from ref. 72. Copyright (2012) American Chemical Society. 
The post-synthesis separation of differently sized fractions of aqueous colloidal CIS and AIS NCs can also be performed by the size-selective precipitation. The method is based on a size dependence of the coagulation kinetics of colloidal NCs – upon the addition of a non-solvent, the larger NCs coagulate and precipitate faster than smaller NCs. By repeating additions of the non-solvent (2-propanol in this particular case59,455) with the separation of the precipitated NCs and their redispersion in aqueous solutions, at least ten size-selected fraction of AIS and ZAIS NCs can be extracted from the original TGA-stabilized aqueous AIS and ZAIS colloids.59
As the average size in the separated AIS/ZAIS fractions decreases, the population of the fractions becomes lower (Fig. 22b) while the absorption band edge and the PL band maximum shift to shorter wavelength resulting in a spectacular change of the PL properties (photograph on Fig. 22b). It should be noted that the size-selective precipitation allows separating NCs with very small differences in the average size. For example, the series of 10 fractions depicted in Fig. 22b shows an average size varying from around 2 to 3.5–4 nm (Fig. 22c). At the same time, the composition of such size-selected AIS (ZAIS) remains roughly the same, indicating the uniform distribution of elements in the original polydisperse NC solutions.59
Colloidal AIS NCs passivated by only the surface charge were produced via a reaction between silver(I) and indium(III) nitrates and (NH4)2S in formamide with an excess of sulfide ions.58
ZAIS NCs in water were synthesized in the presence of GSH via prolonged heating of the precursor solution at ∼100 °C.458
Similar to the heating-up and solvothermal syntheses, the ternary/quaternary Ag-based NCs can also be produced in water by the partial ion exchange. For example, colloidal Ag2S NCs stabilized by bovine serum albumin were exposed to heating at 90 °C in the presence of GSH and In3+ resulting in the indium penetration into the lattice and the formation of AIS NCs.459 By introducing Zn2+/GSH at room temperature a ZnS shell on the surface of AIS NCs can be formed, while heating of such core/shell NCs at 100 °C results in the penetration of Zn into the core and the formation of alloyed ZAIS NCs.459 The average size of original “template” Ag2S NCs can be varied from 2.5 nm to ∼7 nm by adjusting the Ag/S ratio, thus opening the possibilities of the size-selective synthesis of AIS and ZAIS NCs.459
Various multi-functional molecules were also successfully applied for the syntheses of quaternary Cu2ZnSnS4 NCs in aqueous solutions, including TGA, MPA, and GSH. In particular, aqueous colloidal 5–20 nm CZTS NCs were produced from a mixture of MPA complexes of Cu2+, Zn2+, and Sn2+via a mild thermal treatment at ∼90 °C at pH 10 for 24 h.330 Similar syntheses were reported with TGA460 and GSH.461
The content of organics in the CZTS NCs prepared in aqueous solutions can be minimized by applying a mixture of thiourea and Sn2S64− anions as stabilizers.462


2.7 Ion exchange. 
The ion exchange method can be applied for the preparation of MC-containing heterostructures from zinc oxide nanoarchitectures. The broad utilization of zinc oxide in solar cells and other light harvesting systems is largely compromised by its chemical instability in acids and alkalis as well as in sulfide/polysulfide solutions typically used in the NC-sensitized liquid-junction solar cells. At the same time, the instability of ZnO can be used to produce new composite nanoheterostructures by the partial or complete ion exchange of both oxygen anions and zinc cations with elements forming less soluble metal chalcogenides.
For example, ZnO can be partially converted into ZnS by the HTT in the presence of thioacetamide. In this way ZnO NT arrays were converted into ZnO/ZnS NT arrays and then – into ternary ZnO/ZnS/Ag2S nanotubular composites by a partial Zn2+ exchange in zinc sulfide with Ag+.463 Finally, by incorporating In3+ ions the ternary composite can be converted into ZnO/AgInS2 nanotube (NT) arrays preserving the morphology of the original zinc oxide substrate.463
A partial exchange of Zn2+ with Cu2+ in 2D ZnIn2S4 nanosheets grown on titania surface yields CIZS nanosheets with an increased light sensitivity in the visible spectral range.464 The CZTS–Ag2S heterostructures were produced by the partial Cu+-to-Ag+ exchange in CZTS NCs.195 CdIn2S4 was synthesized from the nanocrystalline cadmium sulfide by the gas-phase cation exchange.89


2.8 Successive ionic layer adsorption and reaction. 
The successive ionic layer adsorption and reaction (SILAR) is one of the most simple and, at the same time, potent and versatile methods of the preparation of various oxide-chalcogenide heterostructures with “conventional” binary MC NCs, such as CdS or PbS used in the photocatalysis and the liquid-junction semiconductor-sensitized solar cells (SSSCs). The method is based on the alternating immersion of a metal oxide film into a solution of metal salt and a solution of a chalcogenide ions source. This procedure results in the formation of a metal chalcogenide layer on the metal oxide surface and is referred to as a single SILAR cycle. By repeating the SILAR cycle many times, the content of MC can be controllably increased. Simultaneously, the size of MC NCs and their optical properties can be tuned as well.
The SILAR procedure was successfully adapted for the preparation of heterostructures based on MMC NCs by using several metal precursor solutions or a single solution containing a mixture of metal ions in a pre-set proportion. In particular, layers of CIS NCs can be formed on the surface of mesoporous titania (Fig. 24a),465 TiO2 NT arrays (Fig. 24b),466,467 and TiO2 NRs468 by the SILAR procedure from aqueous precursor solutions. The 10–12 nm-thick CIS nanosheets were produced by the SILAR on the surface of TiO2-coated FTO substrates followed by the annealing at 370–500 °C. The post-SILAR annealing in the N2 atmosphere is a typical procedure and aims at an increased crystallinity of CIS NCs.468,469 In the case of titania NT arrays an additional ultrasound treatment can be applied to enhance the penetration of SILAR solutions into the nanotube voids and to ensure a uniform coverage with CIS NCs.470 To gain a better control over the MMC NC size and shape, the SILAR procedure is often performed after the preliminary decoration of the metal oxide surface with ex situ formed “seed” NCs. In this way, the CIS NCs were grown by the SILAR on TiO2 seeded with 2.5 nm CIS NCs.295
 Fig. 24  (a and b) TEM of CIS NCs deposited by the SILAR on the surface of mesoporous titania (a) and TiO2 NT arrays (b). (c) SEM of SILAR-deposited AIS NCs on the titania NT arrays. Reprinted with permissions from ref. 465 (a), ref. 467 (b), and ref. 471 (c). Copyright (2016, 2011) The Royal Society of Chemistry (a and b); (2015) Elsevier (c). 
The SILAR procedure was successfully used for the decoration of TiO2 NT arrays with 15 nm AIS NCs (Fig. 24c).471 At that, the Ag2S and In2S3 layers were consecutively deposited onto the titania NTs each in a single SILAR cycle and the AIS NCs formation apparently occurred spontaneously as a result of the metal ion interdiffusion. Similarly, TiO2/AIS heterostructures can be produced from layered TiO2/Ag2S/In2S3 precursor with the silver sulfide layer produced by the SILAR and the indium sulfide NCs formed by the chemical bath deposition. TiO2/In2S3/AIS composites form also as a result of Ag+ diffusion into the indium sulfide layer of the binary TiO2/In2S3 nanowires synthesized by the SILAR.472
The post-synthesis alloying approach can be applied to other ternary NCs containing mobile Ag+ ions. In particular, TiO2/AgSbS2 films were produced by the annealing of a TiO2/Ag2S/Sb2S3 heterostructure with both metal sulfides deposited by the SILAR.473 The alloying of SILAR-deposited Ag2S and Bi2S3 NCs into a nanocrystalline AgBiS2 layer on titania occurs already at a moderate annealing temperature of 150 °C in the ambient air conditions.474
A three-layer ZnO NR/CuS/Sb2S3 structure was converted into ZnO/CuSbS2 composite via the annealing at 350 °C necessary for the activation of a solid-state reaction 2CuS + Sb2S3 = 2CuSbS2 + S.475,476


2.9 Electrodeposition. 
The electrodeposition is a convenient method of the formation of nanocrystalline and bulk MC films on conducting substrates. The method is used in two versions – the electrodeposition of MC NCs or the electrodeposition of metals constituting the MC compounds followed by sulfidation/selenidation of the metallic deposits. In particular, CIS NCs were electrodeposited from aqueous solutions of Na2S2O3 and metal chlorides at −1 V versus Ag/AgCl and then annealed to increase the crystallinity of the MC layer.477 The electrodeposition of CIS NCs from DMSO solutions containing elemental sulfur and GO is accompanied by the electrochemical GO reduction and yields RGO/CIS heterostructures.478 Porous nanocrystalline CISe layers were electrodeposited from aqueous solutions containing copper and indium chlorides, selenite ions and additions of potassium hydrophthalate and sulfamic acid aimed at the equilibration of the redox-potentials of all the precursors of CISe NCs.479,480 CISe nanowire arrays (Fig. 25a) were prepared by the electrochemical deposition of the MMC NCs into the pores of anodized alumina membranes using H2SeO3 as a selenium source followed by the chemical etching of the original template membrane.481
 Fig. 25  (a) SEM of CuInSe2 nanowire arrays produced by the electrodeposition. (b) SEM of ZnO NR arrays with a CuInS2 layer electrodeposited in a pulse regime with a different pulse frequency (indicated on the figure). (c) SEM of titania NT arrays and electro-deposited TiO2/CIS NT arrays. Reprinted with permissions from ref. 481 (a), ref. 482 (b), and ref. 483 (c). Copyright (2017, 2011) Elsevier (a and c); (2015, 2016) The Royal Society of Chemistry (b). 
The pulse electrodeposition was shown to be more beneficial for the uniformity and quality of the deposited CIS layer as compared with the stationary electrodeposition.482 The rectangular pulses with a frequency of 1 KHz with alternating deposition voltage and the relaxation voltage (V = 0) allow for the continuous nucleation/deposition and diffusion of fresh reactants, respectively. At that, both too high (1 MHz) and too low (1 Hz) pulse frequencies compromise the quality of the electrodeposited CIS layer on the surface of ZnO nanorod arrays (Fig. 25b) indicating that a balance between the electrochemical reaction and the reactant diffusion into the nanorod arrays should be maintained.482
The pulsed electrodeposition was also reported to produce smooth layers of CIS NCs on the surface of TiO2 NT arrays used as a working electrode.483,484 The CIS NC load depends on the number of applied half-second pulses. The pulse regime favors to the formation of uniform 20 nm CIS NCs and avoids clogging of the NT entrances (Fig. 25c). A similar effect was achieved by alternating the stationary electrodeposition with an ultrasound treatment of the TiO2 NT arrays in the precursor solution which favors the uniform penetration of the electrolyte into the voids of the titania NTs.109 In both cases, Na2S2O3 was used as a sulfur source.
Ce-Doped nanocrystalline tetragonal CIS with a grain size of 35–50 nm was electrodeposited from triethanolamine solutions with a cerium content varying up to 15 molar% with respect to In.485
A nanocrystalline Cu2SnS3 layer was electrodeposited from aqueous solution containing Na2S2O3 and trisodium citrate at room temperature on the surface of ZnO NR array on FTO serving in this synthesis as a working electrode.486 The citrate anions were introduced to balance the reduction rates of copper(II) and tin(II). Thin films of CdIn2Se4 were potentio-statically electrodeposited on the stainless steel from acidic solutions containing H2SeO3.487
The p-type CIS films were produced by the sulfidation of electrodeposited copper and indium metallic layers.488–490 Sodium citrate is typically introduced into the electrolyte to equalize the rates of copper and indium deposition, since the ions have different reduction potentials.490 To exclude the presence of CuxS phases in the final CIS films they were additionally etched in an aqueous KCN solution.490
The CZTS nanosheet films were prepared by the STT of the electrodeposited metal mixture in ethanol suspensions of sulfur.407,491 The deposition results in a ∼350 nm layer of CZTS nanosheets with an average thickness of 30 nm.
The nanocrystalline CZTS/CZTSe films were produced by the co-electrodeposition of copper, zinc, and tin492–496 followed by the sulfidation at ∼600 °C in the H2S/S atmosphere, or the selenization via the STT in ethanol suspension of elemental Se at 200 °C.492 Similar to the case of ternary compounds, sodium citrate was introduced in the precursor solutions to level off the rates of the Cu, Zn, and Sn electrodeposition.
Polycrystalline dense Cu3BiS3 films were electrodeposited onto Mo-covered glass using thiourea as a sulfur source.497 The CBS has a direct bandgap of 1.6 eV favorable for the light-harvesting applications and therefore future progress of the electrodeposition toward the formation of nanocrystalline films with a controlled morphology can be envisaged for this ternary semiconductor.


2.10 Microwave treatment. 
The microwave heating of liquid dispersive media becomes a popular method of the preparation of nanocrystalline MMC compounds. The method is very fast as only a few-minute treatment is sufficient for the formation of well crystallized products due to the rapid and uniform heating of the dispersive medium in the microwave field. For example, GSH-capped 2–3 nm CIS498 and AIS/ZAIS355 NCs were produced in water by the microwave heating of the precursor solution of corresponding metal complexes with GSH. The microwave heating was used as an assisting procedure in the aqueous synthesis of cysteine- and TGA-capped CIS NCs,499 CIGS NCs in benzyl acetate,167 CZTS NCs in OLA500,501 and TOPO/OLA mixtures.502 In the latter case, the application of the microwave irradiation allowed to reduce the synthesis temperature and time to 165 °C and 10 min respectively.
The microwave synthesis of CIS NCs in propylene glycol can be carried out with a variety of sulfide sources, in particular, thiourea, TGA, Na2S2O3, cystine, and thiosemicarbazide.503 Similar sulfur sources were probed for the microwave-assisted synthesis of orthorhombic AIS NCs in ethylene glycol.504 The 3 nm CIS and CISe NCs were prepared in water in the presence of TGA by the microwave heating of a solution of metal chlorides and sodium sulfide or selenide.505
CZTS NC inks were produced by the microwave heating with thioacetamide as a sulfur source.506–508 Because of the rapidity of the synthesis, it can be realized in a flow regime with methylethylketone/1-DDT mixtures as solvents.509 The introduction of TGA allows decreasing of the size of flow-synthesized CZTS NCs down to 3–5 nm.509 A combination of thiourea and TGA was used to form alloyed Cu2(Zn1−xCox)SnS4 NCs (0 ≤ x ≤ 1) in ethylene glycol under the microwave heating.510


2.11 Other methods. 
A solid state reaction occurring in a mortar between Cu2S, Ag2S, Ga2S3, and In2S3 results in CuGa2In3S8 and AgGa2In3S8 powders with spectral responses extending to 600–650 nm.511 Presumably, such reactions may lead to nanocrystalline products with unexpected properties when conducted in the presence of suitable surfactants or/and capping agents.
The nanocrystalline CISe layers grown by the pulsed laser deposition512 are characterized by a gradient structure, the layer composition changing from In2Se3 to CISe along the film cross-section. Such gradient structure, that can be varied by adjusting the deposition conditions,512 is very favorable for the directed transport of the photogenerated charge carriers in the solar cells – the fact making the pulsed laser deposition a promising alternative to other synthetic protocols. This method was also applied to produce quaternary CZTS NCs.513,514 By combining the pulsed laser deposition with simultaneous electrophoresis, the CZTS NC films on Mo substrates can be formed by this method.513 The stoichiometry of the kesterite phase produced from an amorphous CuS/SnS/ZnS precursor depends on the intensity (fluence) of the laser irradiation (Fig. 26a).
 Fig. 26  (a) Composition of the CZTS films produced by the pulsed laser deposition as a function of the laser fluence. (b–d) SEM of CBTS NC film produced by the sputtering (b), CISe nanowires formed by the molecular beam epitaxy (c), and CuSbS2 film synthesized by the atomic layer deposition (d). (e and f) AFM image (e) and roughness profile (f) of the single-layer MnSb2S4 nanosheets. Reprinted with permissions from ref. 514 (a), ref. 520 (b), ref. 521 (c), ref. 524 (d), and ref. 529 (e and f). Copyright (2017) Elsevier (a); (2016, 2017) The Royal Society of Chemistry (b, c, e and f); (2017) American Chemical Society (d). 
Various evaporation/sputtering techniques are broadly applied for the deposition of both nanocrystalline and compact MMC films for the photovoltaic applications, in particular, CIGS,515 (ZnSe)0.85(CIGS)0.15,516 CZTS,517 Cu3SbS4,518 and Cu2BaSnS4.519,520 Cu2BaSnS4 kesterite was deposited in the form of a 100 nm-thick film with a grain size of 100–200 nm (Fig. 26b) exhibiting a bandgap of around 2 eV and intrinsic Cu deficiency leading to the p-type conductivity.519
Highly crystalline CISe nanowires were synthesized by using the molecular beam epitaxy (Fig. 26c).521 The stoichiometry of CISe nanowires was controlled by adjusting the evaporation rates of copper and selenium. ZnIn2S4 nanosheets assembled into the hierarchical microspheres were grown on {100} silicon substrates by the chemical vapor deposition using InP and ZnS as raw materials heated at 1100 °C.522
Bulk chalcostibite samples produced by the mechanochemical alloying of the elemental precursors were used as a source for the electron beam evaporation and deposition of thin CuSbSe2 films with absorption coefficients higher than 105 cm−1 and a bandgap of 1.18 eV.523 Similar films were also deposited by the atomic layer deposition524 (Fig. 26d) and the radio-frequency magnetron sputtering from Cu2S and Sb2S3 targets.525 The CuSbS2 films can be formed in a two-stage process combining the thermal evaporation/re-deposition of a copper layer onto the surface of a Sb2S3 film produced by the chemical bath deposition followed by the annealing in the sulfur atmosphere.526
Thin films of CIS NCs can be produced by the spray deposition of mixed aqueous solution of metal chlorides and thiourea onto glass substrates followed by the heating at 300 °C.527 Nanocrystalline Cu2SnS3 films with a Cu:Sn ratio varying from 0 to 1 and a grain size of 10–30 nm were deposited by the spray pyrolysis with thiourea as a sulfur source.528
The free-standing single-layer MnSb2S4 nanosheets were produced by the thermal elimination of hydrazine molecules from the interlayer galleries of a layered MnSb2S4(N2H4)2 precursor (Fig. 26e and f).529 The bandgap of MnSb2S4 nanosheets, 1.69 eV, is favorable for potential applications in the light-harvesting systems.
The CIS NCs were synthesized by a high-energy mechano-chemical treatment in a planetary ball mill from elemental Cu, In, and S constituents in the argon atmosphere.530 The procedure yielded comparatively large 17–24 nm CIS particles. Nanocrystalline CTS powders with a grain size of around 45 nm can also be produced by the mechanical ball milling of a mixture of elemental copper, tin, and sulfur in ethanol in the H2S atmosphere.531,532 By adding PVP and water the stable inks suitable for the jet – printing of Cu2SnS3-based light-harvesting layers were prepared. A gram-scale mechanochemical synthesis of CZTS NCs from elemental precursors with no solvents or additives was proposed by D. K. Le et al.533 The synthesis takes only 0.5 h to finish indicating that a self-propagating solid-state chemical reaction is induced by the mechanochemical treatment.

3 Solar cells based on MMC NCs
The attempts of the light energy conversion into the electric power stem from the photoelectric effect discovered by Henri Bequerel in 1839.533,534 It should be noted that Bequerel observed illumination-induced electric currents between two electrodes immersed in a liquid electrolyte. Later, the photoeffect was also observed for solid electrolytes and a focus of the solar cell studies was turned to solid photovoltaic materials for many decades to come, till the liquid-junction dye-sensitized solar cells (DSSCs) were introduced.
The first to appear were the silicon-based solar cells. Silicon has a bandgap of 1.1 eV which is close to the optimal value of ∼1.3 eV (the solar irradiation spectrum peak) necessary for the achievement of the highest photoconversion efficiency. Moreover, Si is a photostable, low-toxic and earth-abundant material.5,536,537 The Si-based solar cells, typically referred to as the first-generation semiconductor solar cells, though being unrivaled in terms of the ratio of conversion efficiency vs. production cost, have a number of shortcomings. First, the silicon solar cell technology requires a huge amount of very pure silicon. Typically, the photovoltaic technologies used rejected materials from the semiconductor industry and the necessary amount of raw materials can be maintained only if both industries are developed with the same rate, which is doubtful in view of a recent drastic growth of interest to the solar energy harvesting. The solar cells based on amorphous silicon, which is much less expensive, emerged in 1960s–1970s.535 Another fundamental shortcoming of silicon is the indirect character of the interband electron transitions resulting in a comparatively low linear absorption coefficients. At least a 100 μm-thick silicon layer is required for the complete solar light absorption on the Earth surface thus putting limitations on the minimal thickness (and, therefore, the cost) of the solar cells.
The above shortcomings stimulated a search for new light-harvesting materials, in particular, among the visible and NIR light sensitive MC semiconductors, such as binary cadmium, lead, and copper chalcogenides, ternary indium-based MCs, and more complex quaternary compositions.6,14,17,21,22,42,45–48,535 It was found already in mid-1980s that some of the materials, in particular, Cu2S and CdTe (studied since 1960s), CuInSe2, CuGaSe2, CuInS2 and their alloys CIGS/CIGSe (studied from 1970s), and kesterite CZTS (studied from 1990s), sputtered as thin films can be used as the solar cell light absorbers.5–11,14,15,17,21,22,37,38,40,42,44–48,125,534,538 Most of such compounds are direct-bandgap semiconductors with high absorption coefficients and, therefore, much thinner (1–2 μm) absorber layers are required for the efficient solar light harvesting.536 Additionally, the alloying of several components can be used for a precise variation of the absorber bandgap. The thin-film cells are typically referred to as the second-generation semiconductor cells and showed efficiencies of 20% and higher.42,45,46,48,539 However, the cell production puts very rigorous requirements to the purity of sources used for the thin-film sputtering, and therefore, the second-generation cells are very expensive and found applications mostly in the aerospace industry,42,45,535,540 where the cell cost is not so critical.
Recently, the thin-film solar cell technology gained a new impetus by discovering the possibility of using NC or molecular precursor “inks” for the preparation of nanocrystalline MC thin-film absorbers.4–6,8,9,11,21,22,36,45,46 The ternary and quarternary compounds can be prepared by using well-established methods of the colloidal chemistry and concentrated to the form of inks. In a similar way, other components of the solar cell (metal contact, n-type component, barrier layers, etc.) can also be prepared as the NC inks so that the entire solar cell can be produced by using the ink-jet printing technologies making such solar cells competitive to the conventional silicon-based devices.4,22,46,541–544 In the simplest layout, the NC-based thin-film solar cell comprises a layer of the light-harvesting MC NCs “squeezed” between an electron transporting layer and a hole transporting layer that facilitate the extraction and transport of the photogenerated charge carriers (Fig. 27a, upper structure).
 Fig. 27  Outline of the MMC NC-based solid-state (a) and liquid-junction (b) solar cells. 
Alternatively, the MC NCs can be blended with another light-sensitive components, such as conjugated polymers, fullerene derivatives, hybrid organo–inorganic perovskites, etc. to form bulk p/n heterojunctions (Fig. 27a, lower structure). The photoexcitation of one or both components results in the generation of charge carriers spatially separated by the electric field originating from the p/n heterojunction. As shown below, in some cells the MC NCs can simultaneously act as a light absorber and a charge transporting layer.
The idea of devising of a solar cell with two electrodes – a light-sensitive photoanode/photocathode and a catalytically-active counter electrode, both connected by a liquid electrolyte stems directly from the Bequerels experiments on the photoelectric effect.534,545 This idea was first realized by Fujishima and Honda in their photoelectrochemical cell for the water splitting on a single rutile crystal coupled to Pt.
The sensitization of wide-bandgap semiconductors, like TiO2, with external molecular absorbers is also one of the oldest conceptions of the solid-state photochemistry introduced by Vogel in 1883 for silver halide emulsions used in the photographic process.534,545 A combination of a liquid-electrolyte- (“liquid-junction”-) based photoelectrochemical cell with the dye sensitization approach gave rise to the DSSCs belonging to the third-generation solar cells, according to the generally accepted classification.
The sensitization effect was also observed long ago for the narrow-bandgap semiconductors deposited to/formed on the surface of wider-bandgap semiconductors, for example, for Ag2S produced by an ion exchange on the surface of AgBr (AgI).546 It is, therefore, logical that the conception of DSSCs was later extended to the SSSCs with the same liquid-junction architecture as in the DSSCs.4–9,11,14,16,19,21,22,25,37,38,40,547,548
The DSSC research was strongly stimulated by the fuel crisis of 1973 and over a thousand papers on DSSCs emerged in a few years from the start of the studies attesting to an explosive growth of the DSSC field.534,545,549,550 The solar light is absorbed in the DSSCs by a molecular sensitizer – a dye or a metal complex anchored to a wide-bandgap semiconductor, like TiO2 or ZnO with a highly developed surface area. The DSSC concept is a good example of a system, where the performance of the overall device is better than that of the separate components.16 Indeed, the mesoporous titania cannot absorb efficiently the solar light and also does not conduct electric current. The conventional Ru–bipyridyl complex sensitizers degrade very quickly when illuminated in solutions without any oxide support and redox-shuttles.
