However, a combination of all the components into a united system results in a solar cell that can generate the electric current densities of up to 20 mA cm−2 and exhibits stable performance for more than 15 years in the outdoor solar illumination.550 The toughest challenge for the DSSCs still to be met is to surpass a threshold of 15% efficiency.550 Indeed, quite spectacular efforts applied in the field of DSSC in two recent decades resulted in only ∼11% efficiency for the best performing cells. However, we have to mention here a very promising 28.9% efficiency under ambient illumination (but only 11.3% under the AM1.5G sunlight) achieved very recently by Grätzel et al. for a DSSC with two sensitizers.551 A limited success in the efficiency improvement observed in recent years stimulated the studies of other liquid-junction cell designs, in particular, the SSSCs with the semiconductor sensitizer introduced in the form of NCs.7–9,16,19,21,22,25,40,548
The working principle of the SSSCs is essentially based on a combination of the light-driven photocatalytic processes on a photoanode (photocathode) and the electro-catalytic processes on a counter electrode (Fig. 27b). The incoming light is absorbed by the NCs of a narrow-bandgap semiconductor, for example, CuInS2 (the photosensitizer NCs), resulting in the electron transfer to a wider-bandgap porous semiconductor metal oxide layer (TiO2 or ZnO). The VB hole of the sensitizer NCs is then filled at the expense of the oxidation of sulfide ions – one of the components of the redox couple present in the liquid electrolyte and having a high affinity to the surface of the sensitizer NCs. The elemental sulfur produced as a result of the S2− photooxidation is converted into polysulfide species and diffuses to the CuxS counter electrode where it is reduced by the electrons arriving from the photoanode and with this the photoelectrochemical cycle is finished. The SSSCs started with a modest few-percents efficiency of the light harvesting but showed an accelerated growth and achieved in 2018 efficiencies over 12%. At that, the potential of such cells is still to be realized to a full extent.
This section reviews consecutively the solid-state solar cells based on the MMC NCs (both thin-film and bulk-heterojunction types) and the liquid-junction solar cells with MMC NC-sensitized photoanodes/photocathodes.

3.1 Solid-state solar cells based on MC NCs. 
The simplest design of the multinary NC-based solid-state solar cells includes a MMC NC layer sandwiched between an ETL and an HTL. Typically, CIS or CISe layers are formed on semi-transparent Mo-coated glass as an ETL, and covered consecutively with a buffer CdS layer and a layer of intrinsically doped and aluminium doped ZnO NCs as an HTL. The structure is finished by a top metallic layer (Au, Ah, or Ni–Al). Such configurations showed light conversion efficiencies of ∼1% for CIS NCs,166 3.4% for CISSe NCs,552 0.74% for CISe NCs.359 A similar design was also realized with the absorber layer formed by mixed Cu(InxB1−x)Se2 NCs.375

Tables 3 and 4 summarize some of the reported solid-state solar cells based on various MMC NCs and placed in the order of increasing light conversion efficiency for each light absorber. The tables show the schematic cell composition and the most important photovoltaic parameters of the cells, in particular, the short-circuit photocurrent density (Jsc), the open-circuit photovoltage (Voc), the fill factor of the current–voltage characteristics (FF), and the power conversion efficiency (η).

The CIS/CISe NC absorber layer in the solid-state solar cells can be formed by the direct electrodeposition from molecular precursor solutions479 or by the electrophoretic deposition of the pre-formed NCs.358 The cells formed by the electrophoretic deposition of alloyed CIGS NCs showed the highest photocurrent conversion efficiency (PCE) of 5.57% at a 25% molar fraction of gallium.553
The development of an efficient bulk-heterojunction solid-state solar cell with MC NCs is a multiple task when variations of any possible parameters of the cell (geometry, composition, mixing of the components, thickness of layers, etc.) affect the cell performance to a lesser or larger extent. Among the major factors are (i) the “right” cell geometry allowing for the efficient charge extraction and transport to the opposite electrodes and (ii) the matching of energy levels between the donating and accepting components of the cells. This conception is exemplified by the bulk-heterojunction solar cells based on AIS463 and CAIS NCs445 mixed with P3HT, the mixture deposited onto the surface of ZnO NR array (Fig. 28a). The cell illumination can excite both CAIS NCs and P3HT and a “cascade” electron transfer occurs from P3HT to CAIS and further to ZnO, while the photogenerated holes move in the opposite direction. In this way, the efficient charge separation and directed transport are organized, while the P3HT HTL actively participates in the photogeneration of charge carriers (Fig. 28b). The ZnO NR array acts as a perfect electron carrier allowing for the directed electron flow to ITO and out into the electric circuit.445,463 The copper ions occupy the vacant states in the AIS lattice and increase the NC conductivity resulting in a decreased internal resistance of the bulk heterojunctions between CAIS NCs and P3HT polymer.231 The cells based on the CAIS NC/P3HT bulk-heterojunction reveal a higher PCE of 1.13%, than similar composites with individual CIS and AIS NCs (0.25% and 0.47%, respectively).231
 Fig. 28  (a and b) A scheme (a) and current–voltage characteristics (b) of the solid-state solar cells based on ZnO NR array and ZnO/CAIS/P3HT heterostructures. (c and d) A schematic layout (c) and energy diagram (d) of the cell based on CZTS NC/MEH-PPV (poly(2-methoxy-5-(2-ethylhexyloxy)-1,4-phenylenevinylene) bulk-heterojunction. Reprinted with permissions from ref. 445 (a and b) and ref. 91 (c and d). Copyright (2015) The Royal Society of Chemistry. 
The 1-DDT/OLA-capped CIS NCs produced by the hot injection can be easily mixed with solutions of conjugated polymers, for example, P3HT resulting in a NC/polymer bulk-heterojunction.554 The heterojunction quality and the efficiency of the charge transfers between the bulk-heterojunction components can be increased considerably by the post-deposition ligand exchange.554
The composites of RGO with CIS NCs were applied as an ETL and, simultaneously, as a co-absorber in the bulk-heterojunction with MEH-PPV.370 The RGO nanosheets increased the cell productivity as a result of the directed electron transfer along the RGO basal plane and a percolating effect of the nanosheets.370
The bulk heterojunctions of 3–5 nm CZTS NCs with P3HT also have favorable mutual CB and VB positions allowing for the efficient electron transfer from the photoexcited polymer to NCs and the hole transfer from the photoexcited NCs to the polymer (Fig. 28c and d). As a result, the systems can efficiently absorb the visible light at wavelengths shorter than 900 nm.91 The introduction of CZTS NCs into the bulk-heterojunction of P3HT with a fullerene derivative – PCBM increases the light absorption efficiency and the electrical conductivity of the bulk-heterojunction resulting in an improvement of the cell performance from 3.30% to 3.65%.404 Mixed kesterite CZTSSe NCs can be used instead of the conjugated polymers in a bulk-heterojunction with PCBM acting as a sole light absorber, but the cell efficiency is low (0.01%) in this configuration.270
The light conversion efficiency of 9.51% was reported for “inverted” solar cells based on the bulk-heterojunction of PTB7 conjugated polymer with PCBM and an additional buffer layer of 7 nm CIS NCs placed between a ZnO ETL and the light-harvesting bulk-heterojunction.555 The CIS layer plays multiple roles including additional light absorption, a reduction of the roughness of ZnO/bulk-heterojunction interface as well as an acceleration of the electron transport between the junction and ETL.555
The configuration and performance of the TiO2/CIS-composite based bulk-heterojunction solar cells depends considerably on the titania scaffold morphology.95,465 In the case of a dense TiO2 layer the Zn-doped CIS NCs form a separate layer on top of the titania scaffold (Fig. 29a and b) and thus the junction forms only on the interface between the titania edge and the closest layer of CIS/CIZS NCs, while other sensitizer NCs have no direct contact with the titania. Such cell demonstrated a meager PCE of 0.30%.95 In the case of mesoporous titania scaffolds, the CIZS NCs penetrate the entire volume of TiO2 film and thus each sensitizer NC potentially has a direct contact with the titania NCs (Fig. 29c and d). In such configuration the PCE of the cell increases to 1.16% as a result of the simultaneous increase in the Jsc (Fig. 29e), FF and a 10-fold rise of the EQE.95 The ideality factor (FF) of the cell was found to be much higher for the porous layer-based bulk-heterojunction as compared with the flat-bilayer indicating strongly reduced electron–hole recombination in the porous bulk-heterojunction.
 Fig. 29  Cross-sectional SEM (a), element distribution maps (b and c), current–voltage characteristics (d), and EQE spectra in the Urbach coordinates (e) for flat-bilayer and porous TiO2/CIS composites. Reprinted with permissions from ref. 95. Copyright (2016) The Royal Society of Chemistry. 
The possibility of charge transfer between CIS and ZnO NCs together with the light scattering capabilities of anisotropically-shaped zinc oxide nanoformations were elegantly used to enhance the performance of conventional silicon solar cells.431 The ZnO nanopyramids attached to the silicon surface decreased the light reflectance to 10–15% as compared to 25–40% of the bare Si, thus enhancing the cell performance from 4.5% to 6.6%. In the case of CIS NC-capped ZnO nanopyramids, the cell performance can further be impressively increased to 10% at the expense of the light-harvesting by CIS NCs and the cascade charge transfer from CIS NCs to ZnO to silicon.431
A comparison between compact and porous TiO2-based cells95 provides an excellent illustration of another special feature of the light-harvesting systems based on MMC NCs differing quite drastically from the “conventional” binary MC light harvesters, such as cadmium and lead chalcogenide NCs. As discussed in Section 1, the absorption band edge of CIS and AIS NCs is dominated by the absorbance on the subbandgap states – lattice defects, vacancies and other irregularities abundant in the multinary NCs. It was found that such subbandgap states can take active participation in the light conversion and thus the EQE spectra also show distinct absorption “tails” below the bandgap. These tails can be linearized in the Urbach equation coordinates “ln(EQE) − (hv − Eg)” (Fig. 29f). Quite surprisingly, the characteristic Urbach energy EU being proportional to the density and distribution width of the mid-bandgap states is almost twice lower for the solar cells based on the porous TiO2/CIS layers as compared with the compact bilayer analog.
This phenomenon was explained95 by a partial filling of the mid-bandgap states near VB of CIS NCs with electrons coming from titania. It is well known that the n-conducting TiO2 has an excess of electrons that can cause, for example, “dark” reductive processes even without the photoexcitation events.556 The partial filling of the mid-bandgap states in the porous TiO2/CIS heterostructures lowers an energy barrier for the electron extraction and can be the reason for the enhanced PCE in such devices as compared with the compact architecture.95
The photovoltaic devices produced via the spray-coating deposition of CISe NCs (the cell configuration is Au/CISe/CdS/ZnO/ITO) showed a large and size-dependent Voc reaching around 850 meV for the NCs with Eg = 1.46 eV, that is, 65–70% of the open-circuit voltage theoretically possible for such bandgap.237 It is claimed to be the highest Voc reported for the NC-based solar cells exceeding by far the values reported for CdSe and PbS NC-based solar cells. Contrary to the above-discussed TiO2/NC-based architectures, the large size-dependent Voc is considered to show that the midbandgap states of CISe NCs do not dominate the cell performance.237 It can be speculated from these contradictory results that the surface chemistry of MC NCs may play a crucial role in the charge transfer dynamics thus allowing for or excluding the possibility of the participation of the NC midbandgap states in the photocurrent generation.
The CIS NCs incorporated into the solid-state solar cells with ZnO NCs as a charge extraction layer (the cell configuration is ITO/PEDOT:PSS/CIS/ZnO/Al) reveal a “light soaking” effect when the cell efficiency increases under the illumination (Fig. 30a).557 The effect was found to be considerably enhanced with the UV light being added to the illumination spectrum indicating the involvement of ZnO NCs. Moreover, the effect is reversible indicating a dynamic character of the photoinduced changes. A similar but much more modest light-soaking enhancement effect was observed for the CZTSe-based solid-state solar cells where the PCE increased during the illumination from 6.7% to 7.2%.273 In contrast, for the above-discussed ZnO/CIS heterostructure-based solar cell the light-soaking-induced enhancement of the light conversion efficiency is tremendous as the PCE increases during the illumination from around 2 to 8% (Fig. 30b) indicating that the photoinduced changes affect mostly the wide-bandgap ZnO scaffold. A detailed analysis of possible losses in a CIS/ZnO heterojunction557 revealed two major loss sources: (i) a barrier for the charge extraction at the CIS/ZnO interface lowered by the light-soaking effect and (ii) the recombination of the charge carriers in CIS NCs via the trap states. The latter is unaffected by the incoming light557 and needs to be addressed in other ways, in particular by adjusting the NC synthesis conditions and NC surface passivation.
 Fig. 30  Current–voltage characteristics (a) and EQE spectra (b) of ZnO/CIS composite based solid-state solar cells subjected to the illumination with a mixture of “white” light (WL) and UV light. Inset in (b): PCE as a function of the “light-soaking” duration. Reprinted from ref. 557 – Published by the PCCP Owner Societies. 
A typical design of kesterite-based solar solid-state cells262,263,281,322,327,329,533 includes a semi-transparent molybdenium layer sputtered on the soda lime glass, on top of which a kesterite (CZTS, CZTSSe, CZTSe) micrometer-thick layer is formed by the deposition of various precursors followed by the sulfidation/selenidation at 400–500 °C. Then, a thin (30–50 nm) buffer CdS layer is typically deposited by the chemical bath deposition, followed by the deposition/sputtering of a layer of intrinsically doped ZnO NCs (IZO) and, optionally, an AZO layer. The final top layer of the solar cell is made of ITO NCs covered by the vapor-deposited/sputtered metal (Au, Ag, Ni–Al, etc.) layer.
The kesterite Cu2MSnS4 (M = Fe, Co, Ni) NCs deposited onto the ZnS-coated ZnO nanorod array (Fig. 31a) act as spectral sensitizers enabling the entire visible light harvesting in simple solid-state ITO/ZnO/ZnS/kesterite/Au solar cells with an efficiency of 2.71%, 2.73%, and 3.23% for the Ni-, Fe-, and Co-based NCs, respectively.318 The CB levels of the cell components are properly aligned to induce a cascaded photoelectron transfer from the kesterite NCs to the intermediate ZnS layer, further to the ZnO scaffold and, finally, to ITO (Fig. 31b).318
 Fig. 31  (a) SEM of CFTS NC-coated ZnO/ZnS NR array. (b) Energy diagram of a CFTS NC-based solar cell. (c) Cross-sectional SEMs of a glass/Mo/CZTS NC layer at different stages of the selenidation. The scale bar in (c) is 500 nm. Figures denote thicknesses of the layers. Reprinted with permissions from ref. 318 (a and b) and ref. 306 (c). Copyright (2016) The Royal Society of Chemistry (a and b); (2014) American Chemical Society (c). 
The kesterite CZTS NCs deposited onto the titania NR arrays from water/ethanol suspensions and coupled with a CdS buffer layer and a spiro-OMeTAD HTL exhibited a light conversion efficiency of 2%.319 The rate of electron transfers in the bulk-heterojunction of CZTS and inorganic oxide semiconductors expectedly depends on the NC ligand nature. By exchanging bulky OLA ligands with S2−, the PCE of completely inorganic ZnO/CdS/CZTS/MoO3/Au cell was increased from 1.83% to 3.34%.258
The bulky insulating ligands can be efficiently removed from the surface of CZTS NCs by reacting them with strong alkylating agents, such as methyliodide or the Meerwein's agent (C2H5)3OBF4. The treatment converts primary amines (OLA, dodecylamine, etc.) into the corresponding quaternary amines that have a much lower affinity to the NC surface while not affecting the crystalline structure of the CZTS NCs.193
Cu2NiSnS4 NCs285 and Cu2BaSnS4 NCs558 were reported as light-harvesting layers of the solid-state solar cells with a general configuration Mo/MMC/CdS/IZO/AZO.
A nanocrystalline CZTS layer with a thickness of around 400 nm deposited by the pulsed laser deposition was applied as a light-harvesting component of the solid-state cells with a PCE of 5.2%.514 A similar cell with CZTS NCs synthesized by the liquid-phase pulsed laser/electrophoretic deposition revealed a PCE of 4.77%.513
Solar cells produced from the NC inks. 
One of the most attractive and promising directions in the solid-state photovoltaics based on the MMC NCs is the ink-jet printing of the cells on various substrates (both rigid and flexible) by using pre-formed NC inks. The method allows for unprecedented flexibility in the variation of the ink composition and rheology, substrate nature, as well as the conditions of the post-printing annealing in a sulfur- or selenium-rich atmosphere required for the formation of compact and high-quality MC absorber layers.
The inks composed of amorphous CIS NC with copper and indium monoethanolamine complexes were used to form CISe-based solar cells with a PCE of 10.85%.122 The selenidation of CIS NC inks was recently established as a reproducible method of the production of thin-film solar cells.227
The solid-state solar cells with CIGS absorber layers were successfully formed by the ink-jet printing of CIGS NCs followed by rapid annealing in selenium vapors.559,560 The film uniformity and performance depend crucially on the ink formulation requiring a combination of a low-boiling-point (hexanethiol) solvent and a high-boiling-point (dodecanethiol) solvent in a specific proportion providing a trade-off between the viscosity and volatility of the CIGS NC inks.559 By using three different CIGS inks with a varied In/Ga ratio, a composition-graded absorber layer can be easily formed by the consecutive jet-printing procedures.561 The graded Mo/CIGS/CdS/IZO/n-ZnO/Ni/Al solar cell showed almost twice higher efficiency (PCE of 7.29%) as a non-graded analog.561
The doctor blade deposition of a layer of CIGS NC inks in alkanethiols followed by the selenidation was applied to produce CIGSSe absorber layers.240 The phase of the nanocrystalline inks can be varied from chalcopyrite to sphalerite. Also, NaCl additions were introduced into the NC inks resulting in a uniform distribution of Na+ ions in the final CIGS films. The presence of Na+ positively affects the CIGSSe crystallization producing larger and more densely packed absorber grains and increasing the cell performance from 7.7% up to 12.0%.240 In a similar way, thin CZTSSe films were produced by the doctor blade deposition of CZTS NC inks and annealing in the presence of selenium.256,324 The incorporated Na+ ions were assumed not only to affect the CZTS crystallization kinetics but also to passivate grain surface defects and to increase the charge carrier density thus resulting in ∼50% enhancement of the solar cell performance.260
A typical approach for the formation of light-harvesting kesterite layers of the solid-state solar cells is the annealing of CZTS NC inks in a sulfur263,266,279 or selenium273,306,322,323,325,332 atmosphere at 400–500 °C. The sulfidation/selenidation results in the formation of compact micron grains, the grain size d increasing with the annealing time and temperature.266,323 In the case of CZTSe-based cells the highest PCE of ∼5% was observed in intermediate ranges of both annealing duration (20 min, d = 0.2–0.5 μm) and temperature (500 °C, d = 0.5–1.0 μm).323
Uniform precursor CZTS NC films on Mo-coated glass (Fig. 31c, fragment 1) were produced by the layer-by-layer deposition with the annealing at 350 °C after each consecutive spin coating of the NC ink solution.306 As the selenidation starts, the compact CTZSe layer forms with the front between new CZTSe phase and the precursor CTZS NC phase moving from the outer NC film surface to the Mo layer (Fig. 31c, fragments 2 and 3). This movement resulted in the formation of a dense 550–600 nm-thick CZTSe layer over a residual nanocrystalline Cu- and Se-rich CZTS layer (Fig. 31c, fragment 4) that resists to further selenidation. In this way, the composition and morphology of the absorber layer can easily be controlled by varying the Se treatment duration.
Annealing of a CZTS wurtzite NC ink precursor in a mixed sulfur/SnS vapor atmosphere yielded an absorbing layer with 6.0% efficiency of light conversion.279
“Green” aqueous CZTS NC inks developed by Tang et al.329 can easily be deposited onto Mo-glass substrates and converted into the compact CZTSSe layers by annealing at 400 °C in the presence of a mixture of elemental S and Se. The composition of the final absorber layers depends on a molar S/Se ratio during the annealing. The highest PCE of 5.15% at Voc = 478 mV and a FF of 45% (Fig. 32a) was achieved for the CZTSSe layers with a bandgap of 1.22 eV, intermediate between the pure CZTS and CZTSe phases.329 The cell can harvest the visible and NIR light to ∼1020 nm (Fig. 32b). The colloidal CZTS NCs produced in water/ethanol mixtures were used as inks sprayed non-pyrolytically onto Mo-glass substrates and then annealed either in N2 or in the Se-saturated atmosphere producing compact CZTS and CZTSSe layers with a PCE of 5.0% and 8.6%, respectively.322
 Fig. 32  (a and b) Current–voltage characteristics (a) and EQE spectrum (b) of the CZTSe-based cell produced from the aqueous CZTS NC inks. Inset in (b): EQE spectrum presented in the Tauc coordinates to assess the bang gap of the light absorber layer. (c) Cross-sectional SEM and photographs of flexible CZTS-based cell constructed on the Al foil. (d) Atomic composition of In-diffused CZTS/In2S3 film as shown by the Auger spectroscopic analysis (the etching rate was ∼4 nm × min−1). Reprinted with permissions from ref. 329 (a and b), ref. 349 (c), and ref. 562 (d). Copyright (2014) American Chemical Society (a, b and d); (2012) The Royal Society of Chemistry (c). 
The ITO/TiO2/CdS/CZTSSe/Au cells produced from ethoxycellulose/CZTSe NC inks showed a higher uniformity of the absorber layer and light conversion efficiency than similar devices formed from CZTSe NC dispersion in ethanol.275 An ethanol-suspended CZTS NC ink was designed for the roll-to-roll printing of NC layers on the flexible Mo-coated Al foil.349 After consecutive procedure of the annealing with sulfur vapour, the chemical bath deposition of a CdS layer and the sputtering of IZO, ITO, and Ni–Al layers, a flexible solar cell with a PCE of 1.94% was produced (Fig. 32c).349 All of the cell components can be printed opening perspectives for the industrial production of such devices.
The uniformity and crystallinity of CZTS thin films (and, therefore, their light-harvesting capability) can be enhanced considerably by combining a NC ink with a molecular precursor ink, as discussed earlier for CIS NCs, the latter ink containing acetates/chlorides of copper, zinc and tin in methoxyethanol.506 The sulfidation of the films produced by the consecutive spin-coating of 10 layers of the NC ink with 5 layers of the molecular ink was found to produce the most active light-harvesting layers surpassing by the activity (4.92%) both similar films formed exclusively from the NC inks (2.10%) and solely from the molecular inks (2.80%).506
By combining two NC inks containing CZTS NCs and SnS/Ag2S NCs, the Ag-doped CZTSe films were formed by the rapid selenidation at 500 °C.563 The introduction of Ag+ dopant resulted in an accelerated growth of the kesterite grains, a higher charge carrier density and life time as well as suppressed sub-bandgap absorption indicating reduced formation of defects.563,564
The light conversion efficiency of CZTSSe absorber layers produced by the partial selenization of CZTS NC inks can be enhanced by post-synthesis etching treatments aimed at the elimination of impurity CuxS(Se) phases. The procedure is typically performed by using KCN etching agent but can be made “greener” and safer by applying a combination of ethylenediamine and 2-mercaptoethanol that selectively dissolves copper chalcogenide phases while etches only slightly the kesterite and wurtzite CZTS NCs.565
The inks composed of separate sulfide NCs (CuS, SnS, ZnS, Cu7S4, Cu2SnS3, etc.) were also applied as precursors for the light-harvesting kesterite layers produced by sulfidation/selenidation under annealing conditions.327 The selenidation of the NC mixtures yields compact alloyed CZTSSe layers with a bandgap of around 1.1 eV corresponding to the light sensitivity threshold of around 1100 nm.327
Annealing of a layer of spin-coated OLA-capped CZTS NC inks in a sulfur or selenium atmosphere yields CZTS/CZTSSe light-absorbing layers containing the residuals of the ligand pyrolysis, such as graphitic carbon and thiophene/selenophene moieties that enhance the electrical conductivity of the films.257
A combination of the doctor blading of a NC ink and the selenidation was applied to form multinary Cu–Zn–Sn–Ge–S–Se light-harvesting layers with the bandgap depending on the Sn/Ge ratio in the starting Cu2Zn(Sn1−xGex)S4 NCs.288 By preparing the NC inks with a Cu deficiency and an excess of Zn at Ge/(Ge + Sn) = 0.7, the composition-gradient (in terms of the Ge distribution) CZTGSSe films were produced showing a much higher PCE of 6.8% as compared to 0.51% for the stoichiometric absorber.288 The reported efficiencies for the kesterite solar cells produced from the NC inks vary from 1.66% to 6.0% for CZTS NCs,262,263,266,279,281,348,562 from 4.48% to 9.08% for the alloyed CZTSSe NCs,306,325,329,502 and reach 6–7% for CZTSe NC-based cells.273,533
Alternatively to the jet-printing, the inks can be sprayed upon the substrates followed by the sulfidation/selenidation. In this way, the CIS NC-359 and CZTS NC-based269,502 solid-state solar cells were fabricated. The quality of the CZTS coating was found to depend on the size of CZTS NCs, being optimal for the 15 nm NCs but deteriorating for larger NCs.269 Other techniques were also applied for the CZTS NC inks deposition, including radio frequency magnetron sputtering.348
The electrodeposition coupled to various post-deposition thermal treatments was found to be quite efficient, scalable and reproducible method for the formation of light-harvesting kesterite layers. For example, the electrodeposited CZTS films with a top In2S3 barrier layer subjected to the post-synthesis heat treatment showed a PCE of 6.9%.562 The treatment was found to induce the indium diffusion into the CZTS layer. The surface of the In-doped CZTS sample was etched for different times and the atomic composition was determined by the Auger photoelectron spectroscopy thus providing a “depth” profile of the In distribution (Fig. 32d). The indium incorporation results in a narrowing of the CZTS bandgap from 1.47 eV to 1.40 eV, thus enhancing the photosensitivity and PCE of the cells. The heat treatment of the CZTS film without the In2S3 layer does not affect the PCE, the fact clearly indicating the role of the In diffusion for the cell performance. The In atoms filling the voids between the CZTS grains allow for a more efficient charge transport increasing the charge density of the p-conductive CZTS layer.562
Two types of MMC NCs differing in the conductivity type – p-conducting CZTS NCs and n-conducting copper-diffused AIS NCs were coupled to form a p/n junction with the CB/VB levels allowing for the efficient separation of the photogenerated charge carriers.305 A depletion region forming at the p/n junction creates an electric field favoring to the drift of the minority carriers across the junction. The CZTS and CAIS layer thickness can be adjusted to match the exact length of the depletion region, which can be determined from the capacitance–voltage characteristics of the junction.305 In this way, the undepleted parts of the junction contributing to the internal resistance are eliminated.
Along with the principal light-harvesting function, the MMC NCs were found to be capable of charge transport and, therefore, can combine the charge photogeneration and charge transport functions, thus allowing to exclude additional organic hole transporting layers.
The ZnS-coated CIS NCs were applied to form an HTL on top of the hybrid CH3NH3PbI3 perovskite (Fig. 33a).226 The mutual positions of CB/VB layers of TiO2, perovskite and CIS NCs favor to the electron extraction into TiO2 and the hole extraction into the CIS layer (Fig. 33b) allowing to achieve a PCE of 8.38% without additional organic hole transporting materials.
 Fig. 33  (a and b) Schematic layout (a) and energy diagram (b) of the TiO2/MAPbI3/CIS/Au solar cell. (c) Cross-sectional SEMs of a FTO/CBTS/MaPbI3/PCBM solar cell. (d) Energy diagram of a P3HT/PCBM-based cell with a CZTS transport layer. Reprinted with permissions from ref. 226 (a and b), ref. 520 (c), and ref. 395 (d). Copyright (2014, 2015) American Chemical Society (a–c); (2017) The Royal Society of Chemistry (d). 
The p-conducting kesterite CZTS297 and CBTS NCs520 are promising HTL materials for the CH3NH3PbI3 (MAPbI3)-based solar cells. The CBTS NCs combine a high chemical stability with a high carrier mobility of around 10 cm2 V−1 s−1. For the comparison, the carrier mobilities of two typical HTLs – organic spiro-OMeTAD and inorganic CuSCN are 4 × 10−5 and 0.01–0.1 cm2 V−1 s−1, respectively.520 The insertion of a 100 nm-thick CBTS layer between FTO and the light-harvesting perovskite layer (Fig. 33c) allows to reach a PCE of ∼10%.
The hole transporting properties were reported for Cu2CdSnS4 NCs introduced as a HTL in a P3HT/PCBM bulk-heterojunction solar cell.317 This nanocrystalline HTL enhances the cell performance from 3.08% to 3.63%.
Kesterite CZTS nanospheres introduced as a buffer layer into the bulk-heterojunction solar cells with mixed P3HT/PCBM absorbing layers can act both as an additional light absorber and as an ETL that accepts the photogenerated charge carriers from P3HT/PCBM heterojunction and relays them to the metal electrode (Fig. 33d).395 Although the overall light conversion efficiency of the ITO/PEDOT:PSS/P3HT–PCBM/CZTS/LiF/Al cell is rather small (0.95%) it is almost 8 times higher than PCE of a similar cell produced without the kesterite NCs (0.12%).
One of the current trends in MC photovoltaics is to trade-off the cost of the cell components against the absorber efficiency. It is assumed that the utilization of an abundant, low-toxic and low-cost absorber materials, such as the above-discussed kesterite may compensate for a comparatively low light conversion efficiency. By this reason, new MMC compositions and phases are constantly probed as light-harvesting components of the solid-state solar cells. In particular, ternary CTS NCs are considered as a very affordable alternative to the conventional MC absorbers.249,316 The cells produced from CTS NC inks subjected to additional annealing in the sulfur vapor revealed a reasonable PCE of 2.77% and a high stability to degradation during a 50 day testing in the ambient air conditions.249 The composition-tunable CGSSe NCs were also introduced into the solid-state solar cells operating with a 0.2% efficiency.243 Alternatively, CGS NCs can be used as an HTL in perovskite-based bulk-heterojunction solar cells.566
A CuSbS2 NC-based thin-film solar cell prepared by the spin-coating of the NC ink precursor exhibited a PCE of 0.01%.307 Alternatively, CuSbS2 NCs can be grown on titania by the atomic layer deposition.524 Despite low efficiencies achieved to date, the copper antimony sulfide materials attract growing attention due to a bandgap of around 1.5 eV that matches perfectly the solar spectrum as well as high absorption coefficients (104–105 cm−1). Such properties, along with a broad abundancy of constituents and a low raw material and production costs,524–526 allow to anticipate a progress with this light absorber in the near future. Despite a considerable difference in the lattice type as compared to chalcopyrite and kesterite NCs, the chalcostibite CuSbS2 also reveals a tendency to the non-stoichiometry, in particular, showing a tunable VB hole density of 1016–1018 cm−3 depending on the Sb2S3 excess during the synthesis.525 Recently, a CuSbS2 NC-based solar cell with a PCE of 0.6% produced from a bilayer precursor Cu/Sb2S3 composite was reported.526 The cell composed of ZnO/CuSbS2 and Pt electrodes connected by the solid P3HT electrolyte exhibited an even higher PCE of 1.61%.475 Both mentioned results show a distinct and fast progress with this light harvesting material. Similarly, the preliminary studies of the nanocrystalline Cu4Bi4S9 (Eg = 1.14 eV)386 and Cu3BiS3 (Eg = 1.47 eV)567 attested to a high potential of these semiconductors for the solid-state photovoltaics.


3.2 Photoelectrochemical solar cells based on MMC NCs. 
The heterostructures of MMC NCs with the wide-bandgap semiconductors like TiO2 and ZnO are typically formed either by the ex situ and in situ methods. Table 5 summarizes the light conversion efficiencies of some of the reported liquid-junction NC-sensitized solar cells.

In the ex situ option, the mesoporous wide-bandgap oxide is decorated by separately prepared MMC NCs stabilized by the surface ligands capable of covalent of electrostatic binding to the oxide surface, most often TGA and MPA. Sodium or potassium salts of the acids are typically used for the phase transfer of hydrophobic MMC NCs from organic solvent into polar media (water, DMF, etc.) or used for the direct synthesis of MMC NCs in aqueous solutions, as discussed in Section 2. The sulfur atom of –SH group of the acid binds to a cationic site on the surface of NCs while the ionized carboxyl group can readily attach to the positively charged sites on the surface of titanium or zinc oxides. This bonding enables a strong interaction between the oxide surface and the sensitizer NCs while the electrostatic repulsion between adjacent negatively-charged NCs favors to a uniform coverage of the wide-bandgap semiconductor surface. As the TGA anion is prone to the oxidation by air oxygen to disulfides as well as to the hydrolysis, MPA is a more often used option. An alkyl chain longer than –(CH2)2– hinders strongly the electron transfer from the photoexcited MMC NCs to the oxide scaffold and so the choice of available mercaptocarboxylic “linker” molecules is typically confined either to a less-stable but shorter TGA or to a longer but more stable MPA. This method was applied to produce titania heterostructures with CIS NCs,133,570,571 CIZS NCs,572 ZAISe NCs,187etc. The TiO2/CIS NC photoanode produced from the MPA-capped CIS NCs and combined into a liquid-junction cell with a CuxS counter electrode (CE) and polysulfide electrolyte showed a certified PCE of 6.66% with a champion device reaching 7.04% efficiency.133
Alternatively, the titania scaffolds can be modified by bifunctional molecules, like TGA that adsorbs via the carboxylic groups and can serve as a binder for the sensitizer NCs when the TiO2 film is immersed into the NC colloid. This way was applied to prepare TiO2/CIS68,171,573 and TiO2/AIS heterostructures.141
The PCE of TiO2/CIS photoanodes prepared from the ex situ synthesized cysteine-capped CuInS2 NCs can be enhanced quite drastically by using thiol co-adsorbent on the stage of the soaking TiO2 films in the NC solution.499 By adding GSH or TGA, the PCE of resulting TiO2/CIS photoanode can be increased from 0.29% (no additives) to 2.37% for GSH and to 4.20% for TGA. A PL quenching study showed that the rate constant of the electron transfer from CIS NPs to TiO2 is the highest one for the cysteine linker, 9.5 × 1010 s−1, decreasing to 7.1 × 1010 s−1 for a more bulky GSH.499 In a similar AIS NC-based system the PCE increases from 0.19% to 2.72% in the presence of TGA indicating a general character of the additive influence.499 The effect was attributed to the inhibition of the cysteine oxidation to disulfides on the NC surface resulting in an increased NC loading and enhanced electron transfer from NCs to TiO2 (accounting for a Jsc increase). Simultaneously, a shift of the titania band edges causes an increase in Voc.499
A photoanode can be prepared by soaking of a scaffold with a concentrated NC solution followed by the removal of excessive NCs on the stage of washing. The colloid concentration should be carefully adjusted to avoid a too high loading of the sensitizer NCs that can block the mesopores of TiO2 (ZnO) and hinder the electron transport from outer layers of the photoanode toward the OTE. In this way, the titania electrodes were decorated with the MPA-capped CIS NCs,568 CISe and CIZSe NCs.96,296 The incorporation of Zn ions into the lattice of CISe induces an upward shift of the CB level (Fig. 34a) favoring to a more efficient transfer of the photogenerated CB electron to the titania scaffold. As a result, the electron transfer rate constant increases from 2.4 × 1010 s−1 for CISe NCs to 9.1 × 1010 s−1 for CIZSe NCs, while the PCE of corresponding cells grows from 9.54% (CISe) to 11.66% (CIZSe). An even more drastic enhancement of the photoinduced electron transfer rate induced by Zn2+ incorporation was recently reported for TiO2/CIS nanostructures – from ∼0.3 × 1010 s−1 for pristine CIS NCs to ∼3 × 1010 s−1 for Zn-doped CIS NCs.572
 Fig. 34  (a) Energy diagram of TiO2/CISe and TiO2/CIZSe nanocomposites. (b) Absorption spectra of TiO2/(ZnSe)1−x(AISe)x photoanodes with a different x (given on the figure). (c) Absolute positions of CB and VB levels of the composition-selected ZAISe NCs relatively to the CB level of TiO2. Reprinted with permissions from ref. 96 (a) and ref. 94 (b and c). Copyright (2014, 2016) American Chemical Society. 
The partial substitution of In with Ga in CISe results in a considerable shift of the NC CB level – from −3.88 eV to −3.63 eV versus vacuum level for the 50 mol% gallium fraction.98 The shift results in an increase of the driving force of the photoinduced electron transfer from CIGSe NCs to the TiO2 scaffold. The liquid-junction solar cells with the TiO2/CIGSe photoanode, and CuxS or Ti/carbon CEs showed unprecedentedly high PCEs of up to 11.49% under the AM1.5G illumination versus 9.46% for the undoped CISe NCs.98
The sensitizer NCs can be deposited onto the surface of oxide scaffolds regardless of the NC ligand shell nature and surface charge by the electrophoretic deposition, when a relatively strong electric field (typically higher than 150–200 V × cm−1) is applied between the oxide electrode and a CE forcing the NCs to adhere to the oxide surface. For example, the method was successfully applied to form TiO2/CIS photoanodes.97,139,568
Probably the most straightforward of the ex situ approaches is the direct synthesis of MMC NC in aqueous solutions resulting in TGA-, MPA-, or GSH-capped NCs that can be adsorbed or dropcasted onto the surface of metal oxide scaffolds. In this way TiO2/CIS photoanodes were prepared by using aqueous TGA-capped CIS NCs.452,453 The TGA-stabilized 3–5 nm CIS/ZnS NCs synthesized directly in aqueous solutions55 penetrate uniformly the volume of mesoporous TiO2 films revealing a homogeneous composition of the resulting TiO2/CIS both along the cross section of the films (Fig. 35a and b) and over the outer film surface (Fig. 35c). Both stability and photoactivity of CIS NCs increase upon the deposition of a thin ZnS shell on the CIS NC surface. The TiO2/CIS/ZnS composites act as visible-light-sensitive photoanodes in the SSSCs with polysulfide electrolyte and copper sulfide-based CEs with a total conversion efficiency of around 8%.55
 Fig. 35  Cross-sectional SEM images of FTO/TiO2/CIS/ZnS photoanode (a and b); elements distribution across the photoanode (b) and on the outer surface (c). Adapted and reprinted from ref. 55 – Published by The Royal Society of Chemistry. 
The 5 nm ZAIS NCs were tested as a spectral sensitizer for the ZnO scaffolds manifesting a PCE of 0.72% in the cells with a Pt CE and iodide/triiodide redox-couple.159 The liquid-junction cells with the I−/I3− shuttle were also built on ZnO NR photoanodes sensitized with AIS and ZAIS NCs.161 The TiO2/AIS photoanodes produced by soaking titania with the ligand-free AIS exhibited a PCE of 0.8% in a cell with the polysulfide electrolyte and a gold CE.58
The solid-solution ZAISe NCs immobilized on porous titania scaffolds modified with MPA acted as a spectral sensitizer extending the light sensitivity range of the photoelectrode into the visible range to 900 nm.94 The CB and VB energies of the sensitizer NCs grow continuously as the molar fraction of ZnS is increased (and the x value decreased), thus providing higher driving forces for both the photoinduced electron transfer to titania and the hole transfer to the electrolyte species (Fig. 34c). At the same time, the light absorption range of the sensitizer NCs is reduced for smaller x and thus an optimal ZAISe composition exists, corresponding to ZnSe:AISe = 1:1, at which the light harvesting efficiency η reaches the highest value of 1.9%.
A balance between the CB/VB levels and the light-harvesting range always exists in series of solar cells based on differently-sized MMC NCs. As the NC size d is decreased in a critical range roughly corresponding to d < 2aB, the CB and VB energies of MMC NCs increase as a result of the quantum confinement and, therefore, the driving force of the interfacial electron/hole transfer to other cell components increases as well. However, a size-dependent broadening of the bandgap reduces the visible-light-harvesting capacity of the NCs similar as it happens in the above-discussed case of the solid-solutions of the MMC semiconductors.
As a result, there exists an optimal NC size at which the photoanodes exhibit the highest performance. For example, the largest photoresponse of TiO2/CISSe photoanodes was observed in the case of 4.2 nm sensitizer NCs, being smaller for 3.9 nm and 4.9 nm NCs (Fig. 36a).170
 Fig. 36  (a) Current–voltage characteristics of TiO2/CISSe photoanodes with the sensitizer NC of a different size. (b and c) Size-dependence of the EQE spectra (b), PCE (c, red bars) and PL quantum yield (c, blue bars) for TiO2/CIS photoanodes with size-selected CIS NCs. (d) Photocurrent density (curves 1 and 2) obtained for TiO2/CIS (1) and TiO2/CIS/ZnS (2) photoanodes and PL intensity of colloidal CIS/ZnS NCs (curve 3) as a function of the CIS NC composition Cu:In:S = xCu:5:10. Reprinted with permissions from ref. 170 (a), ref. 132 (b and c), and ref. 55 (d). Copyright (2014) American Chemical Society (a–c); (2017) The Royal Society of Chemistry (d). 
For a series of 2.9–5.3 nm CIS NCs electrophoretically deposited onto the porous TiO2 photoanodes the highest PCE and the broadest light harvesting range (Fig. 36b and c, blue bars) were observed for the intermediate-size 4.3 nm NCs.132 The cell studies were supported by laser flash photolysis and PL investigations that showed such CIS NCs accumulating a higher charge under illumination and revealing a larger rate of the radiative recombination than the smaller and larger NC mates. These results show that the above-discussed intuitively simple balance between the light-harvesting capability and the size-dependent CB/VB levels of the sensitizer NCs is accompanied by a balance of the size-dependent electron–hole recombination. This balance along with the charge accumulation properties of CIS NCs can be decisive for the defect-rich and non-stoichiometric CIS NCs.132
A size-dependence of η for the TiO2/CIS photoanodes follows the size variation of PL emission intensity of original colloidal CIS NCs (Fig. 36c, red bars)132 because both the light emission and the photocurrent generation compete with the non-radiative electron–hole recombination. It should be noted that a distinct correlation between compositional dependences of the PL intensity and photocurrent generation efficiency was observed for the non-stoichiometric TGA-stabilized CIS and CIS/ZnS NCs (Fig. 36d).55 A good agreement between the efficiencies of PL and light conversion for a variety of sizes and compositions of CIS NCs indicates that the PL spectroscopy is a reliable diagnostic tool for assessing of the light-harvesting capability of the ternary MC NC-based photoanodes.
The modification of TiO2/CdS photoanodes with an atomically-resolved tetrahedral [Cu5In30S56H4]13− cluster with a triethanol ammonium counter cation exhibiting the light sensitivity down to 700 nm results in a drastic increase of the photocurrent density showing a great potential of such molecular-like sensitizers,373 especially if they will be combined with conventional multinary NCs by the electrostatic interactions.
The harvesting efficiency of titania NT array-based photoanodes loaded with complementary light harvesters – CdS and CIS NCs, can be increased considerably by using a double-sided array with the NTs formed on both sides of the ITO plates.466
The kesterite CZTS NCs were applied as a co-sensitizer together with N719 complex dye-sensitizer to enhance the visible-light response of mesoporous titania electrodes.574 Both sensitizers can participate in the photoinduced electron transfer into the titania CB, as the electron can come via CZTS NCs in the case of the photoexcitation of the dye adsorbed on the kesterite NC surface. The cell exhibited a light conversion efficiency of 8.10% outperforming similar devices containing only the N719 complex.574
CZTS NCs were attached to the titania electrodes by using thiolated RGO as a bridge instead of conventional MPA/TGA.196 The most efficient liquid-junction cell with the TiO2/RGO/CZTS photoanode, I−/I3− shuttle and a Pt CE exhibited a PCE of 0.133%, which is around 30% higher than for a similar photoanode with the MPA linker.196
The arrays of ZnS- and ZnSe-decorated zinc oxide NRs were sensitized by CZTS NCs and introduced as photoanodes in the liquid-junction cells with a CuxS CE and polysulfide electrolyte exhibiting a PCE of 0.85% and 2.2% for the ZnO/ZnS and ZnO/ZnSe scaffolds, respectively.197
Copper bismuth sulfide NCs were applied as a spectral sensitizer for the titania photoelectrodes.312 When combined with the aqueous polysulfide electrolyte and a CuxS CE, the TiO2/CBS photoanode showed a PCE of 0.62%.
Cu3SnS4 NCs deposited from the gas-phase on the surface of mesoporous titania revealed a bandgap of 1.2 eV and photocurrent generation capacity under the visible-light illumination.518
The in situ methods of the sensitizer NCs deposition are used to a much lesser extent for MMC NCs as compared to the solar cells based on binary cadmium and lead chalcogenides. In particular, TiO2/AIS NC composites prepared in situ via the In3+ incorporation into the TiO2/Ag2S heterostructure were applied as photoanodes in a combination with a Pt CE and polysulfide electrolyte revealing a top PCE of 0.70%.575 A similar two-stage approach was applied to form TiO2/AgBiS2 nanocomposite. The feasibility of using this material as a light-harvesting component of liquid-junction solar cells with polysulfide electrolyte and Au or Pt CE was shown.473,474 The best AgBiS2-based cells showed a PCE of 0.34% at AM1.5G illumination473 and a spectral sensitivity range extending down to 800 nm.474 A NaSbSe2 NC-sensitized TiO2 photoanode with a bandgap of ∼1.5 eV produced by the SILAR manifested a PCE of 2.22% with an Au CE and aqueous polysulfide electrolyte.576
The ZnIn2S4 nanosheets deposited in situ onto the TiO2 surface by the HTT extend the light sensitivity range of the titania photoanode to 600 nm and exhibit a PCE of 0.42% in a liquid-junction solar cell with polysulfide electrolyte and Au CE.425
A combined ex situ/in situ approach to TiO2/CIS photoanodes was developed295 including the adsorption of primary small CIS NCs on the titania surface followed by the SILAR deposition of additional CIS layers to increase the sensitizer loading. The primary nuclei provide the nucleation sites forcing the growth of pre-adsorbed CIS NCs and preventing the formation of new nuclei. For the same CIS content, the ex situ/in situ photoanode manifested an increased PCE (1.84%) as compared with two analogs produced exclusively by the deposition of the ex situ sensitized CIS NCs (0.64%) and solely by the in situ SILAR deposition (1.27%).295
Depending on the composition and synthesis conditions the MMC NCs can inherit both n- and p-type electrical conductivity. The n-conductive NCs are typically used as a photoanode component that fills the photogenerated VB hole at the expense of the oxidation of S2− species in the electrolyte. In its own turn, the p-conducting NCs can be applied as sensitizers of photocathodes, where the NC photoexcitation results in the transfer of a photogenerated VB hole into the electric circuit, while the CB electron is transferred to the sulfur species in the electrolyte.131 Correspondingly, the wider-bandgap metal oxide scaffold should also have the p-type electrical conductivity, while the counter electrode should be catalytically active towards the oxidation of S2− species to complete the photoelectrochemical cycle in the cell. Such “inverted” solar cells can be combined with regular solar cells with the NC-sensitized photoanodes to obtain cumulatively increased Voc values and in this way, the Shockley–Queisser limit of the single cell performance can theoretically be overmounted.
In recent years, an extensive search for transparent and stable p-type metal oxide scaffolds for the photocathodes is performed with nickel oxide being currently the most active and promising material. The CE materials used in such “inverted” liquid-junction solar cells are similar to those applied in the usual cells and include Pt, CuxS, carbon allotropes, etc. Indeed, typically the CEs reveal simultaneously the catalytic activity for both sulfide oxidation and sulfur reduction, as, for example, shown by catalytic experiments for copper sulfide NCs.577 However, contrary to the binary MC NC probed quite extensively as the photocathode sensitizers, in the case of MMC NCs only scarce reports can be found. In particular, a stable and efficient photocathode for the liquid-junction solar cell can be constructed with a mesoporous NiO layer sensitized by CIS or CISe NCs and combined with the aqueous polysulfide electrolyte and a CuxS CE.131,578 The rates of hole injection from the sensitizer NCs to the NiO scaffold are comparable (on the order of 108 s−1) with the electron injection rates in conventional NC-based photoanodes and the maximal PCE is 1.25%.131
The cascade design of the light-harvesting photoelectrodes is another broadly used concept for the rational design of the binary MC NC-based photoelectrodes, which is still to be realized to the full extent for the MMC NCs. In most cases, the light-harvesting efficiency of the liquid-junction cell photoanodes is limited by the photoinduced charge separation between the wide-bandgap metal oxide scaffold and narrow-bandgap MC sensitizer NCs. To achieve the efficient charge separation, both components are typically chosen so that the CB level of the sensitizer NCs is more negative and the VB level – less positive than ECB and EVB of the metal oxide, respectively. Such conditions favor to the transfers of the photogenerated electrons from MC NC to titania and the VB holes – from titania to the sensitizer NCs. Therefore, the final charge-separated state of the composite does not depend on which component absorbs the incoming light. This principle can be extended for multi-sensitizer systems, where several light-harvesting components are used with the CB energies descending (by the absolute value) from the outer components of the photoanode to the inner components and so the photogenerated electrons flow from the photoanode/electrolyte interface to TiO2, further to OTE and, finally to the electric circuit. Such “cascade” design can be realized in several ways, in particular, (i) by using different semiconductor photoelectrode materials with appropriate CB and VB level offsets; (ii) by using alloyed solid solution compounds with a varied or even spatially gradient structure to create a CB (VB) offset from the outer surface of the photoelectrode toward OTE, and (iii) by combining NPs of the same semiconductor but of different size that reveal a strong size-dependence of CB and VB levels and placing them in the order of decreasing of the absolute CB potential from the outer photoelectrode surface toward OTE.
Only the first of these approaches seems to be probed for the MMC NCs. For example, the deposition of a CdSe layer on top of TiO2/In2Se3/CIS photoanodes was reported to result in an increase of the light conversion efficiency from 1.35% to 3.15% as a result of the formation of a cascade CdSe → CuInS2 → In2Se3 → TiO2 → OTE structure.579 This cascade can be extended by the deposition of an outer ZnSe layer with the highest CB level that serves simultaneously as a protector against the charge leakage into the electrolyte and as an additional light absorber, enhancing the PCE of the corresponding cell up to 4.55%.579 The protection of TiO2/CIS photoanodes with a CdS layer not only passivates the surface of CIS NCs but also creates conditions for the cascaded electron phototransfer from CdS to CIS and further to TiO2.97
A CIS NC layer placed between the titania NT array and a layer of CdS NCs on the NT surface acts simultaneously as a co-sensitizer supplying the photogenerated charge carriers to the titania layer, transferring the photogenerated electrons from the adjacent CdS NC layer to the TiO2 nanotube array and serves as a barrier for the electron recombination with the electrolyte species (Fig. 37a).467,570 The co-sensitization was found to increase the PCE up to 4.2% as compared to 1.8% for the binary TiO2/CdS heterostructure (Fig. 37b).540
 Fig. 37  (a) Energy diagram of a TiO2/CIS/CdS photoanode. (b) Current–voltage characteristics of the liquid-junction solar cells with TiO2/CdS, TiO2/CIS and combined TiO2/CIS/CdS photoanodes. (c) Current–voltage characteristics of a TiO2/CISSe photoanode-based liquid-junction solar cell. (d) Light conversion efficiency η and open-circuit voltage Voc in the cells based on TiO2/CIS photoanodes with different MC buffer layers. Reprinted/adapted with permissions from ref. 467 (a), ref. 570 (b), ref. 238 (c), and ref. 579 (d). Copyright (2011, 2012) The Royal Society of Chemistry (a and b); (2013) American Chemical Society (c and d). 
It should be noted that despite the above-discussed broad variability of the stoichiometry and composition of MMC NCs, the photoelectrodes of the liquid-junction solar cells with the cascaded charge transfers between the alloyed NCs of a different composition were still not reported. Also, a great potential of the size-dependent variability of the CB/VB levels of MMC NCs in the regime of quantum confinement was also not explored to the true extent for the formation of cascaded sensitizer layers.
The photocurrent generation efficiency of both solid-state and liquid-junction NC-based solar cells is limited to a far extent by the electron–hole recombination in the sensitizer MC NCs. In the case of the liquid-junction photoelectrochemical solar cells, the additional possibilities of the charge losses exist due to the recombination on the interfaces between the sensitizer NCs and the wide-bandgap scaffold as well as between the photoanode as a whole and the electrolyte species. To suppress the recombination and minimize losses of the photogenerated charges various approaches are developed. One of the most simple and, at the same time, efficient is the formation of additional “protective” (or “buffer” or “barrier”) semiconductor layers either on the sensitizer NC surface, between the sensitizer and the oxide scaffold, or between the metal oxide and OTE.19
The recombination in the sensitizer NCs occurs predominantly via the structural defects introducing additional states in the NC bandgap and allowing for the thermal or radiative dissipation of the light excitation energy. These defects (unsaturated bonds, cation/anion vacancies, etc.) can be passivated by appropriate ligands or a shell of other metal chalcogenide semiconductor, typically with a larger bandgap, such as ZnS. The passivating layers can be very conveniently deposited by the SILAR procedure allowing for a quite precise control over the thickness of the barrier layer. For example, TiO2/CIS photoanodes are routinely covered with a ZnS55,295 or CdS97,453 shell to enhance the chemical/photochemical stability of the sensitizer NCs and to suppress the non-radiative recombination by passivating NC surface defects.295
The modification of TiO2/CIS photoanodes with a CdS layer increases the PCE of a cell with the aqueous polysulfide electrolyte and CuxS CE from 1.14% to 3.91%.97 The SILAR deposition of a CdS layer on ZAISe NCs immobilized on the TiO2 scaffold resulted in a more than an order of magnitude increase in the light conversion efficiency.94 The passivation of CISe NCs by a layer of Mn-doped CdS was shown to enhance the light conversion efficiency from 0.8% up to 3.96%.168
The successive adsorption of In3+ and S2− on the surface of TiO2/CIS photoanodes reduces the number of surface vacancies in the sensitizer NCs resulting in a cathodic shift of the Fermi level of the photoelectrode.452
The SILAR deposition of a ZnS layer was found to affect considerably the light conversion efficiency of TiO2/CISe NC photoanodes resulting in a PCE of 8.10%.171 The impedance studies revealed that the ZnS coating does not alter the energy characteristics of the photoanode but enhances the kinetic characteristics of the charge carrier transfers, resulting in the strong reduction in the efficiency of both non-radiative recombination in the CISe NCs and the recombination on the photoanode/electrolyte interface.171 At the same time, the ZnS shell does not impede the interfacial electron transfer from CISe NCs to titania as confirmed by the cell performance and the efficient PL quenching after the CISe/ZnS NCs deposition onto the titania scaffold.
A thin CdSxSe1−x layer forming as a result of the copper-to-cadmium ions exchange on the surface of CISSe NCs enhances the PCE of the passivated TiO2/CISSe photoanode up to 3.45% (Fig. 37c).238 It should be noted that this photoanode revealed a fill factor of more than 60%238 indicating a high efficiency of the passivating procedure. Similarly high FFs, 58% and 62%, observed for a Zn-doped CIS NC-based photoanode133 and for a Ga-doped CISe NC-based photoanode,98 respectively, are rather an exception, than a steady tendency. In most cases and oppositely to the DSSCs, the NC-sensitized solar cells reveal modest fill factors amounting to around 40%, much rarer – to 50%. The reasons for this moderate performance are considerable recombinative losses in the sensitizer NCs and on the interfaces between the MC NCs and the metal oxide scaffolds, as well as between the photoanode and electrolyte.
Provided the CB levels of the sensitizer NCs and TiO2 (ZnO) are favorable for the electron transfer from NCs to oxide, the photoexcitation of the sensitizer NCs results in extremely fast electron transfer to neighboring TiO2 (ZnO) layer, leaving a hole in the NC valence band. Potentially, the electron injected in the titania CB can recombine with the hole in the sensitizer VB, similar as it occurs in the DSSCs after the electron injection from the photoexcited dye-sensitizer to the titania scaffold. To prevent such charge losses, a blocking layer is often introduced between the sensitizer NCs and TiO2 (ZnO) constituting a potential barrier for the injected electron on its way back to MC NCs. For example, a screening study of interfacial buffer layers for TiO2/CIS heterostructure among various MC materials (Fig. 37d, orange bars) showed In2Se3 to be the most efficient buffer layer enhancing the photocurrent generation by about 120% and increasing the PCE from 0.58% for binary TiO2/CIS photoanode to 1.35% for TiO2/In2Se3/CIS heterostructure.579 An increase in the open-circuit voltage for the cells having an intermediary MC layer (Fig. 37d, green bars) indicates quite clearly that the PCE enhancement effect comes not from (or not only from) the additional light absorption by the buffer layer, but from the inhibition of the recombination at the scaffold/sensitizer interface.
After the injection from the photoexcited sensitizer NCs to the adjacent mesoporous TiO2 (ZnO) layer, the electron starts to migrate through the network of contacting metal oxide NCs till it reaches OTE and comes into the electric circuit. An efficient design of the photoanode of SSSCs requires a high contact area between the photoanode surface and the electrolyte, however, the coverage of the TiO2 surface with the sensitizer NCs is typically not complete. As a result, a possibility exists for the electron to be captured by the components of the electrolyte, for example, by water, S0 or H3O+ ions that can reduce the photoanode performance considerably. To avoid such losses, the TiO2/NCs heterostructures can be covered with an additional protective layer of a wide-bandgap material, most often, zinc sulfide that creates a barrier for the electron to reach the electrolyte. The protective ZnS layer also serves to provide a stronger contact between sensitizer NCs and TiO2 (ZnO) as well as to protect the light-harvesting MC NCs from corrosive processes during the photoelectrochemical events in the SSSC. For example, a leakage of the photogenerated charge carrier was efficiently suppressed by the deposition of ZnS layers onto TiO2/CIS452,570 and TiO2/ZAISe187 photoanodes. In the case of ZAIS NCs, the photoanode was additionally passivated with amorphous TiO2 and SiO2 layers. This final treatment resulted in a PCE increase from 3.41% to 3.57%.187
The insulation of a TiO2/CIS/CdSe photoanode with an outer ZnS or ZnSe layer results in a PCE increase from 3.15% to 3.27% or to 4.55%, respectively.579 A higher efficiency of the ZnSe-coated photoanode is explained by the contribution of ZnSe into the total light absorption.


3.3 Multinary NCs in counter electrodes of solar cells. 
MMC NCs emerged recently as relatively active electrocatalytic materials for the counter electrodes of both DSSCs and SSSCs.25,547,548 For example, CIS NCs supported by a three-dimensional carbon fiber network produced by the egg shell carbonization revealed an activity as a DSSC counter electrode outperforming the bare carbon fiber network and producing photocurrents comparable to those in a similar DSSC with a Pt CE.315,580 Modification of the carbon fibers with RGO resulted in a better dispersion of CIS NCs over the fiber surface and further increased the catalytic activity of the CE.549 Reasonably efficient DSSC CEs were also prepared from CIS NCs on FTO,108,298 CuIn(Al)S2 NCs,581 CIS nanoflakes on Mo substrates,354 CIS nano-sheets grown on CuS,469 and composites of CIS NCs with PEDOT:PSS.225 The exchange of 1-DDT ligands with S2− opened the surface of CIS NCs to the electrolyte species resulting in a drastic increase of the catalytic activity of CIS NC/FTO films toward the triiodide ions reduction.298
Carbon/CIS NC composites were found to be more efficient CEs for the liquid-junction solar cells with TiO2/CdS/CdSe photoanodes than both carbon (a mixture of carbon black and activated carbon) and CIS NCs360 taken separately.
The catalytic activity of a CE can be quite straightforwardly assessed from the slope of the “dark” current–voltage characteristics in a three-electrode scheme with the same electrolyte/redox-couple as used in the solar cell. At that, the steeper is the J–V curve the higher is the catalytic activity of a CE material (Fig. 38a). Another informative method of the assessment of the CE activity is the impedance spectroscopy. A more active CE material is characterized by a lower charge transfer resistance (Rct) and a smaller radius of the semi-circular Nyquist plot. The carbon/CIS example shows that the maximal activity as a CE can be expected with a composite having CIS:carbon weight ratio of 1:1 (Fig. 38b), in accordance with the results of the solar cell trials.360
 Fig. 38  (a) Dark current–voltage characteristics of CIS NCs, carbon and carbon/CIS composites. (b) Nyquist plots for CIS NCs and carbon/CIS with a different component ratio. (c) Current–voltage characteristics of DSSCs based on Pt, CZTS, and CZTSe CEs. (d) “Dark” current–voltage characteristics of FTO/CZTSSe films immersed into the aqueous polysulfide solution. Reprinted with permissions from ref. 360 (a and b), ref. 492 (c), and ref. 271 (d). Copyright (2013) American Chemical Society (a, b and d); (2017) Elsevier (c). 
Tetragonal Cu2SnS3 NCs revealed “universal” catalytic activities toward the redox-couples traditionally used both in the dye-sensitized and NC-sensitized solar cells – I−/I3−, Co(phen)33+/Co(phen)32+,296 and S2−/Sx2−.383 Remarkably, the CTS NCs outperformed both Pt CE in DSSCs (10.26% for CTS versus 9.31% for Pt)314 and CuxS microspheres in NC-sensitized solar cells (4.05% for CTS versus 3.65% for CuxS).383
The kesterite CZTS and CZTSe NCs were proposed as alternatives for the conventional Pt CEs in the DSSCs with I−/I2 redox-couple.492 The kesterite-based CEs slightly outperform the platinum electrode (Fig. 38c) and exhibit a long operational life-time. A CZTS nanofiber-based DSSC CE revealed almost doubled activity as compared to FTO/Pt CE (PCE of 3.9% versus 1.72% for platinum).582 Similarly, the Cu2MSnS4 NCs (M = Fe, Cd,328 and Co274) showed a catalytic effect toward the triiodide anions reduction, similar to that of Pt CEs. A high catalytic activity in the DSSC CE was also found for CZTS(Se) NCs on FTO,274,277,333,392,406,410 CZTS nanoplate arrays,407 CZTS nanosheets,583 CZTS NCs anchored on the multi-wall carbon NTs,584 wurtzite/stannite Cu2CdGeS4 NCs,289 Ag8GeS6 NCs,179 as well as for CZTS/RGO585 and Pt–Co/Cu2ZnGeS4.280
The alloyed kesterite Cu2ZnSnSxSe1−x NCs exhibited a catalytic activity toward the S2−/S0 redox couple transformations when coupled to a NC-sensitized TiO2 photoanode.75,271 The catalytic current of the CE immersed into the polysulfide solutions (Fig. 38d) and the overall solar cell performance depend on the S/Se ratio in the kesterite NCs, being maximal for x = 0.5. The enhanced catalytic activity of CZTSSe NCs was explained by their increased porosity as compared to other compositions.271 The catalytic activities as CEs in the polysulfide-based NC-sensitized solar cells were also reported for the hierarchical CZTS microspheres.389
It should be noted that in most cases the catalytic activity of kesterite and related nanomaterials is compared with bare FTO and Pt, which is not an optimal electrode for the polysulfide electrolyte. At the same time, comparisons with the most frequently used and one of the most active CE materials for the polysulfide electrolytes – copper sulfide are typically not reported, thus making impossible the evaluation of the efficiency and perspectives of such materials for the applications in the NC-sensitized solar cell with the polysulfide electrolytes.

4 Photocatalytic and photoelectrocatalytic processes with MMC NCs
A photocatalytic process starts when a semiconductor NC absorbs a light quantum with the energy hv equal to or (typically) higher than the bandgap energy Eg (process 1 in Fig. 39a). The photogenerated charge carriers rapidly (in tens-hundreds fs) dissipate the energy excess hv − Egvia the electron–phonon interactions acquiring the chemical potentials equal to ECB and EVB levels.
 Fig. 39  A scheme of the working principle of (a and b) photocatalytic systems based on a MC NC/metal NC composite (a) and a binary NC heterostructure (b), and (c) photoelectrochemical system with a MC NC sensitized photoanode. 
A major part of the CB electrons and VB holes recombines either via the radiative pathway 2 emitting PL or in nonradiative processes 3 providing a vibrational energy to the NC lattice. Some portion of the charge carriers migrates and reaches the NC surface prior to the recombination (processes 4) where the carriers get “trapped” by various lattice defects (vacancies, adatoms, undercoordinated atoms, etc.) as well as by the adsorbed species. The photogenerated VB hole typically has a high oxidation potential and gets filled with an electron from various donor species present in the system, for example from water molecules or from a sacrificial electron donor introduced for this purpose such as sulfide ions (Fig. 39a). The photogenerated CB electrons can transfer directly to the electron accepting species through the NC/electrolyte (NC/gas) interface or accumulated on a co-catalyst coupled to the semiconductor NCs (Pt NCs in Fig. 39a). Many vital photocatalytic reactions, such as the reduction of water, CO2, or N2, are multi-electron processes and characterized by relatively high over-voltages of the rate-determining stages.586–590
A co-catalyst is typically required to accumulate the photogenerated charge carrier and to reduce the over-potentials thus increasing tremendously the efficiency of the photocatalytic transformations.76,587–589 As both CB electron and VB hole are consumed the semiconductor NC regains its original state. Provided that fresh donors and acceptors are constantly supplied to the photocatalyst surface it works continuously in a cyclic manner resembling a “pump” transferring electrons from the donors to the acceptors at the expense of the solar light energy.
To avoid the electron–hole recombination, the visible-light-harvesting nanocrystalline MC photocatalyst is typically coupled to another semiconductor having a less negative ECB and a more positive EVB levels (Fig. 39b). In this way the CB electron photogenerated in MC NCs can transfer across the interface to another semiconductor (process 5 in Fig. 39b). The interfacial transfer is typically very fast (occurring in the range of ps or even down to tens–hundreds fs) and results in a lowering of the CB electron chemical potential thus making impossible (or very improbable) the reverse transfer to MC NCs. The VB hole remains separated from the transferred CB electron due to a potential barrier between the valence bands of both components and in this way the electron–hole recombination is efficiently suppressed. Typically, the binary and MMC NCs are coupled to metal oxide, such as TiO2 or ZnO or metallate (titanate, tungstate, vanadate, etc.) semiconductors with a larger bandgap and a lower CB level.
The direct photoexcitation of such wide-bandgap semiconductor also results in a charge-separated state, i.e. the VB hole transfers irreversibly into the less positive VB of the MC NCs (process 5′, Fig. 39b). In this way, the same charge-separated state is produced irrespectively of which component of the heterostructure is photoexcited. Alternatively, the components of the photocatalytic system (semiconductor and metal or two semiconductors) can be physically separated in space and connected via an electric circuit.
Thus, the reactions with the participation of electrons and holes take place in different electrolytes connected via a bridge/membrane (Fig. 39c) and the photoexcitation results in the photocurrent generation. A photoelectrochemical mode is especially beneficial for the processes where recombination can occur between the intermediates or final products of the reaction as, for example, in the water splitting. The two-electrode photoelectrochemical system can also be externally biased allowing to reduce the potential barriers and increase the efficiency of photocatalytic transformations.
Pioneer reports on the semiconductor-mediated photocatalytic processes appeared as early as in 1920s–1930s dealing mostly with the photobleaching of dyes in the presence of titania crystals. In the late 1960s Fujishima and Honda reported on the water photoelectrolysis on externally-biased rutile TiO2 single-crystal electrode coupled to Pt and illuminated with the UV light.76,587,588 Afterwards, the miniaturization of semiconductor crystals to several microns and the direct deposition of Pt NPs on the titania surface allowed to compose suspension-based photocatalytic systems where both water reduction and oxidation took place on the suspended microparticle aggregates. Such systems can function without an external bias as the photogenerated electrons can migrate through the net of contacting crystals thus avoiding the electron–hole recombination.
As titania can harvest less than 5% of the solar light various ways of spectral sensitization of TiO2 micro- and nanocrystals were developed including doping with metal ions and non-metals (N,C,S) as well as coupling to molecular dyes and metal complexes and to narrow-bandgap MC and metal oxide NCs. The latter studies stimulated the development of the photochemistry of individual MC NCs and showed a great potential of these semiconductors for a variety of important processes, including the reduction of water and CO2 with sacrificial electron donors and the transformation of ionic inorganic species and various organic compounds aimed both on the total decomposition and on the formation of some specific synthetic products.
In recent years, nanocrystalline MMC semiconductors and related heterostructures showed an ever-increasing contribution into the development of various photocatalytic systems, including the reduction of water to molecular hydrogen and the destructive degradation of various organic species.11,23,24,36,38,40,41 The MMC NCs are quite broadly studied as light-harvesting components of the photoelectrochemical systems, operating similarly to the liquid-junction solar cells but aimed not at the photocurrent production but on the photooxidation/photoreduction of selected organic/inorganic species.

4.1 Photocatalytic hydrogen evolution with MC NCs. 
Typically, the MC NC-based photocatalytic systems for the hydrogen evolution contain noble metal co-catalysts, such as Pt NCs, that can be formed in situ via the photocatalytic reduction of metal precursors on the surface of MC NCs. This process results in a MC/Pt heterostructure which then acts as a photocatalyst of the water reduction. Both the Pt precursor and water are reduced at the expense of sacrificial electron donors, among the most efficient ones being sulfide and sulfite ions that can strongly adsorb on the NC surface and efficiently fill the VB holes inhibiting the oxidative photocorrosion of semiconductor NCs. The role of a sacrificial donor is often delegated to methanol, ethanol, triethanolamine and other organic substances.
Efficient systems for the hydrogen production were organized using CIS NCs without additional co-catalysts135,456 and decorated with Ru NCs,361 AIS NCs with the photodeposited Pt,364 ZAIS NCs coupled to MoS2 nanosheets,458 CZTS NCs coupled to Au NCs215 and Pt NCs,215,302 and CZTS/CdS nanostructures.591
Early studies of the photocatalytic properties of “classical” semiconductor photocatalysts, such as TiO2, showed that the nanocrystalline semiconductors typically reveal a much higher photocatalytic activity than the bulk counterparts even after the normalization of the H2 evolution rate to the surface area of the photocatalyst.587–589 This effect originates from a balance between the rates of the charge carriers migration to the crystal surface and their recombination which is much more favorable for the photocatalytic reactions in the case of the nanocrystalline materials.76,78 At the same time, among the nanocrystalline materials the highest efficiencies of the photocatalytic H2 evolution were observed for various loosely aggregated materials, such as mesoporous microaggregates of NCs, hollow microspheres, mesoporous NTs, etc. The advanced photocatalytic activity of such materials stems from the possibility of the migration of the photogenerated electrons along the network of contacting NCs that reduces considerably the probability of recombination events. Moreover, the effects of multiple light scattering and absorption within the mesoporous NC aggregates as well as accessibility of the loosely aggregated NCs to the electron donor moieties also contribute to the increased photoactivity.
The arsenal of the above-discussed approaches is broadly and fruitfully applied for the MMC NCs. In particular, the photocatalytic hydrogen evolution from aqueous donor solutions under the illumination with the visible light was reported for mesoporous spherical microaggregates of CIS NCs,413 CIZS NCs,418,592 AgGa1−xInxS2 NCs,379 and ZnIn2S4.366
Similar to the photocatalytic systems based on binary MC NCs, the MMC NCs require the presence of co-catalysts, typically metal NCs and, therefore, steady and considerable efforts are applied to replace the noble metal co-catalysts with less expensive and available species. The most successful examples are MoS2,88,377,403,458,593 RGO,438–441,443 carbon NTs,444 Ni2P,594 and [Co(bpy)3]2+ complex.74 Both molybdenium disulfide and RGO nanosheets feature a high activity in the hydrogenation/dehydrogenation processes, a large available surface area and excellent electron-accepting capacities.595–598
The catalytic activity of MoS2 was found to increase after Li+ intercalation during the hydrothermal synthesis of ZIS/MoS2 composites. The Li+ intercalation was found to transform hexagonal (2H) MoS2 into a metallic (1T) LixMoS2 form with a much higher electron-accepting capacity.599
It was found that growth of a-few-layer MoS2 nanosheets on the surface of ZIS starts on the sulfur vacancies abundant on the outer layers of ZIS particles.593 The components of the ZIS/MoS2 composite are, therefore, connected by common sulfur atoms resulting in a very efficient transport of the photogenerated electrons from ZIS to MoS2 particles, where water reduction takes place.
The activity of RGO as a hydrogen evolution co-catalyst was found to be far superior to that of carbon nanoparticles and carbon NTs.443 Typically, the dependence of the photocatalytic H2 evolution rate on the RGO content is dome-shaped439,441 with the maximal rate observed at around 1 wt% RGO (rel. to the photocatalyst load),441 most probably because of the light-shielding effect of RGO at higher loadings.
[Co(bpy)3]2+ complex can bind strongly to the MMC NC surface and has a more negative potential of the CoII/CoI transition (–0.95 V versus NHE) than the water oxidation potential (−0.41 V at pH 7). As a result, this complex acts as an electron relay accepting CB electrons from the NCs and transferring them to the water molecules in the outer coordination sphere.74
