The quaternary Cu2MSnS4 (M = Zn, Co, Ni) NCs were reported to serve as a co-catalyst of the hydrogen evolution in the presence of Eosine Y as a light-harvesting species and triethanolamine as a sacrificial donor.90,600,601 The photoexcited dye has a redox-potential E = −1.1 V (NHE) and can inject an electron into the CB of kesterite NCs positioned at −0.88 V (Cu2NiSnS4), −0.83 V (Cu2CoSnS4), and −0.65 V (Cu2ZnSnS4) versus NHE. The NCs act as an electron relay transferring the charge to adsorbed water molecules (−0.53 V, pH 9).90,600
The amount of noble metals can also be reduced by a partial substitution of Pt or Pd with catalytically-active transition metals, such as cobalt. The Co/Pt NC-modified CZTS NCs showed twice as high photocatalytic activity in the water reduction as the CZTS/Pt composites with the same nominal metal content (Fig. 40a).213
 Fig. 40  (a) Rate of the photocatalytic hydrogen evolution in the presence of CZTS NCs and their composites with Pt and PtCo NCs. (b) Photoaction and absorption spectra of the single-layer MnSb2S4 nanosheets, inset: a scheme for the photocatalytic hydrogen production over the MnSb2S4 monolayer. (c) Photoaction and absorption spectra of TiO2/CuGa2In3S8 nanoheterostructure. (d) Efficiency and selectivity of the photocatalytic NO3− reduction to N2 over CIS NCs. Reprinted with permissions from ref. 213 (a), ref. 524 (b), ref. 241 (c), and ref. 356 (d). Copyright (2015) American Chemical Society (a); (2016) The Royal Society of Chemistry (b and d); (2016) Elsevier (c). 
Another strategy stemming from the binary semiconductor photocatalysts is the application of various anisotropic MMC NCs, such as nanorods and nanowires. Single-crystalline character of such NCs coupled with the enhanced mobility of the photogenerated charge carriers along a long NC axis allows for a more efficient charge separation and related reactivity, than in the case of isotropic (spherical) NCs. Efficient hydrogen evolution was reported for Pt-decorated CIZS NRs,149 ZAIS NRs,157,602 CdS nanowires decorated with 2D CdIn2S4 nanosheets,415 CZTS NRs and nanoplates,603 ultra-thin CZTS nanosheets604 and the single-layer MnSb2S4 nanosheets.524 The latter compound has a layered structure with a [MnS2]2− sub-layer sandwiched between two [SbS]+ sublayers (inset in Fig. 40b). The calculations showed that the VB of MnSb2S4 is predominantly formed by the d-orbitals of Mn and the p-orbitals of sulfur, while the CB is composed mainly of the p-orbitals of Sb localized on the nanosheet surface.524 Therefore, the photoexcitation directly supplies photoelectrons to the adsorbed water molecules and protons. The VB holes are simultaneously extracted from the middle [MnS2]2− layer by Ce3+ acceptor. The photocatalyst is composed of broadly available and relatively low-toxic elements, it has a bandgap of 1.69 eV appropriate for the solar light harvesting, and can evolve H2 when illuminated by the visible light to 650 nm (Fig. 40b).
Ternary MC NCs are often combined with other, typically, wider-bandgap semiconductor NCs in composites with balanced CB and VB levels. These heterostructures possess a much higher photocatalytic/photoelectrochemical activity than the separate components taken summarily. For example, the graphitic carbon nitride (GCN) decorated with CIS and Pt NCs revealed a high photocatalytic activity in the hydrogen evolution from aqueous Na2S/Na2SO3 solutions, which is ∼3 and ∼6 times higher than the activity of GCN and CIS NCs taken alone, respectively.433 The rate of H2 evolution reaches ∼1.3 mmol g−1 h−1 in this system. Both components of the heterostructure can be excited in different sections of the visible spectrum resulting in the charge separation between CIS and GCN (Fig. 41a) and consequent spatial separation of the water reduction and the oxidation of sacrificial donors. A similar activity enhancement was observed for GCN/ZnIn2S4 heterostructures,434–436,605 where the photogenerated CB electron is transferred from GCN (ECB = −1.12 V, NHE) to ZIS NCs (ECB = −0.68 V, NHE), while the VB holes can decrease their energy by coming from the ZIS VB (+1.62 V, NHE) to the GCN VB (+1.57 V, NHE).429
 Fig. 41  Energy diagrams of the photocatalytic systems for (a and b) hydrogen evolution based on GCN/CIS (a) and cubic/hexagonal ZnIn2S4 (b) composites; (c) CO2 reduction based on CZTS/TiO2 heterostructure; (d) energy levels in TiO2–CaIn2S4 system. Reprinted with permissions from ref. 433 (a), ref. 339 (b), ref. 264 (c), and ref. 430 (d). Copyright (2017, 2015) American Chemical Society (a and d); (2016, 2017) The Royal Society of Chemistry (b and c). 
Similar to cadmium sulfide, ZnIn2S4 can crystallize in cubic and hexagonal forms having different CB/VB positions (hexagonal – ECB/EVB = −1.1/1.5 V, cubic – ECB/EVB = −1.5/0.9 V, NHE382) and thus a heterostructure with offset CB and VB levels forms between the cubic and hexagonal ZIS NCs (Fig. 41c).339,382 The composite can evolve hydrogen without additional co-catalyst, the efficiency of this photocatalytic process being 7.3 times and 3.2 times higher than in the case of sole cubic and hexagonal ZIS NCs, respectively.339
Efficient hydrogen evolution from the solutions of sacrificial donors under the visible light excitation was reported for TiO2/CIS361,422 and TiO2/AIS,428 BiVO4/CIGS606 and BiVO4/RGO/ZIS,440 In(OH)3/ZIS,381,607 ZIS/MoS2,377 ZIS/CdIn2S4/CuS,417 ZnFe2O4/ZIS and ZnFe2O4/ZIS/CdS,341 AIS/ZIS,608 TiO2/Cu3BiS3,609 CZTS/MoS2,403 and CZTS NCs loaded onto silica nanospheres.610 Recently, the combination of ZIS NCs with metal–organic frameworks was demonstrated to be a promising strategy for the design of new H2-evolving photocatalysts.611
To increase the driving force of the interfacial CB electron transfer from CIS NCs various methods of “the band design” can be applied, in particular, a partial substitution of In with Ga that results in a bandgap increment proportional to the gallium content.242,606 The mixed CIGS NCs revealed a higher photocatalytic activity in the hydrogen evolution from aqueous Na2S/Na2SO3 solutions, as compared to pure CIS NCs. A similar effect was also observed for AIGS compounds.511 At the same time, the bandgap increase shifts the light sensitivity threshold of CIGS NCs to shorter wavelengths as the Ga fraction is increased, thus reducing the visible-light-harvesting capabilities of the alloyed NCs. Both tendencies produce a dome-shaped dependence of the hydrogen evolution efficiency on the composition of CIGS NCs (Fig. 42a). The highest photoactivity is observed for CuIn0.3Ga0.7S2 (Eg = 1.75 eV).242 The modification of CIGS NCs with 1 wt% Pt results in a 3-fold increase of the H2 evolution rate reaching ∼1.7 mmol × g−1 × h−1.
 Fig. 42  (a and b) Photocatalytic hydrogen evolution rate as a function of (a) molar Ga fraction x in CuIn1−xGaxS2 NCs and (b) molar Zn fraction x in (CuIn)xZn2(1−x)S2 microspheres. Reprinted with permissions from ref. 242 (a) and ref. 352 (b). Copyright (2014) The Royal Society of Chemistry (a), (2010) Elsevier (b). 
A dome-shaped dependence of the hydrogen evolution QY on the solid solution NC composition is therefore a general phenomenon observed for a variety of MMC compounds, in particular CIZS,149,363,418,592 (AgIn)xZn2(1−x)S2 (peak activity reported at x = 0.574 and at x = 0.35–0.45157), and AgGa1−xInxS2 NCs (peak at x = 0.1).379
The noble metal co-catalysts for CIGS can be replaced with Ni(II),300 which is, most probably, reduced in situ to Ni0 NCs as it happens in CdS and CdxZn1−xS NC-based systems.598 The composition-optimized CuGa2In3S8 NCs showed a maximal QY of the H2 evolution from aqueous ascorbic acid solutions of 12% at 540 nm and the spectral sensitivity to 600–650 nm. It is also notable, that CIGS/Ni2+ system displayed the steady H2 evolution for a prolonged term of around 50 hours while the noble-metal-containing photocatalysts gradually lost the photoactivity, most probably due to the poisoning with the products of ascorbic acid oxidation.300
The highest photocatalytic activity in the water reduction for CIGS NCs deposited into the nanocrystalline titania was observed for a particular composition of CuGa2In3S8 NCs.241 The MMC acts in this system as a spectral sensitizer that absorbs the visible light and supplies the photogenerated electrons to titania where the hydrogen evolution occurs with the participation of Ru NCs. The sensitization effect is unambiguously confirmed by a close matching of the absorption spectrum of CIGS NCs and the photoaction spectrum of the TiO2/CIGS nanoheterostructure (Fig. 40c).
The Ru-loaded 4 nm Ga-enriched CIGS crystals revealed a photocatalytic activity in the hydrogen evolution under the illumination with the visible light up to 700 nm with an apparent QY of 6–7% at 560 nm.191,612
A similar dome-shaped dependence of the photocatalytic hydrogen evolution rate on the composition was observed for the alloyed (CuIn)xZn2(1−x)S2 solid solution microspheres with x varied in a range of 0.01–0.5 (Fig. 42b).352 The hybridized 4s4p orbitals of Zn2+ contribute to the CB of the alloyed NCs and, therefore, the introduction of zinc ions increases the bandgap and the photogenerated CB electron energy thus favoring the water reduction to H2. At the same time, the Eg increase reduces the capability of CIZS microspheres to absorb the visible light resulting in a compromise composition, x = 0.20 (Eg = 1.84 eV), at which the highest QY of the H2 formation, ∼15.5% at 420 nm, is observed. The in situ photodeposited Ru NCs acted as a co-catalyst enhancing the photoactivity of CIZS microspheres by more than an order of magnitude (Fig. 42b).352
A combination of CuGaS2 NCs with ZnS creates a p/n hetero-junction with enhanced separation of the photogenerated charge carriers between the components.412 As a result, the CGS/ZnS heterostructure is a 15-times more active photocatalyst of the hydrogen evolution than sole CGS approaching the photoactivity of “classical” CdS.412
The formation of a p/n heterojunction at the CZTS/ZnS core/shell interface imparted such composite with the photocatalytic activity in the hydrogen evolution exceeding by an order of magnitude the activity of a mechanical mixture of the kesterite and ZnS NCs.401
Colloidal (AgIn)xZn2(1−x)S2 NCs revealed distinct dependences of the photocatalytic activity in the hydrogen evolution from aqueous Na2S solutions both on the NC size and NC composition.93 The authors of ref. 93 selected a synthetic protocol yielding NCs of roughly the same size for different compositions allowing to analyze both types of dependences separately. The dependence of the photoactivity on the composition is “classical” for MMC NCs and similar to that discussed above for copper-based NCs. As the Zn content is increased, the photocatalytic activity of ZAIS NCs increases, reaches a summit at x = 0.5 and then decreases due to a balance between an increase of the CB electron energy and a loss of the light-harvesting capability (Fig. 43c).93 Almost for any studied x the dependence of the H2 evolution rate on the ZAIS NC size also features a maximum explained by another balance between the size-dependent driving force of the photoinduced CB electron transfer to water molecules and an increase in the surface disorder and charge trapping with a decreased NC size.93 In general, the highest photoactivity was observed for the ZAIS NCs with an average size of 4.2–5.5 nm and a bandgap of 2.3–2.4 eV (Fig. 43d).
 Fig. 43  (a and b) Dependence of the photocatalytic H2 evolution rate R(H2) on the average size (a) and bandgap (b) of ZAIS-x NCs prepared at different x. (c and d) Efficiency of the photocatalytic phenol decomposition as a function of the bandgap/size of CISe NCs (c) and the driving force of the photoinduced CISe CB electron transfer to O2 (d). Reprinted with permissions from ref. 93 (a and b) and ref. 87 (c and d). Copyright (2015, 2016) American Chemical Society. 
The calculations of the electron parameters of multinary Ag2ZnSnS4 and Ag2ZnSnSe4 compounds showed them to have a combination of suitable bandgaps and small electron effective masses (me* = 0.14m0–0.16m0 for sulfide, me* = 0.06m0–0.07m0 for selenide).86 These values are very beneficial for the photocatalytic water splitting, though the feasibility of using such compounds for the hydrogen production was experimentally verified only for 100–200 nm AZTS crystals decorated with Pt.613 The introduction of Ag2ZnSnS4 into a ternary Cu2ZnSnS4–Ag2ZnSnS4–ZnS heterostructure was found to impart it with the n-type conductivity and to facilitate the in situ photodeposition of Ru NC co-catalyst.614 The incorporation of ZnS results in an upward shift of the CB level of the heterostructure entailing it with the photocatalytic activity of H2 evolution from aqueous S2−/SO32− solution under the AM1.5G illumination.614
The nanocrystalline AIS and ZAIS microassemblies combined with the sheet-like MoS2 serve as efficient photocatalysts of the hydrogen evolution from aqueous lactic acid solutions.88 As shown by Mott–Schottky measurements, the CB potentials of the studied ZAIS compounds and MoS2 are −0.6 V and −0.1 V (NHE), respectively.88 Thus, the molybdenium disulfide nanosheets can accept the photogenerated ZAIS CB electrons acting as an electron-collecting co-catalyst.
The photocatalytic activity of ZAIS NCs in the hydrogen evolution from water/2-propanol mixtures can be enhanced by Au NCs covered with a protective SiO2 shell.162 The enhancement factor depends on the thickness of the silica shell and can be higher than 1.5 (Fig. 44a). As the insulating SiO2 shell prevents the direct electron transfer from ZAIS NCs to Au NCs, the latter act not as a typical metallic co-catalyst, but rather by enhancing the light absorption and charge separation in ZAIS NCs subjected to the electro-magnetic field created in Au/SiO2 NCs as a result of the photoinduced surface plasmon resonance (SPR). The Au-induced enhancement effect becomes stronger as the overlapping between the SPR band of gold NCs and the absorption band of ZAIS NCs is increased via a tuning of the NC composition.162 Noteworthy, a mechanical mixture of ZAIS and Au/SiO2 NCs did not show any enhancement of the photocatalytic activity indicating on a crucial role of the immobilization of the ZAIS NCs on the surface of silica-coated gold NCs.
 Fig. 44  (a) SPR-induced enhancement factor fenhance of the photocatalytic activity of ZAIS NCs by Au/SiO2 particles as a function of the silica shell thickness. (b) Kinetic curves of the photocatalytic hydrogen formation in the presence of sole CaIn2S4 NCs and their composites with Au and Pt NCs. Reprinted with permissions from ref. 162 (a) and ref. 616 (b). Copyright (2013) American Chemical Society (a); (2016) The Royal Society of Chemistry. 
A near-field enhancement of the photocatalytic hydrogen evolution caused by the SPR excitation was observed for Au/Cu2FeSnS4 composites with a gold core and a 20–30 nm-thick kesterite shell218 as well as for the core/shell Au/CZTS composites formed by the kesterite NRs or nanoplates.603
An effect of the plasmonic enhancement of the photocatalytic hydrogen production was also observed for binary CaIn2S4 composites with alloyed Au–Cu NCs615 and for ternary CaIn2S4/Au/Pt heterostructures.616 In the latter case, the enhancement effect was explained by the transfer of “hot” electrons from the SPR-excited Au NCs into the conduction band of CaIn2S4 and then – to Pt NCs, where the water reduction to H2 took place.616 The term “hot” refers to the electrons with the energy higher than the Fermi level of the CaIn2S4/Au heterostructure that can be generated by the plasmonic pumping of the light energy into the Au NCs. The assumption on the participation of the hot electrons was supported by the fact of hydrogen evolution at the illumination of the CaIn2S4/Au/Pt composite, while no H2 was observed at the same conditions for the non-plasmonic CaIn2S4/Pt heterostructure (Fig. 44b).616
The CZTS NCs revealed a phase-dependence of the photocatalytic activity in the water reduction, being almost 3 times higher for the kesterite modification than for the wurtzite NCs.617 The phenomenon was attributed to differences in the facet population on the NC surface. The dominantly exposed kesterite facets were {112} with an unbalanced charge of exposed anions and cations tending to compensate at the expense of the anions/cations adsorption. At the same time, the wurtzite showed much less polarized {100} facets selectively exposed. The preferential adsorption of protons and sulfide ions on the polar {112} facets of the kesterite CZTS NCs is considered to be the main reason for their enhanced photocatalytic activity.617


4.2 Photocatalytic reduction of CO2 and various ionic species. 
In general, the reported examples of these reactions are quite rare for the MMC NCs but a growing interest for such photoinduced transformations can be envisaged in the light of the abatement of CO2 emissions and related environmental issues.
The mesoporous CZTS coupled to the titania was reported as a photocatalyst of the CO2 reduction to CH4 under the solar irradiation.264 The CZTS NCs act as a spectral sensitizer excited by the visible light and transferring the CB electrons to lower-lying CB level of the nanocrystalline TiO2 (Fig. 41c).
The photocatalytic CO2 reduction to CO was reported for the one-unit-cell-thick ZnIn2S4 nanosheets.340 The key point defining the efficiency of the CO2 reduction is the presence of Zn vacancies on the nanosheet surface. Therefore, the photocatalytic activity of ZIS nanosheets decreased by a factor of almost 4 when the HTT conditions were adjusted to decrease the number of Zn vacancies.340 The photocatalytic activity of ZIS nanosheets can be further enhanced by coupling to the TiO2 nanobelts to form cascaded heterostructures.618 The alloying of ZnIn2S4 with CaIn2S4 results in variations of both CB and VB levels depending on the NC composition and producing the most negative ECB for a Zn0.4Ca0.6In2S4 compound.99 The photocatalytic reduction of CO2 to methane and CO with the participation of this compound is respectively ∼17 and ∼8 times faster than for ZnIn2S4 and ∼7 and ∼4 times faster than for CaIn2S4.99
The photocatalytic reduction of CO2 to methanol and further to methane was observed for titania nanowires covered with CIS nanoplates.619 Isotopic experiments with 13CO2 attested carbon dioxide to be the source of carbon atoms in the final products.
CIS NCs simultaneously loaded with Pt, Ru, and Au NCs were found to be an excellent UV- and visible-light-driven photocatalyst of the nitrate anion reduction to N2 with oxalic acid.356 The process is relatively fast and not accompanied by side processes of ammonia formation or H2 evolution. The highest apparent QY, ∼24%, was observed at 500 nm for the Pt co-catalyst, while the highest selectivity – for Pd NCs (Fig. 40d). The photocatalytic activity of the nanocrystalline CIS in the nitrate reduction by oxalic acid was reported to be equal to that of the nanocrystalline TiO2 Evonik P25,369 which is broadly used as a reference sample for comparing the photoactivity of various semiconductor nanomaterials.
The surfactant-free aqueous CZTS NCs revealed photocatalytic properties in the reduction of Cr(VI) to Cr(III) with only ∼5% loss in activity after ten consecutive cycles of the photocatalytic test.402 The TiO2/CIS/ZnS composites were also reported as a photocatalyst of Cr(VI) reduction in aqueous solutions. The stability of the heterostructure was considerably increased by covering it with an amorphous TiO2 layer.620,621


4.3 Photocatalytic decomposition of organic dyes and other compounds. 
The photodecomposition of organic dyes is often used as a test reaction to assess the photocatalytic properties of MC nanomaterials and compare them with other semiconductor materials. Typically, persistent dyes are used in these studies, that is, the dyes stable to the photochemical and thermal oxidation in the absence of photocatalysts, in particular, methylene blue, methyl orange, Rhodamine B, etc. The photocatalytic properties in the dye degradations were reported for CIS/ZnS,146 CIZS/ZnS NCs150 and CIZS NRs,155 AIS and ZAIS NCs,461 ZIS,353,417,622 CTS,174,532 CZTS178,209,215,388,393,399,402,403,623,624 and CZGS NCs,217 Fe-, Mn-, Co-, Ni-doped CZTS,388 and CFTS NCs,151 In4SnS8 nanosheets,156 CuGaS2 NCs,190 AgGaS2 NCs,172 AgGaSe2 NCs,188 and Ag8SnS6 NCs,176 as well as a variety of nanoheterostructures – TiO2/CIS145 and TiO2/CIZS,625 ZnO/CIS82,177 and ZnO/CIS/GO,432 ZnO/Cu2SnS3,486 ZnO/CISe,82 CdS/ZIS,424 ZIS/AgIn5S8,378 ZIS/CdIn2S4374 and ZIS/CdIn2S4/CuS,417 GCN/BiVO4/ZIS,437 carbon NT/CaIn2S4444 and Au/CaIn2S4,376 GCN/CdIn2S4,337 RGO/Cu2SnS3,442 TiO2/CZTS347 and La2Ti2O7/CZTS.451
The deposition of noble metal NCs (Pd, Au) on the surface of CZTS NCs was found to accelerate the photocatalytic degradation of methylene blue.209,626 This study showed that the presence of a sole co-catalyst NC on the surface of a CZTS NC is preferential than the deposition of multiple smaller metal NCs (Fig. 45a). The most probable reason for this phenomenon is the enhanced electron–hole recombination on the multiple boundaries between CZTS and metal NCs in the latter case.
 Fig. 45  (a) Methylene blue conversion efficiencies with CZTS and CZTS/Pd(Au) heterostructures. (b) A scheme of NIR light upconversion by the NaYF4:Yb,Tm phosphor coupled to CZTS NCs. Reprinted with permissions from ref. 209 (a) and ref. 623 (b). Copyright (2017) American Chemical Society (a); (2017) Elsevier. 
In the studies of the photocatalytic degradation of organic dyes the reported data on the photoactivity of semiconductor materials can be compromised by a self-sensitization effect, when the dye decomposes as a result of the photoexcitation and the electron transfer to the semiconductor NCs present in the system.627 In some cases this pathway can even be dominating, as for the Rhodamine B decomposition over a ZAIS/MoS2 heterostructure, where the photoexcitation of the adsorbed dyes molecules results in the cascade electron transfers from Rhodamine B to ZAIS to MoS2 and, finally, to oxygen.365
Similar to the hydrogen evolution systems, the dyes decomposition processes often reveal a dome-shaped dependence on the MC NC composition. A typical example is the photocatalytic degradation of Rhodamine B over TiO2/(ZnS)x(CuInS2)1−x composites where the highest activity was observed for NCs with x = 0.8 corresponding to Eg = 2.14 eV.625 The dependence originates from a lower light-harvesting capability at smaller x and a lower bandgap at higher x.
A proof-of-concept experiment on the expansion of the light-harvesting range of CZTS-based photocatalysts into the NIR domain by the up-converting approach was reported by Que et al.623 The CZTS NCs were deposited onto the nanocrystalline ZnO support and coupled to the NaYF4:Yb,Tm microplates. The NIR light absorption by the microplates populates higher-lying terms of the dopant atoms followed by the discharging of much more energetic visible and UV light quanta that are supposed to be absorbed by the neighbouring CZTS NCs (Fig. 45b) acting as a photocatalyst of the methylene blue degradation.623
The water-soluble CISe NCs capped with differently-sized polyethylene glycol (PEG) molecules with the terminal –SH groups revealed photocatalytic properties in the oxidation of a series of organic compounds – phenol, N,N-dimethyl-4-phenylenediamine, methylene blue, and thiourea under the illumination with the visible light.87 The universal action of the CISe NCs stems from the same primary and rate-determining step of the photoinduced CB electron transfer to molecular oxygen converting it to the superoxide anion-radical O2˙−. The superoxide-radical protonation to HO2˙ followed by the recombinative decomposition yields hydrogen peroxide that readily decomposes to very active HO˙ radicals:587,588,628,629HO2˙ + HO2˙ = H2O2 + O2,H2O2 = 2HO˙.
The oxygen reduction rate depends on the CB position of CISe NCs and increases as the size of NC is decreased due to the quantum confinement effect. In particular, the photocatalytic phenol conversion increased from 30% to 60% with a decrease of the average CISe NC size from 5.3 nm to 1.8 nm and an increase of the electrochemical (determined by the CV) bandgap from 1.4 eV to around 1.85 eV (Fig. 43a). At that, the phenol conversion efficiency follows almost linearly an increase of the thermodynamic driving force (free Gibbs energy difference, −ΔG) observed for the CISe NCs of a decreasing size (Fig. 43b). Another, but less important, factor limiting the photocatalytic activity of CISe NCs is the capping ligand size. The phenol conversion was found to be slightly (by around 10%) decreased when the number n of elementary (OCH2CH2) fragments in the thiolated PEG ligand increased from 6 to 18.87 At that, the photoactivity of PEGylated (n = 6) CISe NCs was at least 4 times higher as compared to the OLA-capped NCs.
The composites of nanocrystalline titania with microspherical aggregates of CaIn2S4 NCs revealed photocatalytic properties in the oxidation of two pharmaceuticals – isoniazid and metronidazole under the illumination with the visible light.430 The CB and VB levels of both components allow for the electron and hole separation (Fig. 41d). The electrons localized on TiO2 NCs reduce oxygen, while the holes are consumed in the direct oxidation of organic compounds and water to hydroxyl radicals. All three intermediates (O2˙−, h+VB, and HO˙) were confirmed to be active oxidizing species by the radical trapping experiments.430 The metronidazole was also photocatalytically destructed over the composites of hollow TiO2 microspheres with ZIS NC microaggregates.426
The composites of GCN with ZnIn2S4 NCs343,630 and CdIn2S4337 showed photocatalytic properties in the oxidation of antibiotic tetracycline under the visible light illumination. The GCN/ZIS nanoheterostructures were also reported to act as a photocatalyst of methyl orange and phenol oxidation.434
The ternary BiVO4/BiFeO3/CIS nanoheterostructures revealed an increased photocatalytic activity in the degradation of 2,4-dichlorophenol.450 The photoactivity enhancement (as compared to separate CIS and BiVO4/CIS) is associated with the inner polarization introduced by the bismuth ferrite NCs and facilitating the photoinduced electron transfer from CIS to bismuth vanadate. The introduction of RGO into the TiO2 NT array/CIS heterostructures enhances their photocatalytic activity in the 2,4-dichlorophenol degradation by around 40%.484 ZIS microwires composed of nanosheets with preferentially exposed (006) facets showed a high photocatalytic activity and stability in the oxidation of tetracycline and 2,4,6-tribromophenol.622
TiO2/CIS nanoheterostructure immobilized on glass can be used as an active and stable photocatalyst of the 4-nitrophenol oxidation.631 The photocatalytic properties in the 2-nitro-phenol degradation were also reported for AIS nanoplates.632
The TiO2 NT/CIS heterostructures can be applied as a photocatalyst of 2,4-dichlorophenoxyacetic acid degradation under the visible light illumination.483 The TiO2/CIS composites were used as a photocatalyst for the degradation of soil fumigant 1,3-dichloropropene.148,633 To protect the composite in the aggressive conditions of the photoprocess it was covered with a ∼1.3 nm-thick layer of Al-doped ZnS.
A difference between the CB levels of titania (−0.4 V, NHE) and Ag–In–S NCs (−0.57 V, NHE) coupled into a TiO2/AIS nano-heterostructure enables efficient transfer of the photogenerated electrons from AIS to TiO2 resulting in a high photocatalytic activity of such composite in the visible-light-driven gas-phase degradation of 1,2-dichlorobenzene.423
Selective oxidation of aromatic alcohols to corresponding aldehydes driven by the visible light was reported for ZnO/Zn3In2S6 heterostructure.367 The effect of selectivity is attributed to a cooperative catalytic action of zinc oxide that weakens the C–H and O–H bonds of the alcohols with a photocatalytic effect of ZIS NCs supplying oxidants – the photogenerated h+VB and O2˙−.
Mo-Doped ZIS NCs were reported to be an efficient photocatalyst of the cyclization of aromatic amines into imidazoles.634


4.4 Photoelectrochemical/photoelectrocatalytic processes with MMC NCs. 
The photocurrent generation in a photoelectrochemical systems based on MC NCs results from two simultaneous processes – the oxidation of water or additional sacrificial electron donor on a NC-sensitized photoanode and the reduction of water (or other acceptor species) on a counter-electrode (Fig. 39c). The photocurrent density is proportional to the amount of the H2 evolved on the counter electrode and can be adopted as a quantitative measure of the water splitting efficiency.
An effect of the spectral sensitization of photocurrent generation was observed for titania NT arrays decorated with CIS NCs,470 CIZS nanosheets,449 and AIS NCs;471 TiO2 NR arrays with CIS NCs427 and CZTS NCs,203 TiO2 nanowire/In2S3/CIS,472 TiO2/ZnIn2S4/CdS,429 ZnO nanowire arrays decorated with CIS NCs321 or CuSbS2 NCs,476 ZnO NRs with CIS NCs,446,482 Cu2SnS3,486 and AgSbS2 NCs,447 ZnO NTs with Ag–Cu–Sb–S NCs,448 as well as for CdS nanowire/CdIn2S4 heterostructure.89,415
The TiO2 nanowire arrays covered with a buffer In2S3 layer and decorated with CIS NCs revealed unprecedentedly high stability during at least twenty consecutive current–voltage sweeps in an aqueous KOH electrolyte in the absence of typical sulfide-containing sacrificial donors.472 The high MMC stability originates, most probably, from very efficient charge separation and transfers. A similar effect of fast hole transfer was deemed to be responsible for a very high photocorrosion stability of CdS/CdIn2S4 photoelectrodes.89
The MMC NC-based photoanodes are often modified with additional MC shells both to enhance the light-harvesting efficiency and to protect the MC layer from the chemical/photochemical corrosion. For example, a protective ZnS shell was deposited onto the TiO2/CIZS photoanode,464 while the deposition of a Bi2S3 layer over the TiO2/CIS heterostructure increases the photoelectrochemical activity by a factor of 1.5 as a result of an increased light absorbance and a possibility of the cascade charge separation.468 A buffer layer of zinc oxysulfide placed between the TiO2 nanorod array and a layer of the CZTS sensitizer NCs resulted in a photocurrent density of ∼15 mA cm−2 (at 1.23 V versus NHE) which is a record for the CZTS NC-based photoelectrodes.203
The ZnIn2S4 nanosheets were tested as light-harvesting components of a photoelectrochemical system without additional semiconductors and showed a promising light-to-current conversion efficiency.380,522 With the n-type conductivity and a bandgap of 2.6 eV this material can be a good alternative to toxic cadmium sulfide which is broadly applied in the photocatalytic and photoelectrochemical light-harvesting systems. The deposition of a layer of ZnIn2S4 nanosheets on the surface of Si nanowires increased the light conversion efficiency by a factor of 64.416
Along from the water splitting, many other processes can be realized in a photoelectrochemical regime with physically separated reduction and oxidation stages, for example, the visible-light-driven 2-chlorophenol oxidation on the surface of TiO2 NT/CIS photoanode.118
Conventional binary photoactive MC NCs, such as cadmium, zinc and lead chalcogenides reveal usually the n-type conductivity and participate, therefore, in the oxidation reactions with the electrolyte components, while the photogenerated electrons are transferred to a metal oxide scaffold and then – via the circuit to a CE. As opposite, the multinary In, Ga, and Sn-based MC compounds typically have the p-type conductivity that opens the possibilities of constructing photocathodes releasing the photogenerated electrons to the electrolyte species and holes – into the external circuit and on a CE. For example, a p-conductive CIS NC layer was used as a photocathode in a photoelectrochemical system for the selective reduction of CO2 to methanol with pyridinium ions as a co-catalyst.478,489 The process requires an external bias of 20 mV to compensate for the CO2 reduction overpotential and shows a Faradaic efficiency of 97%.
The photoelectrochemical activity of the nanocrystalline CIS is affected by the stoichiometry and the presence of “alien” ions. In particular, the Cu-rich CIS NCs showed a much higher activity in the presence of methylviologen (4,4′-dimethylbipyridyl bication) as an electron acceptor as compared to the Cu-poor CIS NCs.144 The highest photoelectrochemical activity of the non-stoichiometric CIS deposited on ITO was observed at a Cu/In ratio of 0.28.477
The Zn-, Sb-, or Ni-doped CIS films evolve H2 from aqueous S2−/SO32− solutions when illuminated with the visible light,311 the highest activity observed for the Zn-doped films. Doping with Ce was also found to increase the photoelectrochemical activity of the nanocrystalline CIS films; the highest light conversion efficiency was reported for 10 mol% Ce.485
A composite GCN/CIS photocathode evolves hydrogen from 0.1 M H2SO4, the presence of polymeric GCN sheets enhancing considerably the stability of CIS toward the photocorrosion and revealing steady performance for at least 22 h.635 A similar stabilizing effect of S-doped GCN was observed for the CIS/CdS photocathodes.636
The photoelectrochemical efficiency of CIS NC-based photo-cathodes can be multiplied in the presence of Pt,490,637 Au or Ag NCs,635 and additional protective layers, such as In2S3490 and CdS.411,490,637 The introduction of RGO sheets into the CIS-based photoelectrode results in a 2-fold increase of the photogenerated current density.478
The deposition of n-type CdS and TiO2 NCs on the surface of porous CIS results in the formation of p/n junctions enhancing the cathodic photocurrent and increases the apparent light-to-hydrogen conversion to 1.82%.488
CIGS is a popular MC material for the photocathodes with a broad spectral response covering the entire UV, visible and partially NIR range to ∼1200 nm.635,638,639 The CIGS films on the optically transparent electrodes can generate the cathodic photocurrent as a result of the reduction of dissolved molecular oxygen with the simultaneous oxidation of water (or sacrificial donor) on a counter electrode. The photoactivity of CIGS layers can be enhanced drastically by the deposition of Pt NCs acting as relays of the electron transfer to O2 (Fig. 46a). A further acceleration of the cathodic process as well as an enhancement in the photocathode stability can be achieved by the deposition of a ZnS shell onto the CIGS surface prior to the anchoring of Pt NCs (Fig. 46a).638 The generation of cathodic photocurrents in the absence of additional sacrificial donors was also reported for alloyed ZnSe-CIGSe electrodes.516,640
 Fig. 46  (a) Current–voltage curves registered under the chopped illumination for CIGS photocathode, as well as CIGS/Pt and CIGS/ZnS/Pt heterostructures. (b) An energy level scheme of Mo/CIGS/CdS/ZnO photocathode for the water splitting. (c) SEM/scheme of TiO2 NR/ZnIn2S4/CdS photoanode. (d) Energy diagram of FTO/TiO2/ZnIn2S4/CdS photoanode. Reprinted with permissions from ref. 638 (a), ref. 641 (b), ref. 642 (c), and ref. 119 (d). Copyright (2014, 2015) American Chemical Society (a and b); (2017) Elsevier (c); (2015) The Royal Society of Chemistry (d). 
An enhancement of the photocurrent generation on the CIGS photocathodes was observed after the deposition of CdS, TiO2, and ZnxSnyOz layers as a result of the formation of p/n junctions.515 The best CIGS/CdS/TiO2/Pt photocathode exhibited a light-to-current conversion efficiency of 2.63%.515 However, this composite gradually lost the photoelectrochemical activity as a result of the interdiffusion of Pt atoms into the film and the CIGS constituent – in the outer layer as well as the degradation of the protective oxide layer.
A ternary photocathode was formed on the surface of co-evaporated CIGS layer on the Mo-covered glass by depositing consecutively layers of CdS and ZnO (Fig. 46b).641 The CIGS and CdS phases having the p- and n-type conductivity, respectively, form a p/n heterojunction enabling the efficient electron/hole separation and electron transport in the bulk of CdS layer. Then, due to a CB level offset between CdS and ZnO the electron is forwarded to the photocathode/electrolyte interface where it can reduce water to hydrogen. The electrodeposition of a Pt layer onto ZnO enhances the latter process producing the cathodic photocurrents as high as −32.5 mA cm−2.641 Simultaneously, the water oxidation to O2 occurs on the Pt CE thus making this system similar to the natural photosynthesis systems. This principle was also realized in water-splitting systems with hydrogen-evolving TiO2/CdS/CZTS326 and Pt/TiO2/CdS/CBTSSe643 photocathodes.
A photoelectrochemical cell with Ru-modified CGZS photocathode and CoOx-modified BiVO4 photoanode, both absorbing in the visible spectral range, can split water without the application of an external bias.606 Recently, a tandem cell with a ZnSe–CIGSe photoanode and a BiVO4 photoanode was reported to yield 1% efficiency of the water splitting in an unstirred electrolyte.644
Recently, the selective six-electron photoreduction of nitrobenzene to aniline was reported for a nanocrystalline CZTS photocathode put under a small bias.645
One of the rapidly evolving directions of NC-based photo-electrochemistry is the development of various photodriven sensors, in particular immunosensing platforms. For example, a photoelectrochemical immunosensor exploiting simultaneously semiconductor photoanode and photocathode was reported by Zhang et al.646 The sensor comprised an ITO/TiO2/CdS:Mn photoanode and an ITO/CIS photocathode modified by an antibody complementary to a prostate-specific antigen. The photocathode surface free from the antibody is blocked by a layer of chitosan/bovine serum albumine. After the introduction of target species that specifically interact with the photocathode-anchored antibody, the photocathode surface gets completely blocked by the bulky organic molecules that impede the photocathodic oxygen reduction (Fig. 47a). The blocade decreases the photocurrent proportionally to the logarithm of the target concentration allowing the determination in a concentration range of 1 pg mL−1 to 100 ng mL−1 with a detection limit of 0.32 pg mL−1.646 The design of the sensor allows to solve the problem of a low selectivity of typical photoanode-based sensors caused by indiscriminative oxidation of all present organic species by the photogenerated holes. In the present system the molecular oxygen is the sole electron acceptor and the photocathodic process is not interfered by any side reactions.
 Fig. 47  (a) A scheme of the CIS-based photoelectrochemical immunosensor, Ab – complementary antibody, Ag – target antigen, BSA – bovine serum albumin. (b) An energy diagram of the FTO/ZnO/CdS/CZTS photoanode. (c) Chronoamperograms registered for FTO/TiO2, FTO/TiO2/CZTS and FTO/TiO2/CZTS/Ag2S photoanodes with a different size of Ag2S NCs (explanation of H1–H3 designations provided in the text). (d) An energy diagram of the FTO/CZTS/ZnSe/CdS/CdSe photocathode. Reprinted with permissions from ref. 646 (a), ref. 320 (b), ref. 195 (c), and ref. 261 (d). Copyright (2014–2016) American Chemical Society (a, c and d); (2015) The Royal Society of Chemistry (b). 
The ZnIn2S4 NCs reveal the n-type conductivity, similarly to binary ZnS and CdS and can be combined with other n-type semiconductor NCs into the heterostructured photoanodes. For example, the titania NR arrays were decorated by the nanocrystalline ZnIn2S4 nanosheets and converted by the partial ion exchange into the ZIS/CdS heterostructure642 (Fig. 46c). The light-harvesting layer was then decorated with an efficient hole acceptor – Ni(OH)2 that enhanced the electron/hole separation and increased the anodic photocurrent. The fast withdrawal of the photogenerated VB holes by nickel hydroxide imparts the photoanode with a high stability toward the oxidative photocorrosion.642
A combination of TiO2, ZIS and CdS with the CB levels at −0.3 V, −0.4 V, and −0.5 V (NHE), respectively, creates a cascade structure with the CB potential decreasing from the outer CdS layer to the inner TiO2 scaffold allowing for a directed flow of the photogenerated electrons from the electrolyte species through the photoanode components and further – into the external circuit.119 Simultaneously, the VB offsets of the components allow for a directed flow of the photogenerated VB holes in the opposite direction – toward the electrolyte species (Fig. 46d). As a result, the photoelectrochemical activity of the ternary TiO2/ZIS/CdS composite exceeds that of binary TiO2/CdS and TiO2/ZIS heterostructures by 2 and 3 times, respectively.
A similar cascaded charge transfer occur also in the FTO/ZnO/CdS/CZTS photoanodes allowing for the efficient photocurrent generation at the expense of the oxidation of S2−/SO32− and the hydrogen evolution on the Pt CE (Fig. 47b).320 The latter reference reported an original “two-storey” photoanode composed of a ZnO/CdS/CZTS film with an upper layer of stainless steel grid covered with the same ternary heterostructure. The grid absorbs both incident light and the photons reflected/scattered from the bottom FTO/ZnO/CdS/CZTS layer and thus the combined structure produces a much higher photocurrent (∼12.6 mA cm−2) than the separate layers (∼6.3 mA cm−2 and ∼9.6 mA cm−2 for the mesh- and FTO-supported heterostructures, respectively).320
Nanocrystalline kesterite CZTS can be used as a photocathode material for the water reduction after the deposition of protective layers of CdS, Al-doped ZnO, and TiO2, and the decoration with co-catalyst Pt NCs.493 The best Mo/CZTS/CdS/AZO/TiO2/Pt photocathodes showed a photocurrent density of ∼1 mA cm−2 under the AM1.5 illumination and a long-term stability.493 CZTS NCs decorated by the in situ photodeposited Pt NCs revealed a photoelectrochemical activity in the water reduction in aqueous S2−/SO32− solutions.408 The photoelectrochemical activity of alloyed Cu2(FexZn1−x)SnS4 NCs can be modulated by varying the NC composition, the highest photocurrents observed at x = 0.4.284
A nanocrystalline CZTS photocathode was combined with different photoanodes composed of TiO2, WO3 and BiVO4 NCs.494,496 In such Z-scheme the photocathode is capable only of the water reduction, the VB potential being insufficiently positive for the water oxidation to O2. The coupling to a photoanode allows to combine the H2 evolution on the CZTS photocathode with the O2 evolution on the photoanode when both are connected by a salt bridge and a Fe2+/Fe3+ electron-shuttling redox-couple.494
The CZTS NC-based Mo/Cu2ZnSnS4/CdS/Pt photocathode was applied to reduce toluene to methylcyclohexane under the illumination with the visible light.495 This process, along with the benzene reduction to cyclohexane, is considered to be a very promising way of the hydrogen storage in the form of liquid molecular hydrides, because H2 can be recovered relatively easily from these compounds under mild conditions and a corresponding metal catalyst.495
Binary CZTS/Ag2S NCs was used as a spectral sensitizer for the nanocrystalline TiO2 photoanodes.195 Silver sulfide NCs were produced by the partial cation exchange in the parent CZTS NCs and the size of Ag2S NCs varied from 2 nm to 4 nm and to 7 nm (samples H1, H2, and H3 in Fig. 47c) by increasing the Ag+ precursor amount. The peak photocurrent was observed for the 4 nm Ag2S NCs, being by an order of magnitude higher than for the pristine CZTS sensitizer NCs (Fig. 47c). The boosted photoefficiency was attributed to the cascaded electron transfers from CZTS to Ag2S and to TiO2 coupled to the directed hole transfer in the opposite direction.195 The deterioration of the photocurrent generation efficiency for larger Ag2S NCs is explained by possible changes in the crystalline structure of CZTS NCs due to Ag+ inclusions as well as by the depletion of CZTS phase (transforming into Ag2S during the ion exchange) acting as a perfect electron/hole relay in the ternary TiO2/CZTS/Ag2S composite.
In the design of composite MC NC-based photocathodes, a reverse cascade of CB positions is typically organized. The CB potential decreases (in the absolute value) from the inner components of the photocathode to its outer components to facilitate the electron transport to the acceptor species in the electrolyte. The CZTS/ZnSe/CdS/CdSe heterostructure reported by Sivula et al.261 serves as a perfect example of this strategy (Fig. 47d). The transfer of the photogenerated electrons to the accepting Eu3+ species in solution is facilitated by a declining CB in the CZTS/CdS/CdSe cascade, while for the ZnSe layer the authors suggest a midgap-state-mediated electron transfer. The quaternary structure exhibits a higher photocurrent than any other possible binary or ternary combination of the components.261
Nanocrystalline Cu2BaSnS4 with a bandgap of around 2 eV was reported to be a promising material for the water-reducing photocathodes,558 a similar cascade effect being realized in the photoelectrochemical water splitting system with the Cu2BaSnS4 photocathode decorated by consecutive layers of CdS, ZnO, and TiO2.519
The p-conducting nanocrystalline Cu12Sb4S13 and Cu3SbS4 films on ITO revealed a photoelectrochemical activity when coupled with a Pt CE and immersed in an aqueous Eu3+ acceptor solution.246 The modification of Cu2SbS4 films with a layer of In2S3 creates a p/n junction resulting in an enhancement of the photocathodic hydrogen evolution over the Pt-decorated Cu3SbS4/In2S3 film coupled to a Pt CE.497 A photoelectrochemical activity was also observed for the p-type CuSbS2 NCs (Eg = 1.40 eV) on FTO290 and for CuAsS2 NCs (1.25 eV) deposited on Mo/glass substrates.181 Cu2SnS3 NCs formed a Schottky junction with Au NCs allowing for the transfer of the photogenerated CB electrons to the metal NCs and favoring the photocurrent generation.174

Conclusions and outlook
Numerous reports discussed in this review show unambiguously that the MMC NCs differ in many aspects from their binary MC “relatives” when applied as the light-sensitive and auxiliary components of various light-harvesting systems. These distinctions stem mostly from a unique capability of MMC NCs to tolerate a broad variability of compositions and stoichiometries while exhibiting the same and unified structural motif. This feature is multiplied by two other distinctive characteristics of MMC NCs, in particular, the unprecedented capability of forming solid solutions by the partial substitution of either metal or chalcogenide ions as well as the unique tolerance to doping.
The tolerance to the non-stoichiometry allows to vary optical, photophysical and electrophysical properties of ternary and quaternary MC NCs in a broad range providing potent means of tailoring both their optical response and the properties of the photogenerated charge carriers. Apart from the energy characteristics, the conductivity type, the charge carrier density and the structure of the surface double electric layer can be affected by the phase composition and the ratios of components of the MMC NC varied within a certain set of elements – Cu–In–S, Cu–Zn–In–S, Cu–Zn–Sn–S, etc.
Along with the tolerance to the non-stoichiometry, the optical and electrophysical properties of MMC NCs can be affected in a decisive manner by alloying several types of cations/anions. The NC characteristics change gradually and in a continuous manner with the partial substitution of the “central” metal ions, e.g. In with Ga in CIS or Sn with Ge in CZTS. Similar trends are observed if a combination of two chalcogenide ions is introduced simultaneously in a multinary NC compound.
Finally, the doping can be applied to achieve various aims, in particular, to affect the energies of the photogenerated charge carriers and to fill the lattice vacancies like in the case of AIS NC doping with zinc or copper ions. Moreover, the doping can induce a controlled aggregation, as in the case of Sb3+ in CZTS NCs or affect the charge transport properties as in the case of indium doping of CZTS NCs.
These three features – the non-stoichiometry, the alloying and the doping taken together define a specific landscape of the studies of the light-harvesting systems based on the MMC NCs making it quite different from “conventional” binary NC such as cadmium or lead chalcogenides. However, despite the discussed differences the basic principles of the light-harvesting system design, the ideology of enhancing the efficiency of the light conversion and minimizing the losses of the photogenerated charge carriers as well as the protocols for the synthesis of MMC NCs are generally the same as devised and tested for the binary MC NCs.
From the synthetic viewpoint, the multinary NC sensitizers for the solar cells and photocatalysts for the visible-light-driven redox-reactions are produced most often via the heating up/hot injection syntheses and the hydrothermal/solvothermal treatments, respectively – similarly as it happens with the binary MC NCs. However, a much more intricate chemistry lies behind the formation of ternary, quaternary and even more complex MC NCs as compared to the binary counterparts, arising from a different reactivity of metal ion-constituents, the possibilities of ions migration and intermixing, the formation of different phases (even within the single NCs) and solid solutions, etc. In this view, a precise control of the MMC NC size, shape, composition, surface chemistry and other parameters becomes a much more challenging task. The need of the post-synthesis ligand exchange and phase transfer procedures makes handling of these NCs even more difficult. Therefore, even the case of “conventional” heating up/hot injection syntheses cannot be considered as a “done and closed” one. Indeed, further studies on the mechanism of the multinary NC nucleation and growth, the parameters governing the NC shape, phase and composition as well as the routes of more efficient ligand exchange, which is vital for the light-harvesting applications, are still necessary and will undoubtedly be continued in the future.
Also, a broad field for new methods is open for the syntheses of multinary NCs. In particular, the syntheses of concentrated MC NC “inks” directly in polar solvents with relatively small multifunctional capping ligands and a defined composition/size can boost the development of both bulk-heterojunction and liquid-junction solar harvesters and make them much more competitive relative to the organic bulk-heterojunction and dye-sensitized liquid-junction cells. For both cell types, the self-assembly of light-absorbing MC NCs into highly ordered and dense layers with a pre-defined thickness and symmetry is a crucial task requiring the development of new approaches to the NC surface modification. A very promising step in this direction was made by stabilizing the MMC NC with highly charged metal chalcogenide complexes that allow for the self-assembly of the NCs and the conversion of the capping MCC ligands into a conducting interconnecting layer after the thermal treatment.22 A further progress in the chemistry of NC-MCC composites may have far-reaching consequences for the progress of the NC-based solar cells as well as for other fields dealing with the self-assembled NC-based solids.27
The multinary MC NCs were successfully introduced into the solid-state bulk-heterojunction solar cells both as light absorbers and charge transport layers. As the MMC NC are intrinsically rich with lattice vacancies and/or defects with the corresponding electron/hole states lying in the bandgap, the solar light can be harvested not only in the “classical” range of hν ≤ Eg, but also below the bandgap as a result of the light absorption involving the defect-related states. This feature opens the possibilities of the NC design aimed at an optimization of such light absorption and an expansion of the light harvesting range.
The morphology of wide-bandgap metal oxide scaffolds housing the MMC NCs in the solar cells can affect not only the charge separation and transport efficiency but also the character of such below-bandgap absorbance95 and, therefore, the further optimization/design of the oxide scaffold is expected to be one of the major factors affecting the efficiency of MMC NC-based bulk-heterojunction solar cells as it is in the case of hybrid organo-inorganic perovskites.30–32
Additionally to the light harvesting, the MMC NC can also perform as a hole transport layer when introduced in combinations with conjugated polymers and hybrid perovskites. The latter combination seems especially attractive because the MMC NC acts as a second light absorber that enhances the light harvesting of the cell and even extends it to the NIR range. A hint at the perspectives of combining the hybrid perovskites with the MMCs was provided in 2014 by a report of Todorov et al.647 where the solar cells with adjacent layers of the microcrystalline CIGSe or CZTSSe and the hybrid CH3NH3PbI3 perovskite manifested light conversion efficiencies exceeding 20%. It is expected that by extending this strategy to the bulk heterojunctions of nanocrystalline MMC materials even higher light-harvesting efficiencies can be achieved.
The development of the technologies of ink-jet printing of some components of the bulk-heterojunction solar cells or even the entire cell including the ETL, HTL and conducting contact layers is of paramount importance for the progress of such devices. In view of special features of the MMC NCs, this method is expected to yield especially highly efficient cells when applied for the consecutive deposition of NC differing in composition and size. This will produce gradient structures with the CB/VB levels of the absorbing layers inclined to one or other charge transport layer thus enhancing the primary separation and migration of the photogenerated charge carriers as compared to “conventional” devices with a constant composition of the NC absorber layer.
The simplicity and efficiency of the jet printing can be combined with broadly available multinary NC compounds, such as copper/silver bismuth sulfide to produce inexpensive solar cells. Such compounds manifested up to now quite modest light-to-current conversion efficiencies, however a trade-off between the low production cost, low toxicity, high availability and moderate light-conversion efficiency can make such devices a real competitor to both high-production-cost thin-film MC solar cells and the inherently toxic lead-containing perovskite solar cells.
The MMC NCs were found to be quite efficient spectral sensitizers for the wide-bandgap oxide photoanodes in the photoelectrochemical liquid-junction solar cells, similarly to the case of binary CdX/PbX-based cells. The broad variations of the CB/VB levels and the spectral sensitivity range by adjusting the composition and size of MC NCs promised unprecedently versatile possibilities of the design of such cells. These designs may be further extended if coupled to the accumulated knowledge of the basic principles of the optimal cell morphologies and the prevention of charge losses by various additional blocking/buffer layers gained previously for the SSSCs with binary CdX/PbX NCs.
However, the “classical” design of such cells implies the utilization of a photoanode and therefore the sensitizer NCs should preferably have an n-type electrical conductivity along with the correspondence between the CB levels of the MMC and metal oxide NCs. The combination of these requirements de facto restricted the scope of available MMC sensitizers to the n-conducting CIS/AIS compounds and their derivatives, while the highly promising kesterite family did not show acceptable activities in the liquid-junction solar cells. Nevertheless, this restriction can be avoided in alternative cell design, where the light is harvested with a NC-incorporating photocathode, where the sensitizer supplies the VB holes to the p-conducting metal oxide scaffold and accepts electrons from a redox-shuttle in the electrolyte. This design was shown to be efficient for lead chalcogenide absorbers, but only pioneer works appear to be published starting from 2016 on the multinary CIS/CISe NC-based photocathodes of the liquid-junction SSSCs.131 It may be expected that a further development of such cell design and broad involvement of other MC compositions, such as kesterite CZTS/CZTSe NCs will allow to realize more completely a huge potential of multinary MC NCs as the light harvesting components of the liquid-junction SSSCs. The perspectives of this direction are vividly exemplified by a variety of photocathodes applied in the photoelectrochemical light-harvesting systems for hydrogen generation as well as in the photoelectrochemical immunosensors.
This strategy can also be extended to the cells combining a photocathode and a photoanode both absorbing the solar light and contributing to the photocurrent generation. A careful optimization of such tandem cells (a successful example can be found for a PbS-based tandem liquid-junction SSSC648) is expected to add the photovoltages of both electrodes and bring the cell efficiency above the Schockley–Queisser limit.
Among the photocatalytic light-harvesting systems based on multinary MC NCs the most vivid progress was achieved in the area of water splitting/hydrogen evolution systems both in photocatalytic and photoelectrocatalytic versions. The multinary MC compounds are tested as the water-splitting photocatalysts for over three decades now and the photocatalyst crystal size coming into the nanometer range brought a new dimensionality and impetus to these studies. Similar photosynthetic systems for CO2 and nitrogen reduction are not as high developed as for the binary MC photocatalysts but a surge of interest for such systems is expected in view of recent apprehension of the global risks and challenges posed by the over-abundant anthropogenic CO2 emissions and the associated global climate regulation initiatives.649
Various photocatalytic reactions with the MMC NCs can also be used as a test for new and emerging compounds as potential light-harvesters for the solar cells. Indeed, the MMC materials typically used in the solar cells, such as chalcopyrites CIS/AIS and kesterites CZTS/CZTSe appeared to serve as excellent photocatalysts both in the individual state and when introduced into the heterostructures with other nanocrystalline semiconductors, 2D materials, and metals. At that, the range of MMC materials tested as photocatalysts is even broader than that of light-harvesting solar cell components. In this view, the photocatalytic reactions with MMC NCs should serve as an inspiration for the development of new solar cells, as the photocatalytically active MC NCs will most probably appear to be also efficient light absorbers. Some of the multinary compounds gained a reputation as efficient and quite universal photocatalysts with the activity close to or exceeding that of “classical” semiconductor photocatalysts – TiO2 and CdS, but many of such MC compounds were only scarcely tested in the solar cells; the layered zinc indium sulfide is probably the most vivid example. It is expected that the simultaneous development of the MMC NC-based photocatalysis and photovoltaics will be a source of mutual enrichment with new materials and ideas.

Table Content:

Oleksandr Stroyuk
	Oleksandr Stroyuk is presently a Marie Skłodowska-Curie Fellow at the TU Dresden (Germany) in the group of Prof. Alexander Eychmüller and an acting Head of the Laboratory of Organic Photovoltaics and Electrochemistry at L. V. Pysarzhevsky Institute of Physical Chemistry (IPC), National Academy of Sciences of Ukraine. He received his PhD in chemistry (2003) from IPC under supervision of Prof. S. Ya. Kuchmiy and a degree of Doctor of Sciences in physical chemistry from IPC (2011) for a series of works “Semiconductor nanophotocatalysis: nature, features, photocatalytic systems and reactions”.

Alexandra Raevskaya
	Alexandra Raevskaya is a senior staff scientist at the Department of Photochemistry, L. V. Pysarzhevsky Institute of Physical Chemistry (IPC), National Academy of Sciences of Ukraine. She received her PhD in chemistry (2004) from IPC under supervision of Prof. S. Ya. Kuchmiy. Currently she is a visiting scientist at the TU Dresden (Germany) in the group of Prof. Alexander Eychmüller. Her present research is focused on the synthesis, photoluminescence and photochemistry of semiconductor nanocrystals.

Nikolai Gaponik
	Nikolai Gaponik is an ad personam Professor at the TU Dresden (Germany). He received his PhD in chemistry (2000) from the Belarussian State University in Minsk (Belarus) under supervision of Prof. D. V. Sviridov. He was a visiting scientist at the LMU in Munich in 2000 (with Prof. J. Feldmann and Prof. M. Gao), a DAAD-fellow and later a research scientist in the group of Prof. H. Weller at the University of Hamburg in 2000–2005, and an IKERBASQUE-Fellow at the Center of Materials Physics, San Sebastian, Spain, in 2011. He habilitated at TU Dresden in 2013.
